Origins of Caste — The Varna System
From the Rigveda to Manusmriti — how hierarchy became hereditary
Foundation01
Foundation
▼

Caste is a hereditary system of social stratification that has shaped the Indian subcontinent for over 3,000 years. Unlike class — which can theoretically change with the accumulation of wealth or education — caste is determined entirely by birth. You are born into it. You cannot earn your way out, buy your way out, or marry your way out without risking your life and the lives of those you love. It does not matter how much money you make, how many degrees you earn, or how much land you own. Your caste follows you into every room, every marriage negotiation, every temple, every bureaucratic encounter, every conversation about your future.

The Varna system, first articulated in the Rigveda (~1500 BCE), described four categories emerging from the body of the cosmic man Purusha — Brahmins from his mouth, Kshatriyas from his arms, Vaishyas from his thighs, Shudras from his feet. The cosmic symbolism is not incidental: it places priests at the organ of speech and knowledge, warriors at the organ of action and force, merchants at the organ of movement and productivity, and servants literally at the foot of everything else. Those who would later be called untouchables were given no place in the Purusha's body at all. They were written out of the cosmic order before Indian history had even properly begun.

Over millennia, the four Varnas multiplied and hardened into thousands of endogamous Jatis — the actual birth groups people belong to in daily life. There are estimated to be between 3,000 and 4,700 Jati groups in India today. Jati determined what you ate, what you wore, which profession was "yours," which wells you could use, which temples you could enter, and — most crucially — who you could marry. Jati is the biological infrastructure of caste: endogamy (marriage only within the group) ensures that caste identity is inherited reliably, that "purity" is maintained, and that the hierarchy is reproduced across every generation without requiring anyone to enforce it explicitly. You just marry within your Jati, and caste perpetuates itself.

The Manusmriti (Laws of Manu, ~200 BCE) codified these hierarchies into explicit, brutal law. If a Shudra listened to the Vedas, molten lead was to be poured into his ears. If he recited them, his tongue was to be cut out. If he memorised them, his body was to be cut in two. Untouchables — placed entirely outside even the four Varna system — were made to wear bells so upper castes could hear them approaching and move aside. In some regions they were required to drag branches behind them to erase their own footprints, so that upper-caste feet would not be contaminated by the ground an untouchable had walked on. They could not draw water from the same wells. Their shadow falling on a Brahmin was pollution. Their voice addressing an upper-caste man directly — impudence punishable by death. This is not metaphor. These are documented practices from historical sources.

These people — outside the four Varnas, assigned the most degrading hereditary labour (handling corpses, cleaning sewage, working leather, scavenging) — are the ones who today call themselves Dalits. The word comes from Sanskrit and Marathi, meaning "broken," "ground down," "oppressed." It was reclaimed with fierce pride by the Dalit Panther movement of the 1970s and is now used globally as a political identity. The official constitutional term is "Scheduled Caste" — a bureaucratic designation that determines eligibility for reservations and legal protections. But "Dalit" is the word that carries meaning: not just a group, but a condition, a history, a fight.

"Caste is not a division of labour. It is a division of labourers — a hierarchy in which the divisions of labour are graded one above another."
— B.R. Ambedkar, Annihilation of Caste (1936)
①

Brahmin

Priests, scholars. Intellectual & religious authority.

②

Kshatriya

Warriors, rulers. Military & political power.

③

Vaishya

Merchants, farmers. Commerce & trade.

④

Shudra

Servants. Assigned to serve the top three.

⑤ Panchama / Avarnas — DALITS

Placed OUTSIDE the system entirely

Assigned sewage, corpses, leather. Touch = pollution. Shadow = pollution. Voice = punished. Constitutionally recognised as Scheduled Castes in 1950. ~201 million people.

Key Figures — Architects of Dalit Liberation
The people who fought back. From the 19th century to today.
Foundation05
8 Profiles
▼
1891–1956 · Maharashtra / New York / London
✊

Dr. B.R. Ambedkar

Father of the Constitution · Architect of Dalit Liberation

Born into the Mahar caste — designated untouchable — in 1891. His father was a schoolmaster in the British Indian Army. From childhood he faced daily humiliation: made to sit on the floor outside the classroom, denied water by upper-caste classmates, refused service at barber shops. He went on to earn a PhD from Columbia University under philosopher John Dewey, an LLM and DSc from the London School of Economics, and bar qualifications from Gray's Inn London — becoming the most academically credentialed Indian of his generation. And still, in Bombay, upper-caste peons refused to let him touch the water pitcher at the High Court.

He led the Mahad Satyagraha (1927) — fighting for Dalits' right to use a public water tank. He publicly burned the Manusmriti. He negotiated the Poona Pact with Gandhi under duress. He drafted India's Constitution — embedding Article 17 (abolishing untouchability), reservations, and fundamental rights. On October 14, 1956 — six weeks before his death — he converted to Buddhism with 600,000 followers in Nagpur. "I was born a Hindu. I will not die one." His final book, The Buddha and His Dhamma, was completed days before he died. Today he is the most widely depicted person in India — his statues outnumber Gandhi's in Dalit-majority areas.

Columbia PhDLSEConstitutional DrafterBuddhist
1827–1890 · Maharashtra
📚

Jyotirao Phule

Satyashodhak Samaj · Pioneer of Anti-Caste Rationalism

Jyotirao Phule opened the first school for untouchable children in Pune in 1848 — an act so radical that his father, under upper-caste community pressure, threw him out of the family home. He then opened a school for girls. When his wife Savitribai walked to school, upper-caste residents lined the route and threw dung and stones at her. He and Savitribai kept walking, every day.

He founded the Satyashodhak Samaj — Truth-Seekers' Society — in 1873. His book Gulamgiri (Slavery, 1873) compared the condition of lower-caste Indians directly to American chattel slavery and was dedicated to the abolitionists of the United States — a breathtaking intellectual move for a 19th-century Indian reformer. He was the first person to frame caste as a political system of exploitation rather than a natural or divine order. Ambedkar acknowledged him as one of his three greatest teachers — alongside the Buddha and Kabir.

ReformerAuthorEducation Pioneer
1831–1897 · Maharashtra
🏫

Savitribai Phule

India's First Female Teacher · Feminist Before the Word Existed

Savitribai Phule became India's first female teacher at 17, trained by her husband Jyotirao. Every morning she walked to school while upper-caste residents lined the route to throw dung, mud, and stones at her. She brought a second sari to school to change into upon arrival. She ran schools for girls of every caste including untouchables. She and Jyotirao operated a refuge for widows who had been raped — in 19th-century India, an act of radical feminist defiance. They adopted the son of a Brahmin widow who had been raped and would otherwise have been forced to kill or abandon the child.

When plague struck Pune in 1897, Savitribai walked among the sick, nursing them. She contracted plague. She died of it. Her life was not a symbol — it was a daily act of physical courage against every institution that told her she did not exist and her students did not deserve to learn.

PioneerFeministEducator
1879–1973 · Tamil Nadu
🔥

Periyar E.V. Ramasamy

Self-Respect Movement · Dravidian Rationalism

Periyar publicly burned the Manusmriti in 1927 — the ancient law-code that codified untouchability — calling Brahminism "a conspiracy to keep the majority in slavery." He called Brahmins "hereditary criminals." He founded the Self-Respect Movement in 1925, which conducted weddings without Brahmin priests — directly attacking the economic and ritual mechanism through which Brahmins maintained their dominance. He founded the Dravidar Kazhagam political party in 1944.

His core intellectual argument: Aryan Brahminism had colonised the Dravidian peoples of South India just as thoroughly as the British had colonised India as a whole. The Dravidian movement he ignited became the ideological foundation of Tamil Nadu's post-independence politics. The DMK and AIADMK — which have governed Tamil Nadu for most of 70 years — are both Periyarist parties. Without Periyar, there is no explanation for why Tamil Nadu has more advanced OBC/Dalit political representation than any other large Indian state.

RationalistDravidianTamil Nadu
1934–2006 · Punjab / Delhi
🗳

Kanshi Ram

BSP Founder · Bahujan Political Architect

Kanshi Ram was a government scientist at the Defence Research and Development Organisation when a caste-related incident at his workplace transformed him. He quit a secure government job, moved into bare rooms, ate simple meals, and dedicated every resource for the next three decades to building a Dalit political organisation from the ground up — something that had never been done in North India.

He built the Bahujan Samaj Party by uniting Scheduled Castes, Scheduled Tribes, and Other Backward Classes — "85% of the population" — against "the 15% that controls everything." He mentored Mayawati, who became India's first Dalit woman Chief Minister of Uttar Pradesh — the country's most populous state with 200 million people. His analysis was simple and devastating: if you don't hold power, you don't get justice. Power must come first. He died in 2006 after years of illness, having proved his thesis with actual electoral victories.

BSP FounderPolitical StrategistBahujan
1989–2016 · Hyderabad
✍

Rohith Vemula

Scholar · Symbol of Institutional Murder

Rohith Vemula was a PhD student in the University of Hyderabad's School of Social Sciences — a brilliant writer on science, Ambedkar, and astronomy who described Carl Sagan with the same passion as he described caste politics. In 2015, he was one of five Dalit PhD students suspended from their fellowships after a complaint from a BJP-affiliated student group alleged they had assaulted another student. The complaint was fabricated. Multiple inquiry committees found no wrongdoing. The university administration, under pressure from the central government's Ministry of Human Resource Development, repeatedly reinstated the suspensions.

On January 17, 2016, Rohith died by suicide. His final letter remains one of the most extraordinary documents in modern Indian history: "My birth is my fatal accident. I never was a person to them. I was reduced to my immediate identity — a vote, a number, a thing." The government's official response: question whether he was truly Dalit at all, so his case would fall outside the SC/ST Atrocities Act. The cruelty of this response was the point. #DalitLivesMatter was born.

ScholarSymbolHyderabad
1942–2014 · Maharashtra
⚡

Namdeo Dhasal

Poet · Dalit Panthers Co-Founder

Namdeo Dhasal grew up in Kamathipura — Mumbai's red-light district — in poverty. He co-founded the Dalit Panthers in 1972 with J.V. Pawar and Raja Dhale, directly inspired by the Black Panther Party. Their founding manifesto declared solidarity with all oppressed people globally: Dalits, Adivasis, women, neo-Buddhists, workers, the poor of every religion — and anti-colonial movements worldwide. It was the most internationalist Dalit political document ever written.

His debut poetry collection Golpitha (1972) — named after the red-light district — shattered Marathi literary conventions by bringing slum language, sexual realism, and street rage into high literature. Critics called it pornographic. Later critics called it "the most terrifying poetry written in any Indian language." His work transformed Dalit cultural identity from shame into furious, creative, defiant pride. The Panthers lasted a decade as an organisation; their cultural legacy lives in every Dalit writer who came after.

PoetDalit PanthersMarathi Lit
1980–present · Uttar Pradesh
🛡

Chandra Shekhar "Ravan"

Bhim Army Founder · UP's Dalit Street Fighter

Chandra Shekhar Azad adopted the suffix "Ravan" — a deliberate inversion of the Brahminical vilification of the Lanka king — and founded the Bhim Army in Saharanpur after the 2017 caste violence in which Thakur mobs burned Dalit homes and police arrested Dalit activists rather than the attackers. The Bhim Army runs free education centres for Dalit youth across UP and Rajasthan, provides legal aid, and practises confrontational street politics that established Dalit organisations had avoided.

He was arrested under the National Security Act and jailed for 16 months without trial or charges. After his release, the movement only grew. He has become the most visible Dalit youth leader in North India — combining Ambedkarite ideology with a generation's fury after Rohith Vemula, Hathras, and Indra Meghwal. "We have been asking for dignity for 70 years," he has said. "Now we are demanding it."

Bhim ArmyUPContemporary

Historical Timeline — 1500 BCE to 2024
From Rigveda to Hathras — the full arc of caste history
History04
15 Events
▼
~1500 BCE
Rigveda — The Purusha Sukta
The Purusha Sukta hymn in the Rigveda describes four Varnas emerging from the cosmic body of Purusha — the primordial man. Brahmins from his mouth, Kshatriyas from his arms, Vaishyas from his thighs, Shudras from his feet. The cosmic symbolism assigns every group its rank by where in the body they originate — and those who would later be called untouchables were given no place in the body at all. Written out of creation before Indian history had properly begun. This is not metaphor: it is the founding ideological document of a system that would govern the lives of hundreds of millions for three thousand years.
Origin Text
~200 BCE
Manusmriti — The Codification of Caste
The Laws of Manu codified the Varna hierarchy into explicit, brutal law. If a Shudra listened to the Vedas: molten lead into his ears. If he recited them: his tongue cut out. If he memorised them: his body cut in two. Untouchables were placed outside even the four-Varna system — assigned corpses, sewage, leather — and required to announce their presence with bells so upper castes could avoid being contaminated by their proximity. Some regions required untouchables to drag branches behind them to erase their own footprints. Religion became the architectural blueprint of the prison. Ambedkar publicly burned the Manusmriti at the Mahad Satyagraha in 1927. It remains politically contested in India today.
Legal Code
1818
Battle of Bhima Koregaon — Dalits Defeat the Peshwas
January 1, 1818. A small British East India Company force — including approximately 500 Mahar (Dalit) soldiers — defeated the Peshwa Brahmin army of 28,000. The Peshwa rulers had imposed some of the most severe untouchability rules in pre-colonial India: Dalits were required to hang pots around their necks to collect their spittle, and brooms at their waists to erase their footprints. For Dalits, the battle was not a fight for the British Empire — it was a moment of defeating the regime that had dehumanised them most systematically. January 1st is commemorated at the Bhima Koregaon pillar annually. In 2018, Hindutva mobs attacked Dalit pilgrims at this commemoration — and the government arrested 16 Dalit intellectuals, not the attackers.
Victory
1848–1890
Phule Movement — Education as the First Battlefield
Jyotirao Phule opened the first school for untouchable children in Pune in 1848. His father threw him out of the family home under upper-caste community pressure. His wife Savitribai walked to school every day through gauntlets of thrown dung and stones, bringing a second sari to change into on arrival. Together they ran schools for girls and untouchables, operated a refuge for rape survivors, and adopted the child of a Brahmin widow who had been raped. In 1873 Phule founded the Satyashodhak Samaj and published Gulamgiri — comparing caste to American slavery, dedicated to the American abolitionists. Ambedkar acknowledged Phule as one of his three greatest gurus alongside the Buddha and Kabir.
Education
1927
Mahad Satyagraha — The Right to Drink Water
Ambedkar led thousands of Dalits to drink from the Chavadar Tank in Mahad, Maharashtra — a public tank from which Dalits had been banned for generations. Upper-caste mobs attacked the Dalit marchers on the way back. At a second conference later that year, Ambedkar publicly burned the Manusmriti. "We are not fighting for water," he said. "We are fighting for the right to be recognised as human beings." The Mahad Satyagraha was the first major Dalit direct-action campaign in history — predating Gandhi's Salt March by three years.
Satyagraha
1932
Poona Pact — Surrender Under Duress
The British government's Communal Award granted Dalits separate electorates — meaning Dalits would elect their own representatives in their own constituencies. Gandhi declared a fast unto death against it, calling separate electorates a threat to Hindu unity. Faced with the prospect of Gandhi's death triggering a nationwide anti-Dalit pogrom, Ambedkar signed the Poona Pact — surrendering separate electorates in exchange for increased reserved seats within joint Hindu electorates. He called it "a betrayal under duress." Whether Gandhi's fast was sincere concern or political calculation designed to crush Dalit autonomous political power is a question that has never been resolved — and continues to divide Indian political thought today.
Political Deal
1950
Indian Constitution — Ambedkar's Gift and Its Limits
Drafted by Ambedkar. Article 17 abolishes untouchability and makes its practice criminal. Article 15 prohibits caste discrimination. Article 16 guarantees equality in public employment. 15% reservation in government jobs and higher education for SCs, 7.5% for STs. These are among the most progressive foundational provisions in any democracy. The 74 years since have been a sustained struggle to make them real against the institutional resistance of the castes that benefit from maintaining inequality. Ambedkar warned in the Constituent Assembly: "Constitutional morality is not a natural sentiment. It has to be cultivated — and our people have yet to learn it."
Constitution
1956
Mass Buddhist Conversion — Navayana
October 14, 1956, Nagpur. Ambedkar converted to Buddhism along with approximately 600,000 followers in a ceremony he had designed himself after two decades of studying every major religion. He chose Buddhism for its emphasis on reason over revelation, equality over hierarchy, compassion over ritual purity. He called his Buddhism "Navayana" — the new vehicle — distinguishing it from traditional schools by its explicit social liberation focus. He died six weeks later. Today there are an estimated 6 million Dalit Buddhists in Maharashtra alone. The movement has spread to Dalit communities across India, Japan, the US, and beyond.
Religious Movement
1968
Kilvenmani — 44 Burned Alive, All Acquitted
Christmas night, Kilvenmani, Tamil Nadu. Dalit agricultural workers had demanded a wage increase from ₹1 to ₹1.25 per day. Thevar landlords sent men who locked 44 people — mostly women and children — in a hut and burned it. Twenty-two children died. One mother threw her infant out of the burning hut; the mob hacked it and threw it back into the flames. The Madras High Court acquitted all accused in 1974: "insufficient evidence." Not one rupee of compensation was paid to any family. The acquittal is the message the system intended to send.
Massacre
1972
Dalit Panthers Founded — Fury as Art and Politics
Namdeo Dhasal, J.V. Pawar, and Raja Dhale founded the Dalit Panthers in Mumbai's Kamathipura slums, directly inspired by the American Black Panther Party. Their founding manifesto declared solidarity with all globally oppressed people — Dalits, Adivasis, women, neo-Buddhists, workers, and anti-colonial movements worldwide. Their politics were confrontational, their poetry was furious, and their legacy was the transformation of Dalit identity from shame into defiant, creative pride. The Panthers lasted a decade as an organisation. Their cultural and political influence continues in every Dalit rights movement today.
Movement
1989
SC/ST Prevention of Atrocities Act
After decades of Dalit advocacy, Parliament passed the SC/ST (Prevention of Atrocities) Act, listing 22 specific crimes against Dalits with enhanced penalties, mandatory special courts, and provisions reversing the burden of proof. In 2018, the Supreme Court issued a ruling that required a preliminary inquiry before arrest in atrocity cases — effectively weakening the Act's protection. Dalits called a nationwide strike in protest — the largest Dalit mass action in decades. The government passed an amendment to partially restore the original provisions. The episode demonstrated both the Act's political importance and the continuing upper-caste institutional effort to dilute it.
Legislation
1997
Laxmanpur Bathe — 58 Killed, All Acquitted
December 1, 1997. Arwal District, Bihar. One hundred Ranvir Sena men crossed the river into Laxmanpur Bathe village and killed 58 people: 16 children, 27 women, 15 men. Five teenage girls were gang-raped, their breasts severed, then shot. The two ferrymen who transported the Sena were killed to destroy witnesses. The lower court convicted 26 accused and sentenced three to death. The Patna High Court acquitted all of them in 2013. This is the deadliest Dalit massacre in independent India's history, and it has produced zero final convictions. The number 58 stands — and the verdict says: it does not matter.
Massacre
2006
Khairlanji — Caste Motivation Erased by Courts
Bhandara District, Maharashtra. The Bhotmange Dalit family — who had refused to provide false testimony in a land case — was attacked by a 40-person mob. Surekha and her daughter Priyanka were stripped, gang-raped, mutilated, and murdered. Sons Roshan and Sudhir were castrated and killed. Zero media coverage for three weeks. When prosecuted, the accused were convicted under the IPC for murder — but courts held caste motivation was not proven beyond reasonable doubt. They were therefore NOT convicted under the SC/ST Atrocities Act. The caste motivation was the entire reason the attack happened. Indian courts chose to legally erase it from the record.
Massacre
2016
Rohith Vemula + Una — The Year That Changed Everything
Two events in 2016 transformed Dalit politics globally. January: Rohith Vemula's death by suicide at Hyderabad University after systematic institutional persecution. His final letter — "My birth is my fatal accident" — became one of the most widely shared documents in Indian political history. July: seven Dalit men publicly flogged in Una, Gujarat for legally skinning a dead cow. Dalits responded by dumping cow carcasses at government offices — "if we cannot handle them, you deal with them." Both events sparked mass protests. #DalitLivesMatter entered global discourse. Jignesh Mevani and Chandra Shekhar Azad emerged as the faces of a new generation that had stopped waiting.
Turning Point
2020
Hathras — Rape, Death, Midnight Cremation
September 14, 2020: a 19-year-old Dalit woman gang-raped by four upper-caste men in Hathras, UP. She died in Delhi hospital September 29. That night, UP Police transported her body to the village and cremated it at 2 am — without family consent, with the family locked inside their home, with forensic evidence destroyed in the fire. Journalists were arrested at district checkpoints. The CM's office implied for days that the rape had not occurred. Four men were convicted in 2021 — but not specifically for rape, because the body that would have proven it had been cremated at 2 am.
State Violence
2022
Indra Meghwal — 9 Years Old, Water, Death
Jalore, Rajasthan. A nine-year-old Dalit student named Indra Meghwal drank water from a clay pot his teacher Suresh Singh Gurjar had designated "upper castes only." The teacher beat him so severely that the child suffered catastrophic brain injuries. He died in hospital three weeks later, on August 13, 2022. The teacher was bailed within days. The school denied the incident until video emerged. This is not history. The year was 2022. The country had just celebrated 75 years of constitutional democracy. The pot was real. The child was real. The law against this has existed since 1950. None of it mattered on July 20, 2022 in Jalore.
Ongoing
→
🩸
The Massacres — Blood That Was Never Counted
8 documented massacres. Most ended with all accused acquitted. The acquittal is part of the system.
Violence06
8 Massacres
▼

In the decade between 1994 and 2000, the Ranvir Sena — a private army formed by Bhumihar landlords in Bihar specifically to crush Dalit land-reform movements — carried out at least 22 documented massacres, killing over 300 Dalit men, women, and children. In every case: witnesses gave names. FIRs were filed. Trials happened. The Patna High Court acquitted the accused in essentially every case that reached verdict. Not one senior Ranvir Sena leader has ever been convicted. This is not a failure of the justice system. This is the justice system functioning exactly as designed for the communities it was designed to serve.

"In almost all these cases, perpetrators were identified, photographed, and named by survivors. The acquittals were not findings of innocence. They were exercises of caste power wearing legal robes."
— Sabrang India, citing NCRB and High Court records
25 Dec 1968 · Nagapattinam, Tamil Nadu
44killed

Kilvenmani

Kilvenmani Village, Nagapattinam District

Christmas night. Dalit agricultural workers had gone on strike demanding a wage increase from ₹1 to ₹1.25 per day — twenty-five paise more. Thevar landlords sent armed men to the village. They rounded up 44 people — mostly women, children, and elderly who could not flee — locked them in a hut, and set it ablaze. One mother threw her infant out of the burning structure through a window. The mob caught the child, hacked it with knives, and threw it back into the flames. Women pressed their bodies against the burning door to keep it shut from outside — their charred remains were found in that position. Twenty-two of the 44 dead were children. The Madras High Court acquitted all accused in 1974: "insufficient evidence." Not one rupee of compensation was ever paid.

⚖ All accused ACQUITTED — Madras HC 1974. Impunity complete.
6 Aug 1991 · Guntur District, Andhra Pradesh
8+killed

Tsundur

Tsundur Village, Guntur District

A Dalit boy accidentally brushed the foot of a Reddy-caste woman while walking past and immediately apologised. The Reddy community declared a total economic boycott of all Dalits in the village — no one would sell to them, employ them, or speak to them. After a month of this strangulation, approximately 400 armed Reddy men attacked. Eight Dalit men were killed, their bodies thrown into a canal. The real cause was not the foot incident: Dalit children had been getting educated, entering professions the upper castes considered their hereditary preserve, and refusing to perform degrading labour. Progress was the provocation. The Andhra Pradesh High Court acquitted all accused in 2014 — 23 years after the massacre. The Supreme Court restored some convictions in 2022, but the decades of impunity had already sent their message across generations.

⚖ All acquitted AP HC 2014. SC partially restored convictions 2022.
11 Jul 1996 · Bhojpur District, Bihar
21killed

Bathani Tola

Bhojpur District, Bihar

The Ranvir Sena — the Bhumihar landlords' private army — attacked Bathani Tola because Dalit and OBC communities there had been organising for minimum wages and land redistribution, directly threatening the economic domination of the landlord class. They entered the village at night and killed 21 people — mostly women and children left behind while the men worked distant fields. The killers were identified. Arrests were made. A lower court convicted multiple accused. The Patna High Court acquitted all 23 in 2012, citing "contradictions in witness testimonies." Rights groups say those contradictions were manufactured through 16 years of witness intimidation. The acquittals stand.

⚖ All 23 ACQUITTED — Patna HC 2012
1 Dec 1997 · Arwal District, Bihar
58killed

Laxmanpur Bathe

Arwal District, Bihar

The deadliest Dalit massacre in independent India's history. One hundred Ranvir Sena men crossed the river into Laxmanpur Bathe village. They killed 58 people: 16 children — the youngest was one year old; 27 women; 15 men. Five teenage girls were gang-raped, their breasts were severed with knives, and then they were shot. The two ferrymen who had transported the Ranvir Sena across the river were subsequently murdered to eliminate witnesses. The lower court convicted 26 people and sentenced three to death. The Patna High Court acquitted all of them in 2013, citing contradictions in witness testimony — testimony that rights groups document was extracted from traumatised survivors over a 16-year period during which witnesses were systematically threatened. The acquittals stand. They will always stand.

⚖ All convicted ACQUITTED — Patna HC 2013. 58 dead. Zero accountability.
1 May 1997 · Madurai District, Tamil Nadu
6killed

Melavalavu

Madurai District, Tamil Nadu

Murugesan was the first Dalit man ever elected panchayat president in the Melavalavu area — elected by legitimate democratic vote. The dominant Thevar community refused to accept the result. They boycotted the panchayat. On May 1, 1997 — Labour Day — a Thevar mob intercepted Murugesan and his colleagues on the road. They killed six people. They severed Murugesan's head and paraded it through the village streets. His crime: winning a democratic election. The district collector had been warned in advance that Thevar groups were threatening violence if Dalits attempted to exercise their democratic mandate. No protective security was deployed. The message: your vote counts on paper. On the ground, we decide who leads.

⚖ Partial convictions — reduced and challenged on appeal over decades
29 Sep 2006 · Bhandara District, Maharashtra
4killed + rape

Khairlanji

Bhandara District, Maharashtra

The Bhotmange family — Surekha, her husband Bhaiyalal, their sons Roshan and Sudhir, and their daughter Priyanka — were a Dalit family who had refused to provide false testimony in a land dispute favouring upper-caste neighbours. On September 29, 2006, a mob of approximately 40 people arrived. Surekha and Priyanka were stripped naked, gang-raped in front of the assembled village, mutilated, and murdered. Roshan and Sudhir were castrated and killed. Bhaiyalal survived by hiding. The bodies were thrown into a canal. There was not a single line of media coverage for three full weeks — until Dalit organisations forced the story into print. When the case was prosecuted, the accused were convicted for murder under the IPC — but the court held that caste motivation had not been proved beyond reasonable doubt, and therefore they were NOT convicted under the SC/ST Atrocities Act. The caste motivation was the entire reason the attack happened. The courts chose to legally erase it.

⚖ Convicted under IPC — NOT SC/ST Act. Caste motivation legally erased.
16 Jun 2000 · Jehanabad District, Bihar
32killed

Miyapur

Jehanabad District, Bihar

Between 400 and 500 Ranvir Sena members entered Miyapur village and fired indiscriminately on sleeping residents. Thirty-two Dalits were killed. The Sena described it as "retaliation" for Naxalite activity — a framing that treats every Dalit in a village as a legitimate collective target for any alleged Maoist connection anywhere in the district. This collective punishment logic has been used repeatedly across Bihar's massacre belt. The Patna High Court acquitted nine of ten accused in 2013. The cycle: massacre → acquittal → impunity → next massacre. It has not stopped.

⚖ 9 of 10 ACQUITTED — Patna HC 2013
19 Apr 1985 · Prakasam District, Andhra Pradesh
6killed + rape

Karamchedu

Prakasam District, Andhra Pradesh

Dalit residents used a village pond that had traditionally been "reserved" for the Kamma landowning caste. Six Dalit men were killed, women were raped, and dozens of Dalit homes were burned. The massacre galvanised Andhra Pradesh's Dalit movement and directly triggered the formation of the Andhra Pradesh Dalit Mahasabha — one of the most important Dalit civil society organisations in South India. Legal convictions were progressively reduced on appeal and challenged over the following decades — the multi-decade appellate erosion of massacre verdicts has become a specialised mechanism of impunity in Indian courts: even when lower courts convict, higher courts whittle it away over years until accountability is effectively zero.

⚖ Partial convictions — reduced on appeal, eroded over decades
→
⚠
Atrocity Types — A Taxonomy of Terror
Honour killings, devadasi, rape as weapon, police violence, witch-hunting, economic strangulation
Violence07
6 Categories
▼

In India, falling in love across caste lines can be a death sentence. Honour killings — murders committed by families and communities to punish inter-caste love — are not spontaneous rage. They are organised, deliberate, socially-sanctioned mechanisms to maintain caste endogamy: the biological infrastructure of the entire caste system. You cannot have hereditary caste without endogamy. Inter-caste marriage does not merely violate a social norm — it threatens the system's fundamental architecture, because it produces children whose caste identity is ambiguous. The violence that follows is therefore not personal. It is the system protecting itself.

Khap Panchayats — unelected village councils in Haryana, Rajasthan, and western UP — have passed death sentences for inter-caste marriages for generations. They have declared entire surnames as "siblings" to prevent Dalit-upper-caste unions through incest taboo. The Supreme Court ruled in Shakti Vahini vs. Union of India (2018) that Khap Panchayats have no legal authority over marriage choices and individual freedom of marriage is a fundamental constitutional right. Within the same week, multiple Khap Panchayats held press conferences announcing they would continue operating exactly as before. Not one Khap Panchayat leader has been prosecuted under that judgment as of 2024.

NCRB officially records approximately 25–33 honour killing cases nationally per year. Tamil Nadu NGO Uyirmai documented 195 cases in that state alone in five years. Maharashtra NGOs document 25+ per year. The gap between official and actual is because police routinely register honour killings as "suicide," "accident," or "illness" — particularly when the victim's own family is complicit, which is the majority of cases. A 2021 study found honour killings are highest not in areas of extreme Dalit deprivation, but precisely in areas where Dalit communities are gaining education and economic mobility. Progress is the provocation. Every Dalit who rises triggers a violent backlash from those whose social identity depends on Dalits remaining in their assigned place.

"We do not oppose love. We oppose love that breaks the social order. The social order must be maintained."
— Khap Panchayat leader, Haryana, 2019 (translated from Hindi)
The Standard Pattern

What Honour Killing Actually Looks Like

A Dalit man and an upper-caste woman fall in love. Her family discovers it. He is tracked down by a group — typically including the woman's father, uncles, and brothers — and killed. Sometimes both are killed together. Police is told the Dalit man "harassed" the woman. His death is registered as a brawl or accident. The woman is married off within weeks. This sequence repeats with remarkable consistency across UP, Haryana, and Rajasthan in the majority of documented cases.

Data Gap

33 Official Cases — 195 in One State Alone

NCRB 2021: 33 honour killings nationally. Tamil Nadu NGO Uyirmai: 195 in TN over 5 years alone. Maharashtra: 25+ annually. The gap exists because officers from dominant castes share perpetrators' social networks and have direct incentive to register these deaths as anything other than honour killings — which would require prosecuting powerful community members.

Supreme Court 2018

Ruled Illegal — Continues Openly

Shakti Vahini vs. Union of India held Khap Panchayats have no legal standing to interfere in marriage choices. Individual constitutional right to choose a partner upheld. Specific directions issued to state governments. No Khap leader prosecuted under these directions. Panchayats continue holding public meetings, issuing public diktats, publicly interfering in marriages. The law said no. The power structure said yes. Power won.

The devadasi system is the institutionalisation of sexual violence against Dalit girls through religious sanction. A girl — almost universally Dalit, invariably from the poorest households, typically between ages six and fourteen — is "dedicated" to a goddess in a ceremony that resembles a wedding. The girl wears bridal attire. A "marriage" is conducted with the deity. From that point she belongs to the temple and to the upper-caste men who pay priests and temple trustees to access her. She can never marry a human man. She can never leave. Her children will have no father — they too belong to the goddess. When she is no longer young enough to attract patrons, she survives by begging.

The system has different names in different states — Jogini in Telangana and Andhra Pradesh; Basavi in Karnataka; Matangi in Maharashtra; Thevardiyar in Tamil Nadu; Murali in Goa. Different names. The same girls. The same outcome. The National Commission for Women estimated 450,000+ devadasis across India as of 2018, with 93% from SC/ST communities. The system was banned by Karnataka in 1982, Maharashtra in 1988, Andhra Pradesh in 1988, and nationally under the Immoral Traffic Prevention Act. It continues openly in 2024.

The Yellamma temple at Saundatti on the Maharashtra-Karnataka border is the largest concentration of the system — an estimated 250,000 devadasis in the surrounding districts. Temple trustees are upper-caste men. The annual Yellamma fair draws hundreds of thousands, and rights organisations document new dedications of girls at each fair, despite the prohibition. HIV prevalence in some devadasi communities near Saundatti reaches 30–40% — among the highest concentrated rates anywhere in Asia, documented in peer-reviewed medical journals since the 1990s. Government rehabilitation programmes have budgets that are largely unspent and deliver almost nothing to actual devadasi women.

"I was dedicated at age nine. I did not know what it meant. By the time I was eleven, I knew. There is no word in any language for what it means to be a child and to know."
— Devadasi woman, Yellamma region, testimony to NHRC
NCW Data

450,000+ Devadasis — 93% SC/ST

National Commission for Women survey. The 93% SC/ST figure is consistent across every state survey. The system exists to provide sexual access to Dalit girls for upper-caste male patrons at rates impoverished families cannot resist. Religious sanction is the ideological mechanism that makes the transaction feel divine rather than criminal — for the perpetrators. For the girls, there is no ambiguity.

Karnataka Govt Survey 2018

22,491 Documented in Just 6 Districts

The Karnataka government's own survey found 22,491 documented devadasis in six districts. The survey was commissioned after activist campaigning, then quietly shelved. The Yellamma temple at Saundatti — the epicentre of the devadasi belt — is administered by a government-recognised trust. The state is not merely failing to stop the system. In some instances it is administering the temples that sustain it.

Public Health Crisis

HIV Prevalence: 30–40% in Some Communities

Multiple peer-reviewed studies from Karnataka and Maharashtra document HIV prevalence of 25–40% in devadasi women — compared to India's national HIV rate below 0.3%. The devadasi system is a vector for HIV that extends into the wider community through the men who pay for access. This has been documented in medical literature since the 1990s. The government response has focused on condom distribution rather than ending the system.

The rape of Dalit women in India is not incidental to caste oppression — it is structural. It is not a byproduct of poor men doing terrible things. It is a systematic mechanism of social control: upper-caste men rape Dalit women to punish Dalit men who assert rights, to terrorise entire communities into submission, to remind every Dalit family that their women's bodies belong to the upper castes whenever upper castes choose to make that point. Human Rights Watch documented this comprehensively in their 1999 report "Broken People" — the first international report to name rape as a systematic instrument of caste terror rather than crime by individual bad actors.

The NCRB records approximately 3,600 rapes of Dalit women annually — approximately ten per day, every day. Every rights organisation working on the ground estimates the actual figure is ten to twenty times higher. The barriers to reporting are extraordinary: the police officer you report to is likely from a dominant caste, may know the perpetrators, may be related to them, and has full discretion over whether to register your FIR. Women who insist on reporting face counter-cases — charges of public nuisance, theft, prostitution — filed by the accused's family. The conviction rate for rape under the SC/ST Atrocities Act averages below 15%.

The Hathras case of 2020 became the global symbol of this system's most brazen operation. A 19-year-old Dalit woman gang-raped on September 14, 2020. She died September 29. UP Police cremated her body at 2 am on September 30 — without family consent, with the family physically barred from leaving their house, destroying forensic evidence in the fire. The Chief Minister's office spent days implying the rape had not occurred. A government media team filmed the victim's father appearing to contradict the rape — rights groups documented this was conducted under coercion. This is not exceptional. This is what the state does when caste violence and sexual violence intersect and the perpetrators are politically powerful.

"Sexual violence against Dalit women is not merely a crime — it is a political act. The most direct statement of who owns whom in a caste society."
— Human Rights Watch, "Broken People" (1999)
HRW 1999 — "Broken People"

The Global Document That Named the Pattern

Human Rights Watch's landmark report documented hundreds of cases of rape and sexual torture across AP, Tamil Nadu, Bihar, UP. Key finding: rape is used as punishment when Dalit men assert rights — demand wages, refuse degrading labour, win elections, own land. It changed how UN and international bodies categorised Indian caste violence.

NCRB 2022

10 Rapes/Day — Massively Undercounted

3,676 registered rape cases against Dalit women. Activists estimate 36,000–70,000 actual cases annually based on community surveys and health data. The FIR registration rate for Dalit rape victims is estimated under 10% in UP and Rajasthan — because police stations are staffed by officers from dominant castes who share the accused's social world.

Prison Statistics India

75%+ Undertrial Prisoners Are Marginalised

Dalits, Adivasis, OBCs, Muslims make up over 75% of India's undertrial prison population. Upper castes: tiny fraction. This is not crime patterns — it is who gets arrested, who gets bail, who has lawyers.

Tamil Nadu 1995 — "Jai Bhim" Case

Rajakannu: Tortured to Death in Custody

Dalit tribal man arrested for alleged theft. Tortured, died. His wife Sengeni fought for years. Advocate Chandru (later Madras HC judge) took the case. Officers convicted — a rare exception. Became the film Jai Bhim (2021).

Bhima Koregaon 2018–2021

Stan Swamy: Died in Custody

84-year-old Jesuit priest with Parkinson's arrested under UAPA as "Urban Naxal." Denied a sipper to drink water for weeks. Died in custody July 2021 without bail. First political prisoner to die in modern India's custody. 16 activists arrested total.

Pattern

Upper-Caste Police, Dalit Victims

India's police is dominated by upper castes. When Dalits file FIRs, officers often share perpetrators' caste background. FIR denial, case burial, counter-cases against victims — systematic, documented, standard.

Witch-hunting — accusing a woman of witchcraft and killing her — is overwhelmingly directed at Dalit and Adivasi women in Jharkhand, Bihar, Odisha, Chhattisgarh. It is almost always cover for land-grabbing or silencing women who resist sexual violence or assert inheritance rights. Stripped, beaten, forced to eat excreta, paraded, killed. The community participates — which destroys accountability.

NCRB 2000–2016

2,500+ Women Killed

Official count — massive undercount. Jharkhand alone estimated 500+ cases/year by activists. No central anti-witch-hunting law. States with laws see near-zero enforcement. Victims overwhelmingly Dalit and Adivasi women.

Social Boycott

Economic Strangulation of Villages

When Dalits assert rights — file complaints, refuse degrading labour, win elections — the dominant caste response: total economic boycott. No one sells, employs, or speaks to any Dalit. Crops rot. Doctors refuse treatment. Children barred from school. Can last months or years.

ILO / Anti-Slavery

40 Million in Bonded Labour

India has the world's largest bonded labour population. Dalits and Adivasis are the overwhelming majority. Bonded Labour Abolition Act 1976 is law. Continues openly in brick kilns, quarries, farms, carpet factories — especially Rajasthan, UP, Bihar.

Documented 2020s

Untouchability — Still Happening

Dalits' own cups at tea stalls. Children sitting separately at school meals. Teachers refusing to check Dalit students' work. Dalits beaten for wearing shoes, riding horses at weddings, trimming moustaches. These are 2023 cases from Rajasthan and UP — not history.

NCRB 2022 / Manual Scavenging Act

Sewer Deaths: Ongoing State Killing

282+ sewer deaths officially 2017–2020 (vast undercount). Almost all Dalit Valmikis. Banned since 1993, re-banned 2013. Government pays ₹10 lakh "compensation" — activists call it institutionalised murder. No structural action.

✂
Partition 1947 — The Forgotten Wound
India, Pakistan, Bangladesh — Dalits fell through every crack in the new nations
History—
3 Countries
▼

When the British carved the subcontinent in August 1947, 14–17 million people were uprooted and up to 2 million killed. The Dalit story inside Partition is distinct and almost entirely untold: Dalits were being counted as "Hindus" for census purposes despite resisting Brahminical Hinduism for generations. They had no homeland. They were abandoned by both new nations.

"If Muslims must have Pakistan because they are a nation, then the Untouchables must have their own separate state."
— B.R. Ambedkar — ignored by everyone at the time
🇮🇳

India — The Incomplete Promise

Dalits who migrated from East Pakistan were placed in camps. Upper-caste Hindu refugees received priority land grants and government jobs. Dalit communities remain in "refugee colonies" in West Bengal today — 77 years later.

  • Dalit refugees from East Bengal received near-zero government land allotments
  • Rehabilitation Ministry records show systematic discrimination against SC refugees
  • Promised land redistribution never reached landless Dalit cultivators
🇵🇰

Pakistan — Erased Entirely

Hindu Dalits in Sindh and Punjab couldn't migrate — no resources, no community willing to help. Stayed and faced double marginalisation: discriminated as Hindus by the Muslim majority AND as Dalits by upper-caste Hindus.

  • 2–4 million SC Hindus among the poorest communities in Sindh and Punjab
  • Bonded labour in brick kilns overwhelmingly Dalit Hindu
  • Forced conversions of Dalit Hindu girls documented in thousands
  • Enemy Property Act seized Dalit land; virtually no government representation
🇧🇩

Bangladesh — Slow-Motion Erasure

In 1947, Hindus were 28% of East Pakistan. By 2011: 8.5% of Bangladesh. This demographic collapse — across 1947, 1964, 1971, and beyond — is one of history's most ignored slow-motion ethnic cleansings.

  • 1971 Liberation War: Pakistani army specifically targeted Hindus as "pro-India collaborators"
  • Vested Property Act: ~2.6 million acres seized from ~1.2 million Hindu households
  • Dalit Hindus face triple discrimination: Muslim majority + upper-caste Hindus + state
  • 2021–2024: Mob attacks on Hindu temples continue driving out remaining community
🔴
Today's Crimes — This Is Happening Now
Caste violence is not history. 50,000 registered crimes/year — and that is a vast undercount.
Violence08
9 Cases
▼

The NCRB registered 50,291 crimes against Scheduled Castes in 2022 — one registered crime every ten minutes, every single day. Rights organisations estimate this is 10–20% of what actually happens. The remaining 80–90% never reaches a police station, or gets turned away at the door by officers from dominant castes who share the perpetrators' social world. The cases below are not historical illustrations. They are drawn from the last eight years to show, with total clarity, that nothing in this archive is past tense.

2016
Rohith Vemula — Hyderabad University. A 26-year-old PhD scholar in the University of Hyderabad's School of Social Sciences — a brilliant writer on science, Ambedkar, and astronomy. In 2015, he and four other Dalit students were suspended from their fellowships after a BJP-affiliated student group filed a complaint the university's own inquiry committees found to be fabricated. The suspensions were reinstated under Ministry of Human Resource Development pressure. On January 17, 2016, Rohith died by suicide. His final letter: "My birth is my fatal accident. I never was a person to them — only a vote, a number, a thing." The government's official response: deny his Dalit identity, so the SC/ST Atrocities Act would not apply to his case. Denying the identity of a dead man to erase accountability — this is what the state chose to do. #DalitLivesMatter began here.
2016
Una Flogging — Gujarat. July 11, 2016. Seven Dalit men in Una, Gir Somnath district, were stripping a cow carcass — legally, as is their traditional and constitutionally protected occupation — when a group of cow vigilantes arrived. The men were stripped naked, tied behind a vehicle, and publicly flogged through the streets while onlookers filmed. Police arrived and arrested the Dalit men, not the vigilantes. In response, Dalit communities across Gujarat began dumping dead cow carcasses at government offices and Collector's bungalows across the state. Their message: "If we cannot handle this, you deal with it." It was the most creative, defiant act of Dalit resistance in a generation. Jignesh Mevani was elected to the Gujarat assembly from this movement. The vigilantes were bailed and faced minimal prosecution.
2018
Bhima Koregaon — The State Attacks Its Critics. January 1, 2018: Hindutva mobs attacked Dalit pilgrims at Bhima Koregaon, killing one, injuring hundreds. The Maharashtra government then arrested 16 academics, lawyers, poets and activists — labelling them "Urban Naxals" who had allegedly plotted to assassinate the Prime Minister. Among them: Stan Swamy, 84 years old, with advanced Parkinson's disease, who had spent decades fighting for Adivasi rights in Jharkhand. In custody, he was denied a sipper cup for weeks — forcing him to attempt drinking from a regular glass despite his tremors. Bail was repeatedly denied. He died in custody on July 5, 2021 — without ever being tried for anything. The people who organised the mob that attacked the Dalit pilgrims were never seriously prosecuted.
2020
Hathras — Rape, Death, Midnight Cremation. September 14, 2020: a 19-year-old Dalit woman was gang-raped in a field in Hathras, UP, by four upper-caste Thakur men. She died in hospital in Delhi on September 29. That night — without her family's knowledge or consent, with her family physically barred inside their house by police — UP Police transported her body to the village and cremated it at 2 am. The entire district was sealed. Journalists were arrested at checkpoints. The Chief Minister's office spent days implying rape had not occurred. A government media team filmed the victim's father apparently contradicting the rape claim; rights groups documented this was done under coercion. All four men were eventually convicted in 2021 — but not for rape specifically, because the court found rape could not be proven. The body that could have proven it had been cremated at 2 am.
2022
Indra Meghwal — Nine Years Old, Beaten to Death. Indra Meghwal was a nine-year-old Dalit boy at a government school in Jalore, Rajasthan. On July 20, 2022, his teacher Suresh Singh Gurjar beat him severely for drinking water from a clay pot the teacher had designated "upper castes only." Indra suffered catastrophic brain injuries and died in hospital on August 13 — three weeks later. The teacher was arrested, then bailed within days. The school's administration initially denied anything had happened until video evidence made denial impossible. The year was 2022. The country had just celebrated "Amrit Kaal" — the golden age of 75 years of independence. The pot of water was real. The dead child was real.
NCRB 2022
50,291 Registered Crimes — The Official Undercount. UP: 12,287. Rajasthan: 8,651. MP: 7,732. Bihar: 6,799. These four states — 70% of all registered Dalit crimes in the country. Every registered crime required a survivor to go to a police station, report to an officer from a dominant caste, file an FIR, and then hope that it would not be dismissed, watered down, or countered with a false case against themselves. Most did not make that journey. Actual crimes: estimated 250,000–500,000 per year. That is a crime against a Dalit person every 60–120 seconds. Every single day. The Republic of India. The world's largest democracy.
Ongoing
Sewer Deaths — The State's Weekly Price. Manual scavenging was banned in 1955, re-banned in 1993, and banned again with teeth in 2013. It continues. The government officially recorded 282 deaths in sewer-cleaning work between 2017 and 2020 — approximately one every five days. Activist organisations document approximately 1,500 deaths in the same period, because most are registered as "asphyxiation" or "drowning" in hospital records. The 2013 Act requires ₹10 lakh compensation per death and prosecution of those who hire manual scavengers. Government pays the money. Nobody is prosecuted. In 2023, three Valmiki Dalit workers died in a single Jaipur sewer in one week. The Chief Minister expressed condolences. A review committee was announced. Another week came. Another sewer. Another family. Another ₹10 lakh.

International & Diaspora — Caste Has No Borders
UK, USA, Japan, West Africa, Nepal, Sri Lanka, Pakistan, Bangladesh — 260 million affected globally
Society16
NEW8 Regions
▼
"Caste is not a relic of South Asia. It is a living system that has travelled with its practitioners to every country where South Asians have settled — and it shares its deep structure with other systems of hereditary hierarchy the world over."
— Isabel Wilkerson, Caste: The Origins of Our Discontents (2020)

Year Cisco settled landmark US caste discrimination lawsuit
US Federal Court
USA — Silicon Valley Didn't Leave Caste Behind

The United States has two intersecting caste stories. The first is America's own — what Isabel Wilkerson's Pulitzer Prize-winning book Caste (2020) argues is a racial caste system with the same structure as India's: hereditary, fixed at birth, enforced by terror. The second is the South Asian diaspora carrying India's caste system directly into American workplaces, temples, and communities.

Equality Labs' 2020 survey of 1,500 South Asian Americans found: 67% of Dalits experienced caste discrimination at work, 59% experienced caste discrimination in educational settings, 52% experienced slurs, and 26% experienced physical assault. These are not just immigrant nostalgia patterns — they are active discrimination in 2020s America.

Indian Americans are the largest South Asian group in the US and hold disproportionate power in Silicon Valley — running Google (Sundar Pichai), Microsoft (Satya Nadella), Twitter/X (Parag Agrawal before Musk), and hundreds of startups. A 2023 Carnegie Mellon study found upper-caste Indians are dramatically overrepresented in US tech leadership. Dalit engineers describe being frozen out of teams, excluded from social networks, given worse reviews, and passed over for promotions by upper-caste managers who recognise their surnames.

"I changed my surname on LinkedIn. The callback rate tripled."
— Anonymous Dalit software engineer, Equality Labs survey 2020
The Cisco Case — 2020 to 2023

America's First Caste Discrimination Lawsuit

In 2020, California's Department of Fair Employment and Housing sued Cisco Systems — the networking giant — for allowing two upper-caste Indian managers to discriminate against a Dalit Indian engineer. The engineer was passed over for promotions, had his work stolen, and was excluded from team activities. In 2023, Cisco settled the lawsuit. The case established that caste discrimination is actionable under US civil rights law as a form of ancestry and ethnic discrimination — a landmark legal precedent.

California 2023

Caste Discrimination Ban — A Battle

California's legislature passed a bill adding caste to the list of protected characteristics under state anti-discrimination law — making it the first US state to do so explicitly. Governor Gavin Newsom vetoed it, citing existing protections. The veto was condemned by Dalit rights organisations as capitulation to upper-caste Hindu lobby groups who had campaigned aggressively against the bill, arguing it was "anti-Hindu."

Wilkerson's Framework

Race in America IS a Caste System

Isabel Wilkerson's Caste (2020) draws direct parallels between anti-Black racism in the US, the Indian caste system, and Nazi Germany's race laws. All three share eight "pillars": divine will, heritability, endogamy, purity, occupational hierarchy, dehumanisation, terror, and inherent inferiority. Her book became a global bestseller, reigniting the race-caste debate and energising Dalit diaspora activism in the US.

UK — 1.5 Million South Asians, Caste Imported in Full

The UK has the largest South Asian diaspora outside South Asia — approximately 1.5 million people of Indian, Pakistani, and Sri Lankan origin. Caste discrimination has been documented in British workplaces, temples, gurdwaras, and social institutions since the 1970s. In 2010, the Equality Act included a provision that could be used to make caste a protected characteristic — but successive UK governments refused to implement it, citing opposition from upper-caste Hindu groups who argued it would "stigmatise" the community.

A 2010 survey by the Anti-Caste Discrimination Alliance found that 58% of Dalit respondents experienced caste discrimination in the UK. Forms included: being refused service or employment, being given inferior work, being socially ostracised at temples, gurdwaras, and community events, and being subjected to slurs and verbal abuse based on caste identity.

The National Institute of Economic and Social Research's 2010 report for the UK government concluded that caste discrimination was "a real and ongoing problem" in British society — affecting tens of thousands of people. The government commissioned the report and then largely ignored it under pressure from upper-caste Hindu lobby organisations.

UK Gurdwaras — Sikhism's Caste Problem

Separate Entrances, Separate Langars

Despite Sikhism's foundational anti-caste theology, caste segregation persists in many British Sikh gurdwaras. Some gurdwaras in Birmingham, Wolverhampton, and Southall are effectively "Jat gurdwaras" or "Ravidassia gurdwaras" — the Dalit Sikh community built separate places of worship because they were made unwelcome in Jat-dominated ones. The formation of the separate Ravidassia religion in 2010 (after the Vienna assassination) was partly driven by UK Dalit Sikhs.

Legal Vacuum

Equality Act 2010 — The Clause Never Enacted

Section 9(5) of the Equality Act 2010 gave the government power to make caste a protected characteristic. It was never enacted. In 2018, the government announced caste protection would be achieved through existing race law — a decision denounced by Dalit groups as inadequate. Caste discrimination remains a legal grey zone in Britain.

Workplace Discrimination

NHS, Finance, Tech — Documented Cases

Caste discrimination documented in NHS trusts (where South Asian staff is significant), British financial services, and technology companies. Common patterns: Dalit professionals excluded from social networks, given worse performance reviews, excluded from mentorship by upper-caste senior colleagues, and subjected to derogatory comments about their surnames or regions of origin.

Japan — The Burakumin: Asia's Other Untouchables

Japan's feudal Edo period (1603–1868) maintained a rigid caste-like system with the Burakumin at the bottom — descendants of those who performed "spiritually impure" work: slaughtering animals, executing criminals, handling corpses, tanning leather. The parallel with India's untouchables is unmistakable — the same logic of ritual pollution, the same hereditary assignment of degrading occupations, the same violent enforcement.

The Meiji government legally abolished feudal caste distinctions in 1871. It changed almost nothing socially. The Burakumin Liberation League (Buraku Kaihou Domei) was formed in 1922 — Japan's equivalent of the Dalit movement — and has fought discrimination ever since. Japan officially has approximately 3 million Burakumin, concentrated in the Kansai region (Osaka, Hyogo, Nara, Kyoto).

The most pernicious ongoing form of discrimination is the "Buraku list" — privately compiled databases of Buraku village names, neighbourhoods, and family names that employers and families use to screen job candidates and marriage partners. In 2016, a Tokyo company was caught selling a database of 5,000 Buraku settlements to 200+ clients including major Japanese corporations. Japan's government introduced a Basic Act for Buraku Liberation (2016) but it has weak enforcement mechanisms.

"My family had lived in Japan for generations. We looked Japanese, spoke Japanese, were Buddhist. But we were not Japanese enough — because of what our great-grandparents had done for a living."
— Buraku Liberation League testimony, 2019
The Buraku List Scandal — 2016

Selling Discrimination for Profit

A Tokyo company sold databases identifying Buraku settlements to over 200 corporate clients — used for employment screening and marriage background checks. The clients included major Japanese real estate companies, staffing agencies, and manufacturers. The company was fined — the clients faced no consequences. The practice continues underground.

Marriage Discrimination

"Kōseki" — The Family Registry as Weapon

Japan's koseki (family registry) system traces lineage. Families hiring private detectives to research a potential spouse's koseki — to check for Buraku ancestry — is a documented practice. Marriage refusal based on Buraku identity remains common. It is illegal but nearly impossible to prosecute.

Comparison

India vs. Japan — Same System, Different Name

Both systems: hereditary, based on "ritual pollution" logic, associated with specific occupations (leather, corpses), legally abolished (India 1950, Japan 1871), socially persisting in marriage and employment discrimination. Key difference: Japan has never produced an Ambedkar-scale political movement or constitutional affirmative action. The silence around Buraku identity in Japan is itself a form of oppression.

West Africa — Caste Without Hinduism

West Africa's caste systems are among the most overlooked in global discourse — partly because they exist in Muslim-majority societies, which complicates the narrative that caste is specifically a Hindu institution. In fact, hereditary occupational castes exist across the Mande, Wolof, Hausa, Tuareg, Fula, Soninke, and Mandinka peoples — predating both Islam and European colonisation.

The basic structure is similar to the Indian system: certain occupational groups — blacksmiths, leatherworkers, weavers, and griots (oral historians and praise-singers) — are designated as "caste" and considered socially inferior and ritually distinct. They form endogamous groups that cannot intermarry with the non-caste "freeborn" majority. Violating endogamy rules is still punished with social ostracism, violence, and in some cases death.

Griots — whose role is to carry oral history, perform music, and negotiate social events — occupy an ambiguous position: essential but stigmatised. In many communities, griots' bodies cannot be buried in the ground (considered polluting) but are instead placed in hollow trees. The practice persisted into the 20th century in Senegal and Mali.

Senegal & Gambia — Wolof Castes

Géwël (Griots) — Essential but Untouchable

The Wolof people of Senegal and Gambia maintain strict caste divisions between géér (freeborn), géwël (griots/praise-singers), and ñeeño (artisan castes — blacksmiths, leatherworkers, weavers). Inter-caste marriage is prohibited. In 2018, a Senegalese footballer's engagement was broken off by his family when they discovered his fiancée was géwël. Caste discrimination is not illegal in Senegal.

Mali & Guinea — Mande Castes

Nyamakala — The Untouchable Artisans

Among the Mande-speaking peoples (Mandinka, Bambara, Soninke), the nyamakala are hereditary castes of blacksmiths, leatherworkers, and griots. They are essential to society's functioning but socially marginalised. Marriage between horon (freeborn) and nyamakala is forbidden. The social boundary is maintained through the concept of nyama — spiritual power or pollution attached to certain materials and people.

Horn of Africa

Somalia's Madhiban & Ethiopia's Manja

Somalia's Madhiban (traditional hunters and leatherworkers) and Tumaal (blacksmiths) face severe social discrimination from the Somali clan majority. Ethiopia's Manja people — associated with pottery and hunting — are considered "impure" by neighbouring communities. Both groups face marriage bans, restricted land access, and violence when asserting rights — a caste system with no Hindu theology but identical social logic.

Nepal — Where Caste Was Law Until 1963

Nepal's Muluki Ain of 1854 — a legal code enacted by Prime Minister Jung Bahadur Rana — was arguably the most detailed legal codification of caste hierarchy in history. It assigned every group in Nepal a specific caste rank, prescribed specific punishments for inter-caste interactions, and explicitly defined which communities were "water-unacceptable untouchables." It remained the law of the land until 1963.

Nepal's Dalit population is approximately 3.5 million — about 13% of the country. They are concentrated in the Terai (lowland plains, closer to the Indian border) and in hill communities. The main Dalit communities include the Kami (blacksmiths), Damai (tailors and musicians), Sarki (cobblers and leatherworkers), and Chamar. As in India, these are hereditary occupational designations that became prisons.

Nepal's Caste-Based Discrimination and Untouchability (Offence and Punishment) Act 2011 criminalises caste discrimination — but enforcement is almost non-existent. Nepal's Supreme Court has repeatedly had to intervene on cases of untouchability. In rural Nepal, inter-caste marriage couples are regularly murdered by their own families — and police typically register these as "suicide."

Ajit Mijar Case — 2020

Beaten to Death for Inter-Caste Marriage

Nabaraj BK (Dalit) and five friends were beaten to death in Rukum, Nepal, after he visited his girlfriend's (upper-caste) family to ask for her hand in marriage. Twelve people killed in total. The killers were from the upper-caste Malla family of the village. The girlfriend was forced into hiding. The case shocked Nepal and led to renewed calls for caste law enforcement.

Water Access

Untouchability Persists in Village Nepal

Studies by INSEC (Informal Sector Service Centre) Nepal document that in 65% of surveyed rural Nepalese villages, Dalits are still not allowed to touch water sources used by upper castes. Dalits are excluded from temples in 75% of villages surveyed. These practices are illegal — and continue openly because police refuse to enforce the law.

Political Marginalisation

Parliament — Dalits Are Missing

Nepal's 2015 Constitution mandates inclusion of Dalits in government. In practice, Dalits remain severely underrepresented — especially in the judiciary, security forces, and senior bureaucracy. The dominant groups (Brahmin/Chhetri hill elites) retain near-monopoly control. Nepal's Dalit organisations argue the federal structure has made their marginalisation worse, not better.

Sri Lanka — Caste Among Sinhalese AND Tamils

Sri Lanka's caste system is less discussed than India's but is deeply entrenched in both Sinhalese Buddhist and Tamil Hindu communities — demonstrating again that caste is not exclusively a Hindu phenomenon. In the Sinhalese community, the Goyigama caste (traditionally farmers, ~50% of Sinhalese) dominates politics, military, and religious institutions. The Rodiya are Sri Lanka's closest equivalent to Dalits — historically considered so "impure" that even their shadow was considered polluting. They were forced to beg and perform degrading labour.

Among Sri Lankan Tamils, the Vellalar caste (landowners, traditionally the elite) have dominated Tamil politics and cultural institutions. The Paraiyar and Pallar — Dalit Tamil communities — face discrimination from both the Sinhalese majority (ethnic discrimination) and upper-caste Tamils (caste discrimination). During the civil war (1983–2009), Dalit Tamils were often excluded from LTTE structures dominated by Vellalar elites — facing enemy fire from government forces AND caste discrimination from their own community's armed movement.

The Indian Tamil (Up-Country Tamil) plantation workers — brought to Sri Lanka by the British to work tea plantations — are among the most marginalised communities in Asia. Stateless until 1988, they remain socially isolated on tea estates, earning poverty wages, with extremely limited access to education and healthcare. Many are Dalit in origin. The estate Tamil community is simultaneously marginalised by caste, by ethnicity, by class, and by geography.

Estate Tamils — The Plantation Underclass

150 Years of Exploitation

Brought from South India (mostly Madurai and Tirunelveli) by British planters from 1820s onwards. Mostly Dalit Paraiyar origin. Stateless from 1948 to 1988 — neither Indian nor Sri Lankan citizens. Today ~800,000 plantation workers remain on tea estates. Earn some of the lowest wages in Asia. Excluded from main Sinhalese and Tamil political processes.

Civil War (1983–2009)

Dalit Tamils — Double Marginalisation

During Sri Lanka's civil war, Tamil Dalits faced violence from both sides. The LTTE (Tamil Tigers) was dominated by Vellalar-caste Tamils who practiced caste discrimination within the movement. Dalit Tamil fighters were given the most dangerous roles. Post-war "rehabilitation" has almost entirely excluded Dalit Tamil communities from reconstruction aid.

Buddhism and Caste

The Theravada-Caste Contradiction

Buddha himself was born into the Kshatriya caste and explicitly rejected the caste system. Yet Sinhalese Buddhist institutions in Sri Lanka maintain caste structures — the dominant nikāyas (monastic orders) like Siam Nikaya historically restricted ordination to the Goyigama caste. Lower-caste Sinhalese Buddhists formed their own nikāyas as a result. Ambedkar's choice of Buddhism was in part because he saw it as India's original anti-caste religion — but Sri Lanka shows even Buddhism was eventually colonised by caste.

Pakistan — Caste + Religion = Double Persecution

Pakistan's Scheduled Caste Hindu population is estimated at 2–4 million, concentrated in Sindh and southern Punjab. They are among the most multiply marginalised communities in Asia: persecuted as religious minorities (Hindus in a Muslim-majority state), as low-caste people within the Hindu community, and as the most economically destitute group in already-poor rural communities. Their total invisibility in Pakistani national discourse is itself a form of violence.

The Kolhi, Meghwar, Bheel, and Chamar communities are the main Scheduled Caste Hindu groups in Pakistan. They work overwhelmingly as agricultural labourers on Sindhi feudal landlords' land — in a system of semi-bondage that has barely changed since Partition. Pakistan's landholding structure in Sindh is feudal: vast zamindars (landlords, typically upper-caste Muslim) own everything; Dalit Hindu tenants own nothing and owe everything.

Forced conversions are the most documented violence against Dalit Hindus in Pakistan. Girls — predominantly from Scheduled Caste families, aged 12–18 — are abducted, forcibly converted to Islam, and married to Muslim men. The Human Rights Commission of Pakistan documents 1,000+ cases annually, mostly in Sindh. Pakistani courts have repeatedly ruled in favour of the "converts" even when evidence of coercion is overwhelming. There is no Pakistani law against forced religious conversion.

HRCP Data — Annual

1,000+ Forced Conversions Per Year

Human Rights Commission Pakistan documents over 1,000 cases of forced conversion annually — with Dalit Hindu girls as the primary victims. Pakistan's Sindh province passed an anti-conversion bill in 2016; it was withdrawn after religious pressure. Pakistan's federal government has never passed legislation protecting religious minorities from forced conversion.

Bonded Labour

The Brick Kiln System — Modern Slavery

Pakistan's brick kilns — concentrated in Punjab — employ an estimated 4.5 million bonded labourers, the majority of whom are Dalit Christians and Dalit Hindus. Workers take an "advance" (peshgi) that they can never repay because wages are set below the interest. Children are inherited into the debt. The system has been documented by the ILO, HRW, Anti-Slavery International, and Pakistan's own HRCP for 30 years with minimal government action.

Political Invisibility

Zero Representation in Pakistani Institutions

Dalit Hindus have no representation in Pakistan's National Assembly, Senate, judiciary, or military officer corps. Pakistan's reservation system for non-Muslim minorities (5% of seats) benefits primarily upper-caste Hindu families. Dalit Hindu leaders who advocate for their communities face harassment, arrests under Pakistan's blasphemy laws, and violence. Many have gone into exile.

Bangladesh — From 28% to 8.5% — A Demographic Disappearance

In 1947, Hindus were approximately 28% of East Pakistan's population. By 1971 (Bangladesh's independence): 18.5%. By 1991: 11.6%. By 2011: 8.5%. This catastrophic decline — from over a quarter to under a tenth of the population in 65 years — is the result of continuous waves of violence, land seizure, and forced migration. It is one of the largest forced demographic transformations in modern history and receives almost no international attention.

The Vested Property Act (originally the "Enemy Property Act" from 1965, retained and expanded by Bangladesh after 1971) allowed the state and private actors to seize "enemy" — meaning Hindu — property. An estimated 2.6 million acres were seized from approximately 1.2 million Hindu households between 1965 and 2006. The Act was finally repealed in 2011, but the seized land was never returned. A generation of Hindus — disproportionately Dalit Hindus who were most vulnerable — lost everything.

Within Bangladesh's Hindu community, Dalit Hindus face an additional layer of marginalisation: discriminated against by the Muslim majority AND by upper-caste Hindus within their own temples, social events, and community institutions. They are doubly dispossessed — of land, of security, of community, and of dignity.

"We are squeezed between two kinds of contempt — the contempt of those who consider us infidels, and the contempt of those who consider us untouchables."
— Dalit Hindu community leader, Barisal district, 2019
1971 Liberation War

Hindus Specifically Targeted for Genocide

Pakistani army commander General Niazi's documented strategy in 1971 included specifically targeting Hindus as "Indian agents." Multiple international witnesses and the Hamoodur Rahman Commission report (Pakistan's own inquiry) confirm that Hindu communities were disproportionately massacred. An estimated 500,000 to 3 million people were killed; historians estimate Hindus were 30–40% of the victims while being 18% of the population.

2021 Cumilla Violence

Durga Puja Attacks — October 2021

Social media posts alleging a Hindu man had placed a copy of the Quran at a Durga Puja site (later proven false or staged) triggered mob attacks on Hindu temples, homes, and shops across Bangladesh. Dozens of temples destroyed, hundreds of homes burned, multiple Hindus killed. International media coverage was minimal. Bangladesh's government condemned the attacks but prosecuted very few perpetrators.

Dalit Hindus Specifically

The Most Isolated, Most Vulnerable

Bangladesh's Dalit Hindu communities — Namasudra, Dom, Mehtar, Dhobi — are concentrated in isolated rural colonies (bastis) with no political representation, no access to mainstream Hindu temples, minimal government services, and no land. They work in sanitation and manual scavenging — the same hereditary occupations as in India — with the same zero dignity and zero safety. Bangladesh's constitution has no specific protection for Dalit Hindus as a distinct group.
