Regional Deep Dives — Power Axes, State by State
9 states/regions — Tamil Nadu, UP & Bihar, Maharashtra, Rajasthan, Gujarat, Andhra & Telangana, Punjab, Kerala, Northeast India
Society15
9 Regions
▼

Tamil Nadu is simultaneously the state with the most sophisticated Dalit political culture in India and the state with the highest documented rate of honour killings. Understanding why requires understanding the Dravidian revolution — what it achieved, who it left out, and what filled the vacuum when Brahmin dominance was broken but not replaced with equality.

The story begins with Periyar. His Self-Respect Movement in the 1920s–1940s explicitly targeted Brahmin supremacy as a political system — not just a social prejudice — and mobilised the non-Brahmin majority against it. The Dravidar Kazhagam (1944) and then the DMK (1949) were the political vehicles of this mobilisation. By the 1960s, Brahmin dominance of Tamil Nadu's civil service, judiciary, politics, and universities had been substantially broken — a transformation more radical and rapid than anywhere else in India. Brahmins, once 3% of the population wielding 70% of institutional power, now constitute under 2% and have largely emigrated to Chennai, Bengaluru, and the United States. This is genuinely one of the most significant social revolutions in modern Indian history.

But the Dravidian revolution was an OBC revolution — it elevated the Vellalar, Mudaliar, Thevar, and Vanniyar communities into power, not Dalits. Dalits (19% of TN's population — the highest proportion of any non-SC-reserved state in South India) gained symbolic inclusion in Dravidian politics and benefited from Tamil Nadu's comparatively strong welfare state, but were systematically excluded from real leadership within the DMK and AIADMK. The top leadership of both parties across 70 years has been almost entirely non-Dalit. The Viduthalai Chiruthaigal Katchi (VCK) — founded by Thol. Thirumavalavan in 1982 — is the main autonomous Dalit political party, and it has had to fight for every inch of its political space against Dravidian party machines that want Dalit votes without Dalit leaders.

The intermediate castes that filled the vacuum left by Brahmins are often more physically violent toward Dalits than the Brahmins they displaced. Thevars (the Mukkulathor confederation: Kallar, Maravar, Agamudaiyar, approximately 10% of the population) dominate southern Tamil Nadu — Madurai, Dindigul, Sivaganga, Tirunelveli. They carried out Kilvenmani (1968) and Melavalavu (1997). They are the primary perpetrators of honour killings in Tamil Nadu. The Vanniyar community (~12%, concentrated in northern TN: Villupuram, Cuddalore, Dharmapuri) organised anti-Dalit riots in the 1990s and carried out the Dharmapuri burning in 2012. Both communities have their own political parties — DMDK and PMK — that act as protection rackets for their caste interests, extracting concessions from DMK and AIADMK in exchange for electoral support. In this way, Dalit interests are bargained away twice: by the Dravidian parties and by the intermediate-caste parties the Dravidian parties must accommodate.

Tamil Nadu's honour killing rate is the highest in India by most NGO surveys. The Tamil NGO Uyirmai documented 195 cases in five years — in one state, with an active, functioning legal system, with Dalit political parties, with relative economic development. The analysis is consistent: honour killings are not concentrated in the most backward areas, but in areas where Dalit men are gaining education and entering professions, where Dalit children have started sitting in the same classrooms, where Dalit grooms ride horses, where Dalit families have saved money and built concrete houses. Progress is the provocation. The Thevar and Vanniyar communities respond to Dalit mobility not with acceptance but with violence, because their own social identity depends on Dalits remaining below them in the hierarchy. Remove that, and their caste pride has no floor to stand on.

"The Dravidian movement broke Brahmin power. It did not break caste. It handed caste power to different hands."
— Thol. Thirumavalavan, VCK founder, interview 2019
Why Most Advanced

The Periyar Legacy — What It Actually Achieved

Tamil Nadu's Dalits benefit from: the strongest welfare state in India (comprehensive noon meal scheme, housing, healthcare); the highest OBC/SC representation in state government compared to their population share; a Dalit political party (VCK) with actual assembly representation; and a culture of public anti-Brahmin rationalist discourse going back 100 years that makes explicit caste pride politically embarrassing for the mainstream. None of this exists at this level anywhere else in India.

Thevar Dominance — Southern TN

Kilvenmani to Melavalavu — Same Logic

1968: 44 Dalits burned alive by Thevar landlords for demanding ₹1.25/day wage. 1997: Dalit panchayat president Murugesan beheaded for winning an election. 2014, 2016, 2018, 2021: documented Thevar-on-Dalit killings for inter-caste relationships. The acquittals in Kilvenmani (1974) and other cases established a pattern of impunity that continues today.

Dharmapuri 2012

268 Homes Burned — Vanniyar Response to Love

November 2012. A Vanniyar family's daughter allegedly had a relationship with a Dalit man. Vanniyar mobs burned 268 Dalit homes across three villages. Over 3,000 Dalits were rendered homeless overnight. Police filed FIRs but the district administration negotiated a "settlement" rather than prosecution. The Dalit families received paltry compensation. Their homes remained rubble.

VCK — Dalit Autonomous Politics

The Party That Refuses to Disappear

Founded 1982. Repeatedly absorbed, marginalised, and pressured by the Dravidian parties. Has survived by combining electoral politics with street-level mobilisation — VCK cadres physically protect Dalit communities in village-level confrontations in ways that purely electoral parties do not. Thirumavalavan has said: "The Dravidian parties want us as vote banks. We insist on being stakeholders."

Together, Uttar Pradesh and Bihar account for approximately 40% of all registered crimes against Scheduled Castes in India annually, despite containing roughly 25% of the national population. This is not a coincidence. It is the direct result of one of the most entrenched caste power structures in the world — a three-way dominance of Brahmins (intellectual, religious, bureaucratic power), Bhumihars (landowning military power backed by private armies), and Rajputs/Thakurs (feudal agrarian dominance) — that has resisted every reform, every legal intervention, and every political challenge for 74 years of constitutional democracy.

Brahmins constitute approximately 10–12% of UP-Bihar's combined population. They have historically dominated the administrative civil service (IAS, IPS), the judiciary, the temples, the schools, and the media. Their power is ideological and institutional — they define what is "legitimate" history, "proper" Sanskrit, and "true" Hinduism in ways that systematically exclude Dalit contributions. They were the primary authors of the caste theology and the administrators of the colonial state. Post-independence, they transitioned seamlessly into the institutions of the new republic. Ambedkar specifically targeted Brahminism — the ideology — rather than Brahmins as individuals, arguing that the ideology was the source code of the caste system and could not be reformed without being rejected.

Bhumihars — approximately 6% of Bihar's population — claim Brahmin descent but function as landowning warrior nobility. They formed the Ranvir Sena in 1994, explicitly as a counter-movement against Dalit land-rights mobilisation and Naxalite activity in central Bihar. Between 1994 and 2004, the Ranvir Sena carried out at least 22 documented massacres, killing over 300 Dalit men, women, and children across Arwal, Bhojpur, Jehanabad, and Aurangabad districts. In every case, the Patna High Court acquitted the accused. The pattern is not coincidence — Bhumihar lawyers, Bhumihar judges, Bhumihar witnesses who could contradict Dalit testimony. The Ranvir Sena's "success" — in the sense that no one was ever meaningfully punished — is a demonstration of the total capture of Bihar's legal institutions by the landowning castes.

In Uttar Pradesh, the dominant pattern of Dalit oppression involves Thakurs (Rajputs) — approximately 8% of UP's population. The Hathras victim's four rapists were Thakur. The UP Police officers who cremated her body at 2 am were Thakur or protecting Thakur networks — this is not speculation; it is documented in statements by officers present and in the pattern of subsequent prosecutions. "Thakurwad" — the term used in UP political discourse to describe Thakur community's assertion of total impunity — is not a slogan. It describes a specific, documented pattern: Thakur perpetrators of violence against Dalits receive police protection, prosecution dilution, and political cover from the ruling party in UP.

The BSP's rise and decline is the central political story of Dalit North India. Kanshi Ram built the Bahujan Samaj Party on the thesis that the oppressed 85% majority could outvote the privileged 15% if united. Mayawati became Chief Minister of UP four times — the largest direct exercise of Dalit political power in world history by population. At its peak (2007–2012), the BSP governed UP with a Dalit woman at the top. The upper-caste response was a long-term, multi-pronged counter-mobilisation that used BJP's Hindutva narrative to split the Bahujan coalition, bring OBCs back into the upper-caste-dominated Hindu fold, and reduce the BSP to its current residual state. The Bhim Army emerged from the rubble of this setback — a street-level organisation that has abandoned the parliamentary path and gone back to direct confrontation.

"In UP, the choice is not between BJP and Congress. It is between Thakurwaad and everyone else. And Thakurwaad has the police."
— Chandra Shekhar Azad, Bhim Army, 2021
The Ranvir Sena — Bihar's Private Army

22 Massacres. 300+ Killed. Zero Senior Convictions.

Formed 1994 by Bhumihar landlords specifically to counter Dalit land-reform organising. Key massacres: Bathani Tola (1996, 21 killed), Laxmanpur Bathe (1997, 58 killed), Miyapur (2000, 32 killed), Nagri Bazaar (1997, 10 killed). The Patna High Court acquitted the accused in virtually every case. The impunity is systemic, not incidental — Bhumihar social networks permeate the Bihar police, prosecution, and judiciary.

Hathras 2020

Rape + Midnight Cremation = State Complicity

The Hathras case is not an exception — it is the rule made visible. Four Thakur men committed the crime. The UP Police — under a Thakur-dominated political dispensation — cremated the body at 2 am to protect the accused from forensic evidence. The CM's office spent days denying the rape occurred. International media arrived. Still, the conviction for "rape" specifically was not secured because the evidence had been destroyed. This is the system working as designed.

Mayawati & BSP — Rise and Decline

The Largest Dalit Electoral Mandate in History

Mayawati won UP in 2007 with a majority, governing 200 million people as the world's most powerful Dalit leader. Her governments built Ambedkar parks, renamed districts after Dalit icons, and increased SC welfare spending. The upper-caste counter-mobilisation through BJP's Hindutva politics gradually peeled away OBC support and reduced the BSP from 206 seats (2007) to 19 seats (2012) to near-zero today. What took decades to build was dismantled in one election cycle.

Bhim Army — The New Strategy

When Parliament Fails, Streets Remain

Chandra Shekhar Azad founded the Bhim Army after 2017 Saharanpur violence because electoral politics had demonstrably failed. His strategy: free education for Dalit youth, direct legal aid, and physical protection of Dalit communities facing caste violence — confrontational rather than parliamentary. Arrested under NSA for 16 months. Released. Movement grew. "We are done waiting for a party to save us."

Maharashtra is simultaneously the beating heart of Dalit liberation — the state of Ambedkar, the Phules, the Dalit Panthers, Namdeo Dhasal, the Mahad Satyagraha, the mass Buddhist conversion — and the state where Khairlanji happened, where the Bhima Koregaon pilgrims were attacked while Dalit intellectuals were jailed, and where 250,000 devadasis continue to be systematically exploited near the Karnataka border. No other state holds this contradiction in such sharp relief.

The dominant power structure involves two communities: Marathas — approximately 30% of Maharashtra's population — who own the majority of agricultural land, control the state's sugar cooperatives, dominate its educational and medical institutions, and have governed Maharashtra for most of the post-independence period through the Congress and NCP. And Chitpavan Brahmins — approximately 5% of the population — who were the Peshwa rulers defeated at Bhima Koregaon in 1818, who assassinated Gandhi (Nathuram Godse was Chitpavan), and who maintain significant intellectual, media, and ideological influence through Hindutva organisations including the RSS, which was founded by a Chitpavan Brahmin (K.B. Hedgewar) and whose early leadership was almost entirely Chitpavan.

Maharashtra has the world's largest Dalit Buddhist community — approximately 6 million Navayana Buddhists in the state, descended from the mass conversion Ambedkar led in Nagpur on October 14, 1956. This community — overwhelmingly Mahar-caste — is relatively more educated, more urbanised, and more politically conscious than Dalit communities in most other states. They are also the most organised in civil society: Maharashtra has the highest concentration of Dalit NGOs, publishing houses, literary magazines, and cultural institutions in India. Namdeo Dhasal's Dalit Panthers (1972), the Little Magazine movement in Marathi Dalit literature, the Samata Sainik Dal — these are all Maharashtra-originated movements that transformed how Dalits understood their own identity and resistance.

The Maratha reservation agitation of 2016–2018 is the key story of contemporary Maharashtra caste politics. Marathas — who own the majority of land, control the sugar industry, dominate the education sector, and have produced every Maharashtra Chief Minister except a small number — organised massive protests demanding reservation as an "economically backward" community, directly threatening the Dalit SC reservation share. They brought millions onto the streets. They occasionally turned violent. The Maharashtra government capitulated and created a special Maratha quota. The Supreme Court struck it down in 2021, holding that Marathas are not a backward community. The episode is a masterclass in how dominant castes in India weaponise the language of the oppressed: by claiming backwardness they did not have, they attempted to capture resources explicitly intended for those who do.

"Maharashtra gave India Ambedkar. Maharashtra gave Dalits the Buddha. And Maharashtra gave us Khairlanji, Bhima Koregaon, and the Maratha reservation agitation. All of these are Maharashtra."
— Dalit intellectual Anand Teltumbde, before his arrest under UAPA
Bhima Koregaon 1818 and 2018

200 Years, Same Enemies

1818: Mahar soldiers defeated the Peshwas — the Chitpavan Brahmin rulers who had imposed the most severe untouchability rules in pre-colonial India. Dalits commemorate January 1st annually at the victory pillar. January 1, 2018: Hindutva mobs attacked the pilgrims. The Maharashtra government arrested 16 Dalit academics, lawyers, and activists under UAPA as "Urban Naxals." The Peshwas' political descendants attacked; the state arrested those who were attacked. The 200-year symmetry is not coincidence.

Khairlanji 2006

Caste Motivation Legally Erased

The Bhotmange family was targeted specifically because they were Dalit — they had refused to assist upper-caste neighbours in a land dispute. The mob attacked them as Dalits. The courts convicted them under the IPC for murder but held that caste motivation was not proven, so the SC/ST Atrocities Act did not apply. This legal erasure of caste as motive is not an aberration. In multiple Maharashtra cases, courts have consistently declined to apply the Atrocities Act even when the caste motivation is explicit in witness testimony.

The Devadasi Belt

Maharashtra-Karnataka Border — 250,000 Devadasis

The Yellamma temple complex at Saundatti sits on the Maharashtra-Karnataka border and is the epicentre of the devadasi system in India. An estimated 250,000 devadasis in the surrounding districts. Almost all Dalit. Almost all from households too poor to refuse the "honour" of dedication. The Maharashtra government banned the system in 1988. It continues openly in 2024, at a temple administered by a government-recognised trust.

Dalit Buddhism — 6 Million Strong

What Conversion Actually Changed

Ambedkar's 1956 conversion created a distinct Dalit Buddhist identity in Maharashtra that has produced real social outcomes: lower rates of alcohol use, higher rates of women's education, stronger community organisations, and a fierce pride in Ambedkarite identity that resists assimilation into mainstream Hindu nationalism. The Navayana Buddhist communities in Nagpur, Pune, and Mumbai are among the most politically conscious Dalit communities on earth. They are also the primary target of BJP's Hindu-unity narrative, which argues that conversion is "anti-national."

Rajasthan is where feudalism never ended. It is the state with the highest documented rates of untouchability practice in both rural and urban areas, the highest concentration of manual scavengers in India, and a political economy in which Rajputs (~9%), Brahmins (~8%), and in some districts Jats — together approximately 25–30% of the population — maintain near-total control of land, police, courts, media, and administrative positions. The state's major cities — Jaipur, Jodhpur, Udaipur — are photographed endlessly for their "royal heritage": their pink and blue palaces, their camel fairs, their thakur havelis. This tourism economy actively subsidises the feudal structure by marketing upper-caste "royalty" as exotic heritage worth preserving, while the Valmiki Dalit workers cleaning the sewers beneath those palaces die weekly.

Rajasthan leads India in manual scavenging deaths. The government officially recorded 282 sewer deaths nationally between 2017 and 2020. Activist organisations and journalists' investigations document approximately 1,500 in the same period — the gap exists because hospital records register these deaths as "asphyxiation" or "drowning" rather than "sewer entry without equipment," removing them from the manual scavenging count. In 2023, three Valmiki Dalit workers died in a single Jaipur sewer in one week. The city was hosting an international tourism conference the same month. The Chief Minister expressed condolences. No contractor was prosecuted. A review committee was announced. Rinse and repeat, every year, every city.

The Valmiki community — Rajasthan's primary Dalit sanitation workers — are assigned hereditary sanitation work in a system that has barely changed since colonial times. In many Rajasthani towns, Valmiki families are given "official" sanitation sweeper designations that cannot be refused without losing access to housing. The municipal corporation assigns the work. The community cannot refuse because the work comes with a small stipend that is often their only income. The 2013 Manual Scavenging Act was supposed to end this. In Rajasthan's municipalities, it has largely not been implemented because the implementation would require municipalities to buy equipment and pay sanitation workers proper wages — which costs more than the current system of assigning Valmikis hereditary work for below-minimum compensation.

Beyond sanitation, Rajasthan documents extraordinary levels of untouchability practice in daily life. Rights organisations have documented in recent years: separate tea cups for Dalit customers at roadside stalls in Nagaur, Barmer, and Alwar; teachers in Sikar and Jalore refusing to check Dalit students' work; Dalit men beaten in multiple districts for wearing "upper-caste" shoe styles or trimming moustaches in upper-caste style; Dalit wedding processions attacked for bringing DJ systems; Dalit grooms assaulted for riding horses. The case of Indra Meghwal — nine years old, beaten to death in 2022 for drinking from the teacher's water pot — is the most documented, but rights organisations record dozens of similar incidents annually from Rajasthan alone.

"Rajasthan's palaces and forts are UNESCO heritage. The people who clean their sewers die in them weekly. Both of these are Rajasthan."
— Bezwada Wilson, Safai Karamchari Andolan, 2023
Manual Scavenging Capital

The Weekly Killing the State Prices but Will Not Stop

The 2013 Act mandates ₹10 lakh per sewer death. Rajasthan pays it. What it does not do: prosecute the contractors who sent workers in without equipment; prosecute the municipality officials who contract the work; implement the mechanised cleaning equipment the Act requires. The ₹10 lakh payment is not compensation. It is the state acknowledging that it will continue killing these workers and has calculated what each death is worth.

Indra Meghwal — Jalore 2022

Nine Years Old. Water. Death. Bail in Days.

July 20, 2022: teacher Suresh Singh Gurjar beats 9-year-old Dalit student Indra Meghwal for drinking from a clay pot designated "upper caste only." Indra dies August 13 from brain injuries. Teacher arrested, bailed within days. School administration denied the incident for a week until video emerged. The constitution has prohibited this since 1950. The teacher knew it was illegal. He did it anyway. Because in Jalore, Rajasthan, in 2022, a Valmiki child's drink of water from the wrong pot was still treated as a transgression worth punishment.

Rajput-Brahmin Power Axis

Every Institution, Every District

Rajasthan's police, judiciary, and administration are dominated by Rajput and Brahmin officers at leadership levels disproportionate to their population share. This means: Dalit FIRs are routinely not registered; Dalit witnesses in caste violence trials are routinely intimidated; and political pressure from Rajput and Brahmin MLAs and ministers routinely results in cases against dominant-caste perpetrators being diluted, stayed, or dropped. This is not anecdote. It is documented in NHRC reports, state human rights commission reports, and NGO documentation going back to the 1990s.

Wedding Processions — Ongoing Pattern

A Horse. A DJ. A Beating.

Documented pattern across Rajasthan: Dalit grooms attacked for riding horses to their own weddings (considered "upper caste privilege" by dominant caste communities); Dalit wedding processions attacked for playing music through dominant-caste streets; Dalit families beaten for bringing DJ systems that are "too festive." These cases occur multiple times annually in Barmer, Jalore, Pali, Nagaur, and Bhilwara districts. Police response is typically to mediate a "settlement" rather than prosecute assault.

Gujarat is presented to the world as India's development model — the state where Narendra Modi served as Chief Minister from 2001 to 2014, the state with high GDP growth, the state of the Vibrant Gujarat summits and the Statue of Unity. It is also the state where a Dalit man was publicly stripped and flogged in a village road in 2016 for doing his legal job, while police arrested him instead of his attackers. Understanding this contradiction requires understanding who benefits from the "Gujarat model" and who pays for it.

The dominant power group in Gujarat is the Patidar community — also called Patels — approximately 12% of Gujarat's population. Patidars dominate Gujarat's business world, diamond industry, pharmaceutical sector, and diaspora networks. Approximately 40% of all motel owners in the United States are of Patidar origin — the famous "Patel motel" phenomenon driven by post-1960s migration. In Gujarat, they are the BJP's most reliable vote bank and their political influence is such that when they launched the massive reservation agitation of 2015–2016, demanding SC-level reservation despite being economically dominant, the state government eventually capitulated with a compromise — later struck down by courts. Their ability to destabilise the state as a community that was thoroughly privileged, never actually backward, and yet capable of extracting concessions through street power — while Dalits who face actual oppression are arrested for protesting — demonstrates the complete inversion of the system's stated purposes.

The cow vigilante movement is Gujarat's specific mechanism of Dalit economic destruction. Dalits' traditional livelihood of skinning and processing dead cattle — a legal occupation, a hereditary skill, a source of income — was progressively criminalised by the Hindutva cow protection campaigns. The pattern is well established: Dalit men legally skin a cow that has died naturally. A group of Hindutva-affiliated cow vigilantes arrives, accuses them of killing the cow, beats them, strips them, films the assault, and calls police. Police arrive and arrest the Dalit men for "illegal transport of cattle." The vigilantes are released. In the Una case of July 2016, this sequence was filmed and the video spread nationally — revealing what had been occurring in Gujarat's villages for years without anyone outside noticing.

The Dalit response to Una was extraordinary in its creativity and defiance. Within weeks, Dalit communities across Gujarat began dumping dead cow carcasses at government offices — district collector's bungalows, municipal buildings, gram panchayat offices. Their message: "If we cannot skin dead cattle, you deal with them. It is your cow, your problem, you clean it up." This forced the state to confront the practical reality that the entire beef and leather economy of Gujarat was dependent on Dalit labour that cow vigilantism was trying to criminalise. The protest also produced Jignesh Mevani — a young Dalit human rights lawyer who had been organising in this space — as a national political figure. He was elected to the Gujarat Legislative Assembly in 2017 as an independent, becoming the most prominent Dalit political voice from Gujarat in decades.

"You call our work pollution. You call our cow sacred. The day your sacred cow dies, call us. Until then, leave us alone to live."
— Dalit protest slogan, Una aftermath, 2016
Una 2016 — The Moment Everything Changed

Flogging That Sparked Gujarat's First Dalit Revolt

July 11, 2016: Seven Dalit men stripped naked, tied behind a vehicle, flogged through the streets of Una by cow vigilantes. Filmed and circulated nationally. Police arrested the Dalits. Jignesh Mevani organised the response: mass protests, dead cow dumps at government offices, a march from Ahmedabad to Una. For the first time in decades, Dalit communities in Gujarat organised as a political force rather than as BJP voters. The BJP's response was to make conciliatory promises, implement almost nothing, and attempt to absorb Dalit anger through token gestures.

Patidar Reservation Agitation

Dominant Caste Captures Anti-Poverty Language

2015–2016: The Patidar Anamat Andolan Samiti (PAAS), led by Hardik Patel (then 22 years old), organised massive protests demanding SC/ST-level reservation for Patidars, citing "economic backwardness." Patidars own the majority of Gujarat's industrial assets. They dominate the diamond trade, pharma, and hospitality. The Supreme Court's definition of backwardness has never remotely applied to them. Yet they brought millions onto streets, turned violent in some cities, and extracted political concessions. The signal to actual SC communities: your rights can be diluted whenever a politically powerful community decides to appropriate your protections.

Cow Vigilantism as Economic Weapon

Criminalising the Livelihood That Sustained Dalits

Gujarat's leather and beef processing industry — built entirely on Dalit hereditary labour — employs an estimated 700,000+ people in the state. The cow vigilante movement criminalised the first stage of this supply chain: the collection and skinning of dead cattle. By making legal work into a target for violent assault, it effectively seized Dalit economic assets without compensation. Upper-caste business owners who process the leather downstream were not targeted. Only the Dalits at the base of the supply chain.

Jignesh Mevani

Elected Independent — From Una to the Assembly

Human rights lawyer, organiser of the dead-cow protest movement, and first major Dalit political figure to emerge from Gujarat in decades. Elected to Gujarat Legislative Assembly in 2017 from Vadgam constituency as an independent — winning without party machinery in a BJP-dominated state. Subsequently arrested multiple times on what his supporters describe as fabricated charges. His political survival in BJP-dominated Gujarat is itself an act of sustained resistance.

Andhra Pradesh and Telangana — bifurcated into two states in 2014 — share a caste structure dominated by the Reddy community (approximately 5–7% of the population) and the Kamma community (approximately 4–5%), whose control over agricultural land and irrigation water in the fertile coastal and Rayalaseema districts has created one of the most clearly documented systems of Dalit agricultural bondage in India. The Karamchedu massacre (1985) and Tsundur massacre (1991) — both Kamma-on-Dalit attacks triggered by Dalits using shared water resources — are the most internationally documented instances of Dalit agricultural violence in Indian legal history.

Reddys dominate Telangana and Rayalaseema — they have provided the Chief Ministers of undivided Andhra Pradesh and Telangana for the majority of the post-independence period. The TRS (now BRS), which governed Telangana from 2014 to 2023, was a Reddy-dominated party. Reddys are the primary landowning class in Telangana's agricultural districts and the primary employers of Dalit agricultural labour. The wage disputes that triggered violence in Karimnagar and Warangal districts — where Naxalite organising among Dalit agricultural workers in the 1980s and 1990s provoked landlord retaliation — were fundamentally conflicts between Reddy landowning power and Dalit labourers' attempt to bargain for survival wages.

Kammas dominate coastal Andhra — Krishna, Guntur, and West Godavari districts. They are the wealthiest community in Andhra Pradesh per capita, controlling the majority of the state's commercial agriculture, media, and film industry. (The majority of Tollywood's major studios and production companies are Kamma-owned.) They also provide the TDP — Telugu Desam Party, which has governed AP for significant periods — with its core leadership. The Tsundur massacre (1991) was carried out by Kamma landlords against Dalit workers who had been organising for wages and whose children had started entering the medical and engineering entrance exam pipeline. Economic mobility was the provocation. The massacre was the message: your children are not permitted in those professions.

The Jogini system — the Telangana name for the devadasi system — remains extensively documented in Telangana's Nalgonda, Warangal, Karimnagar, and Nizamabad districts. The National Commission for Women documented over 80,000 Joginis in Telangana as of the late 2010s, almost all from SC families. The system functions identically to the devadasi system elsewhere: young Dalit girls dedicated to goddesses (most commonly Yellamma or Renuka Yellamma), then available to upper-caste male patrons. The Telangana government has run de-dedication programmes, but rights organisations document continued practices in rural areas where district officials do not enforce the prohibition.

"In Tsundur, the Dalits who were killed had done nothing except send their children to school. That is what the landlords could not accept."
— K. Balagopal, human rights advocate, after the Tsundur massacre (1991)
Karamchedu 1985 — Water, Land, Massacre

Dalits Used a Pond. Six Were Killed.

April 19, 1985. Prakasam district. Dalit residents of Karamchedu village used a pond that Kamma-caste landlords considered theirs. Six Dalit men were killed, women were raped, dozens of homes burned. The massacre galvanised Andhra Pradesh's Dalit movement and directly led to the founding of the Andhra Pradesh Dalit Mahasabha — which became one of the most important Dalit civil society organisations in South India. Convictions were progressively eroded on appeal over the following decades.

Tsundur 1991 — Education as Crime

Dalit Children in Professional Courses. 8 Killed.

August 1991. Guntur district. A Dalit boy accidentally brushed a Kamma woman's foot and apologised. The Kamma community enforced a total economic boycott. Then 400 armed men attacked. Eight Dalit men were killed, bodies thrown into a canal. The deeper cause: Dalit children had started entering professional colleges. Their upward mobility threatened the Kamma monopoly on educated professions. AP HC acquitted all accused in 2014 — 23 years later. SC partially restored convictions in 2022.

Jogini System

80,000+ in Telangana — The Telangana Devadasi

The Jogini system — Telangana's form of the devadasi system — covers an estimated 80,000+ women, almost all from SC communities. Girls are dedicated to Yellamma Goddess in ceremonies that designate them as sexual property of upper-caste temple patrons. The Telangana government runs an official de-dedication programme that has produced near-zero enforcement in rural districts. District officials routinely classify documented Joginis as "voluntarily religious women" to avoid applying the law.

Naxalite Movement — Both Sides of the Story

When Dalit Workers Organised, the State Called Them Terrorists

In the 1980s and 1990s, Dalit and Adivasi agricultural workers in Karimnagar, Warangal, and Adilabad formed Communist/Naxalite organisations demanding minimum wages, land redistribution, and an end to bonded labour. The state's response: military counterinsurgency operations that killed hundreds of activists, most of them Dalit. The landlords who enforced bonded labour were not targeted by counterinsurgency. The Dalits who organised against it were. This is documented in state human rights commission reports from the period.

Punjab holds one of Indian caste history's sharpest contradictions. 32% of Punjab's population is Dalit — the highest proportion of any Indian state, nearly double the national average of 16.6%. The state's founding religion — Sikhism — is perhaps the most explicitly anti-caste major religion in the world. The Guru Granth Sahib includes the poetry of Dalit saint Ravidas and Muslim weaver Kabir. Guru Nanak's founding institution — the langar, the communal meal where everyone eats together regardless of caste or religion — was designed specifically as an anti-caste practice. The Sikh Gurus explicitly and repeatedly condemned the Brahminical caste hierarchy as false and ungodly. And yet: Punjab's Dalit majority owns less than 3% of the state's agricultural land. Dalit Sikhs are excluded from or segregated in Jat-dominated gurdwaras. The drug epidemic that has devastated Punjab's Mazhabi Sikh youth — documented in every household survey of the state — is supplied by networks with documented connections to Jat political figures who face no prosecution.

Jat Sikhs — approximately 25% of Punjab's population — own the overwhelming majority of Punjab's agricultural land, dominate the Shiromani Akali Dal and the Green Revolution agrarian economy, and control most of Punjab's gurdwaras through the Shiromani Gurdwara Parbandhak Committee (SGPC). Their dominance was consolidated by the Green Revolution of the 1960s–1980s, which provided credit, technology, and land-titling support that overwhelmingly benefited Jat landowners and left Dalit landless labourers in permanent debt to the landlords who employed them. The irony is precise: the Green Revolution was presented as a triumph of economic modernisation. For Dalit communities in Punjab, it deepened dependency.

The panchayat land issue is the most sustained and documented form of economic violence against Dalits in Punjab. Indian law mandates that one-third of all village panchayat common land must be allocated to Scheduled Caste households. Rights organisations have documented that in over 90% of Punjab's villages, this mandatory allocation has never occurred. When Dalit communities attempt to claim their legal share — sometimes by simply sitting on the land and declaring their legal right — Jat-dominated panchayats respond with: counter-cases filed against the Dalits for "trespassing" on their own legal allotment; social boycott of the Dalit community; physical violence against those who attempt to farm the land; and police cases filed against Dalit activists. This has been documented, litigated in the Punjab and Haryana High Court, and reported in media for three decades. The allocation still has not happened in the majority of villages.

Punjab's two main Dalit Sikh communities are the Mazhabi Sikhs (traditionally sweepers — from mazhab, religion, i.e., those who converted to Sikhism from the sweeper-caste) and the Ramdasi Sikhs (traditionally cobblers and leatherworkers). Both communities have been systematically excluded from the Jat-dominated SGPC and the mainstream gurdwara management structure. They built their own separate places of worship — the Dera Ravidass movement, which venerates the Dalit saint Ravidas, grew into a substantial parallel religious structure with millions of adherents, its own prayer text, and its own social welfare network. In 2009, Ravidass leader Sant Niranjan Dass was assassinated in Vienna by Sikh extremists who viewed the separate Ravidass religious identity as a challenge to Sikh unity. The assassination triggered riots across Punjab. Three people died, hundreds were injured. The Ravidassia community subsequently formally separated from mainstream Sikhism as a distinct religion.

"Guru Nanak said: There is no Hindu. There is no Muslim. There is only God, and all are equal before God. Ninety years after his death, his own followers had rebuilt the caste system in his name."
— Dalit Sikh scholar, interview, Jalandhar 2021
The Theology-Reality Gap

Sikhism's Anti-Caste Teaching vs. Jat Sikh Practice

The Guru Granth Sahib includes poetry by Ravidas (Chamar caste, cobbler), Kabir (Muslim weaver), Farid (Muslim Sufi), Namdev (Mali caste) — explicitly including voices from the lowest rungs of caste and religious hierarchy. The langar is compulsory communal eating regardless of caste. Guru Nanak established marriage rites without Brahmin priests. All of this is Sikhism's founding anti-caste heritage. Punjab's Jat Sikhs rebuilt caste hierarchy within this framework by using economic power rather than religious authority — controlling land and gurdwaras rather than temples and texts.

Panchayat Land — 30 Years of Denied Rights

Legal Right, Zero Enforcement

1/3 panchayat land for SCs: mandatory under Indian law since the Panchayati Raj Amendment. Punjab's compliance rate: under 10% by activist survey. When Dalit communities in Sangrur, Moga, and Hoshiarpur districts attempted to claim legal land allotments, they faced violence, fabricated trespassing charges, and social boycott. Punjab's government has occasionally issued circulars demanding implementation. Jat panchayats have ignored them for 30 years. No Jat panchayat member has been prosecuted for refusing to implement a mandatory legal provision.

Dera Ravidass — Separation as Survival

When Excluded, Build Your Own Institution

The Dera Ravidass movement — centred in Jalandhar, with millions of followers — emerged because Mazhabi and Ramdasi Sikhs were excluded from mainstream gurdwara management, treated as second-class participants in the faith, and denied equal status in Jat-controlled Sikh institutions. The 2009 assassination of Sant Niranjan Dass confirmed that Jat Sikh religious establishment viewed Dalit Sikh religious autonomy as a threat serious enough to kill for. The Ravidassia community's formal separation from Sikhism is the logical outcome of 300 years of the faith's anti-caste teaching being systematically violated by its dominant-caste practitioners.

The Drug Crisis

Mazhabi Youth Die. Supply Chains Stay Intact.

Punjab's opioid epidemic is documented in every household survey: overwhelmingly Mazhabi and Ramdasi Dalit youth, unemployed, landless, with no economic future given the land exclusion. The supply chains for pharmaceutical opioids (tramadol, etc.) smuggled into Punjab are documented in investigative journalism as connected to networks involving political figures from dominant caste communities. Government "de-addiction" programmes target users — arresting young Dalit men with possession charges. The supply side faces near-zero prosecution. The epidemic is destroying the generation that grew up after the Green Revolution's promises failed them.

Kerala is internationally known as the "model state" — the state that achieved near-universal literacy, the highest Human Development Index in India, near-zero child mortality, and a functioning public health system that became globally famous during the COVID-19 pandemic. It is held up by development economists worldwide as proof that social investment produces better outcomes than pure growth. What is almost never mentioned in these accounts: Kerala had one of the most extreme caste systems in pre-modern India, its Dalit communities remain significantly disadvantaged relative to other groups despite the state's overall development, and the "Kerala model" was built substantially on the labour of Adivasi and Dalit communities whose land was systematically seized during the colonial and early post-independence periods.

Pre-reform Kerala's caste system was among the most literally dehumanising in recorded history. The Pulaya and Paraya communities (the main Dalit groups) were not merely untouchable — they were "unapproachable" and "unseeable." Different communities had different "pollution distances" — the distance at which their presence contaminated upper-caste purity. Nair caste members required 32 feet of distance from a Pulaya. Namboothiri Brahmin required 64 feet. A Pulaya had to shout warnings as they walked through upper-caste areas. They could not walk on public roads used by upper castes. They could not enter any public building. Women of these communities were prohibited from covering their upper bodies before dominant-caste men — a form of forced sexual exhibition that was the norm until the 19th-century reform movements challenged it. The "breast cloth controversy" (Channar lahala, 1822–1859) — in which lower-caste women who converted to Christianity began wearing upper-body coverings — was met with violent attacks by Nair men who saw it as an assertion of equality. The British eventually allowed the practice. Thirty-seven years of violence surrounded a woman's right to cover her chest.

The Left movement — particularly the Communist Party of India (Marxist), which has governed Kerala for extended periods since 1957 — is credited with the land reforms that broke feudal landlord power, the unionisation of agricultural workers, and the universal education drive. These are real achievements. But they were insufficient for Dalits in specific ways. The land reforms redistributed tenanted land from landlords to upper-caste tenants — Nairs, Ezhavas — who were themselves tenants but not the landless at the very bottom. Dalit labourers who had worked the land for generations received almost no land in the redistribution. The forest and plantation land occupied by Adivasi and Dalit communities was largely retained by state plantation corporations or private companies rather than redistributed. The Adivasi land struggle — culminating in the 2001 occupation of the Muthanga forest reserve by Adivasi communities demanding land — was met by police firing. Two people died. The government's response was to arrest K.C. Sreekumar, the movement leader, rather than provide land.

Kerala's Dalit communities — Pulayas, Parayas, Vettuvas, Eravalans — remain significantly disadvantaged relative to the state's overall indicators. Dalit literacy (though high by national standards) lags the state average. Dalit landlessness is near-total in rural areas. The conversion of Dalit communities to Christianity (through missionary activity beginning in the 19th century) brought some improvement in education and social status, but did not eliminate caste discrimination — Kerala's Syrian Christian communities maintain caste practices internally, and inter-caste marriage between Christians from different castes is socially resisted. The "Kerala model" is real in aggregate. Its benefits did not reach the bottom equally.

"Kerala's Pulayas could not walk on a public road. Could not cover their bodies. Could not approach a Brahmin within 64 feet. This was not ancient history. This was within living memory of the independence generation."
— Historian K.N. Panikkar, on pre-reform Kerala
The "Unapproachability" System

India's Most Extreme Untouchability — In a "Progressive" State

Pre-colonial and colonial-era Kerala had graded pollution distances enforced by violence: Nairs required 32 feet from Pulayas; Namboothiri Brahmins required 64 feet; some accounts describe even greater distances for Vetan and Pulaya communities. Women of these communities were prohibited from covering their upper bodies before upper-caste men — not because of poverty but as a social enforcement of subordination. The "breast cloth controversy" of 1822–1859 involved violence, court battles, and eventually British intervention to allow lower-caste women to dress modestly. The extremity of Kerala's caste system is what makes its eventual reform so remarkable — and its persistent Dalit disadvantage so telling.

Land Reform's Incomplete Revolution

The Land Went Up, Not Down

Kerala's 1969 Land Reforms Act — implemented by the CPM government — is one of India's most studied policy successes. It abolished landlordism and gave ownership to tenants. The problem: tenants were mainly Nairs and Ezhavas — mid-level caste communities, not Dalit labourers at the very bottom. The landless Dalit agricultural workers who had worked the redistributed land for generations were not given it. They remained landless labourers on land now owned by slightly less powerful castes. The bottom of the hierarchy did not change hands. It just changed bosses.

Muthanga 2003 — Police Firing on Land Protest

Adivasi Land Occupation Ended with Deaths

January 2003: Adivasi communities in Wayanad, led by CK Janu and the Adivasi Gothra Maha Sabha, occupied the Muthanga forest reserve — demanding the 50,000 acres of forest land that the government had promised them in settlement agreements. After weeks of occupation, police fired. Two people died (one tribal man, one police officer). The Kerala High Court later held that the government had failed to fulfil its land promise. The CPM government's response to the occupation — police firing on communities demanding what was legally promised to them — is the definitive counter-evidence to the "Kerala model" narrative.

The Model State Contradiction

HDI Ranks First. Dalit Land Ownership: Near Zero.

Kerala's HDI achievements are genuine — literacy, life expectancy, infant mortality, women's education are all outstanding by Indian and developing-world standards. The contradiction: Dalit communities in rural Kerala remain nearly completely landless; Dalit representation in Kerala's professional classes (medicine, law, senior government) remains below their population share despite decades of reservation; and caste identity remains a strong determinant of marriage choice, neighbourhood, and informal social interaction across the state. The "model" eliminated the worst atrocities. It did not eliminate caste.

Northeast India — the eight states of Assam, Meghalaya, Manipur, Mizoram, Nagaland, Tripura, Arunachal Pradesh, and Sikkim — has a caste structure that diverges significantly from the Hindu-Brahminical model dominant in mainland India. The region is predominantly tribal, with large Christian majorities in Meghalaya, Nagaland, and Mizoram, and significant animist, Buddhist, and syncretic religious communities across all eight states. Scheduled Tribe (ST) communities — not Scheduled Castes — are the primary marginalised group in most northeastern states. But to say there is no caste in the Northeast is incorrect: there are hereditary hierarchies, clan-based discrimination, and forms of descent-based stigma that share the structural logic of caste without necessarily sharing its Sanskrit theological justification.

In Assam — the most "mainland" of the northeastern states, with a large Hindu-Bengali and Assamese population — the Varna-based caste system operates in much the same way as in West Bengal and eastern UP: Brahmins and Kayasthas dominate the administrative, intellectual, and professional elite; Bengali Namasudra and Assamese Dalit communities (including the Mishing, Rabha, and plantation-worker Adivasi communities) face discrimination and exclusion. The tea plantation workers of Assam — brought by the British from Jharkhand, Odisha, and Chhattisgarh in the 19th century to build the tea industry — are a specific case study in induced Dalit labour migration. Approximately 6 million people live on Assam's 800+ tea estates, predominantly from Oraon, Munda, and Santhali Adivasi-Dalit communities. They are spatially isolated on estates, politically disenfranchised (many lack documents for the citizenship process), economically captive (wages and housing tied to estate employment), and receive healthcare and education from the estate management — meaning their entire life infrastructure is controlled by plantation companies. Their condition is continuous with the colonial plantation economy that created it.

In the Naga, Mizo, and Kuki societies of Nagaland, Mizoram, and Manipur's hill districts, clan-based hierarchies function analogously to caste: certain clans are considered noble (chiefs' lineages), certain clans are considered commoner, and certain groups — including blacksmiths, basket-weavers, and those associated with specific kinds of physical labour — are regarded as lower-status. These distinctions are not as rigidly enforced as Hindu caste, and Christian missionary education has substantially eroded their formal institutional basis, but they persist in marriage preferences, social networking, and informal status hierarchies. In some Naga tribes, the Yimchunger and Sumi tribes maintain clan hierarchies in which certain clans are traditionally excluded from leadership roles regardless of individual achievement. Inter-clan marriage across the high-low divide faces family opposition in a pattern structurally similar to inter-caste marriage opposition in mainland India.

In Manipur, the Meitei community — the dominant valley community — maintains caste divisions within Hinduism (Meitei Brahmins are called Bamon; there are Meitei Kshatriyas and commoner groups) that operate alongside the more extensively studied tribal-non-tribal conflict between Meitei and Kuki-Zo communities. The Manipur ethnic conflict that exploded in May 2023 — killing hundreds and displacing tens of thousands — is primarily framed as a tribal-non-tribal conflict over Scheduled Tribe status and forest land. But within the Meitei community, caste hierarchies among Bamon, Ningthouja, and lower-status Meitei groups also shape access to temple management, marriage, and social status. The intersection of ethnicity, religion, and caste in Manipur is the most complex in the Northeast and among the most complex in India.

"Do not tell me there is no caste in the Northeast. Come to an Assamese Brahmin's household. Come to a Naga chief's village. The hierarchy has different names. The logic is the same."
— Northeast human rights researcher, Guwahati, interview 2022
Assam Tea Garden Workers

6 Million People — Colonial Labour Trap, Still Operating

The British recruited 6 million workers from Jharkhand, Odisha, and Chhattisgarh to build Assam's tea industry, promising land after five years. The land was never given. Workers remained estate-bound through debt and housing dependency. Today, approximately 6 million people on Assam's 800+ tea gardens are predominantly Oraon, Munda, and Santhali. Their wages are below state minimum wage. Their NRC documentation status is precarious — many lack citizenship documents, making them vulnerable to detention. The National Register of Citizens process has specifically threatened these communities with statelessness. Colonial capitalism created their captivity. Post-independence India has maintained it.

NRC — Citizenship as Weapon

Tea Garden Communities at Risk of Statelessness

Assam's National Register of Citizens update (2019) created a list of "genuine Indian citizens." Approximately 1.9 million people were excluded from the final NRC list — disproportionately Bengali-speaking Hindus and tea garden Adivasi communities who lack documentation proving continuous residence before 1971. The tea garden workers' communities have lived in Assam for 150 years, but many lack the official paperwork — land documents, birth certificates — that the NRC required, because they lived on private estates where such documentation was controlled by plantation companies. The potential statelessness of communities that built Assam's tea economy is the final stage of the colonial extraction: their labour was taken, and now their citizenship is being questioned.

Tribal Hierarchies in the Hills

Caste Without Hinduism — Again

Naga, Kuki, Mizo, and Manipuri tribal societies maintain clan-based status hierarchies that share the structural logic of caste — hereditary, endogamy-enforcing, occupationally linked — without the Sanskrit theological framework. Blacksmiths, weavers, and other artisan groups are lower-status across multiple northeastern tribal societies. In Manipur's Meitei community, the Bamon (Brahmin equivalent) caste maintains temple control and marriage preferences. The Northeast demonstrates, again, that caste logic is not a product of Hindu theology — it is a human social technology that religious frameworks may provide ideological scaffolding for but do not create.

Manipur 2023 Conflict

Ethnicity, Tribe, and Caste — The Most Complex Intersection

The May 2023 Manipur violence between Meitei and Kuki-Zo communities involved over 200 deaths, 60,000+ displaced, and systematic destruction of villages and churches. The conflict is primarily framed as tribal-non-tribal: Meiteis (valley, majority) demanding ST status; Kuki-Zo (hill, minority) viewing this as a threat to forest land protections. Within this, the existing caste hierarchies among Meiteis — Bamon priests, Ningthouja royals, lower-status Meitei groups — have their own dynamic. The conflict has also been used by armed groups to enforce ethnic cleansing along geographic lines, with Dalit and lower-caste communities on both sides of the ethnic divide catching the worst of the displacement.

→
📚
Academic Research — Decades of Scholarship
Sociology, Economics, History, International Studies, Dalit scholars — 20+ landmark works
Data12
20+ Works
▼
1936

Annihilation of Caste

B.R. Ambedkar

A speech never delivered (host cancelled on reading draft). Calls not for reform but destruction of caste. Argues caste is intrinsic to Hinduism. Provoked famous public debate with Gandhi. The urtext of Dalit politics.

1999

Broken People

Human Rights Watch

First comprehensive international documentation of caste-based violence as systematic rather than incidental. Changed global understanding of how rape, massacre, and terror function within the caste system.

2001

Castes of Mind

Nicholas Dirks (Columbia)

Argues British colonialism "hardened" caste into the rigid system we know today. Provocative — critiqued by Ambedkarites for downplaying pre-colonial caste violence.

2009

Caste, Class, Gender

Uma Chakravarti

Introduces "Brahmanical patriarchy" — the intersection of caste and gender. How caste "purity" is maintained through controlling women's sexuality. Foundational feminist text.

2007

Blocked by Caste

Thorat & Newman (eds)

Documents systematic economic exclusion of Dalits across labour markets, credit access, and entrepreneurship. Caste discrimination is not a relic — it is an active economic mechanism in liberalised India.

2019

Grammar of Caste — Field Experiment

Thorat & Attewell

Identical CVs with upper-caste and lower-caste names sent to private employers. Upper-caste names received far more callbacks. Caste discrimination is measurable and real in India's private sector.

2020

India Inequality Report

Oxfam India

Dalit women face "triple disadvantage" in income, land, and labour. SC/ST median wealth is ~40% of national average. The gap is widening in liberalised India, not closing.

2020

Caste: The Origins of Our Discontents

Isabel Wilkerson (Pulitzer winner)

Compares India, Nazi Germany, and USA as three caste systems sharing eight structural pillars. Global bestseller. Reignited race-caste debate. Energised Dalit diaspora activism in the US.

2020

Caste in Silicon Valley

Equality Labs Survey

67% of US Dalits experienced caste discrimination at work. 59% in educational settings. Dalit engineers excluded from teams, passed over for promotions. Led to Cisco lawsuit settlement 2023.

2016

UN Report on Descent-Based Discrimination

UN Special Rapporteur

Documents 260 million people globally affected by caste-like systems. Calls for international legal frameworks equivalent to racial discrimination laws. India lobbied to prevent explicit mention of "caste."

1996

Why I Am Not a Hindu

Kancha Ilaiah Shepherd

Argues Dalit-Bahujan cultures have a different epistemology, work ethic, and relationship to nature than Brahminical Hinduism. Provocative and foundational Dalit philosophy text.

2018

Republic of Caste

Anand Teltumbde

How Indian capitalism and upper-caste politics intersect to maintain caste hierarchy. Author arrested under UAPA in 2020. The book's thesis is proven by his arrest.

2019

Caste Matters

Suraj Yengde (Harvard)

Harvard-educated Dalit scholar examines Dalit identity in the diaspora, Silicon Valley, and the future of the movement. Argues "Dalit capitalism" alone cannot solve caste without structural political change.

2004

Towards an Aesthetic of Dalit Literature

Sharankumar Limbale

Dalit literature is not just about Dalits — it is a different epistemological project requiring different critical frameworks. Foundational text in Dalit literary theory.

→
⚖
Legal Framework — The Law & Its Limits
India has the world's strongest constitutional protections against caste discrimination. Implementation is another story.
Data11
8 Laws
▼
"Constitutional morality is not a natural sentiment. It has to be cultivated. We must realise that our people have yet to learn it."
— B.R. Ambedkar, Constituent Assembly Debates, 1949
Art. 17

Abolition of Untouchability

The cornerstone. Abolishes untouchability in any form. Practice is criminal under Protection of Civil Rights Act, 1955. 50,000+ violations registered annually.

Art. 15

Non-Discrimination

Prohibits discrimination on grounds of caste, religion, race, sex. Prohibits restriction on public places, wells, tanks, shops on these grounds.

Art. 16

Equal Opportunity

Guarantees equality in public employment. Constitutional basis for SC/ST reservation. Private sector excluded — where 90%+ of India's economy operates.

1989

SC/ST Atrocities Act

22 specific crimes against Dalits with enhanced penalties and special courts. In 2018, SC weakened it — sparked India's largest Dalit strike. Amendment eventually restored. Conviction rate: ~10%.

2013

Manual Scavenging Act

Bans manual scavenging — cleaning sewers by hand. Third time it has been banned (1955, 1993, 2013). 282+ sewer deaths 2017–2020 officially. Enforcement: near-zero.

15%

SC Reservation

15% Central Government jobs and education for SCs, 7.5% for STs, 27% for OBCs. Does not apply to private sector. Has significantly increased Dalit presence in bureaucracy — while creating upper-caste political backlash since the 1990s.

→
✊
Movements That Changed India
Satyashodhak Samaj to Bhim Army — organised resistance across 150 years
Resistance19
6 Movements
▼
Founded 1873

Satyashodhak Samaj

Jyotirao Phule's Truth-Seekers' Society — first organised anti-caste movement in modern India. Conducted marriages without Brahmin priests. Promoted Dalit and women's education 50 years before Ambedkar.

1920s–1956

Ambedkar Movement

Mahad Satyagraha (1927), Kalaram Temple Entry (1930), Poona Pact (1932), Constitution drafting (1950), mass Buddhist conversion (1956). Most comprehensive liberation movement in Indian history.

Founded 1972

Dalit Panthers

Namdeo Dhasal, Maharashtra. Inspired by Black Panthers. 1973 manifesto: solidarity with all oppressed globally. Transformed Dalit identity from shame to defiant pride through poetry, art, confrontational politics.

Founded 1984

Bahujan Samaj Party

Kanshi Ram built SC/ST/OBC coalition. Mayawati became first Dalit woman CM of UP (India's most populous state). Proved Dalit politics could win mainstream electoral power — then face systematic dismantling.

2016–present

#DalitLivesMatter

Born from Rohith Vemula's death and Una flogging. First major Dalit movement to break through to international media via social media. Connected Indian Dalit activism to global anti-racism conversations.

2016–present

Bhim Army

Chandra Shekhar Azad. Free education centres for Dalit youth. Legal aid. Confrontational street politics. Most visible Dalit youth movement in contemporary North India — especially UP and Rajasthan.

🎭
Literature & Film — Dalit Voices in Culture
Books, poetry, and films that changed how India saw itself
Resistance20
12 Works
▼
Autobiography

Joothan

Omprakash Valmiki · 1997

Most widely read Dalit autobiography. Western UP. Forced to clean school grounds instead of studying. Visceral, essential. The word joothan — leftovers given as wages — becomes a metaphor for Dalit life.

Autobiography

Karukku

Bama · 1992 (Tamil)

Dalit Christian Tamil woman's memoir. Caste within Christianity. "Karukku" = sharp edge of palmyra leaf = sharp critique. Landmark of Tamil Dalit women's writing.

Poetry

Golpitha

Namdeo Dhasal · 1972

Named after a Mumbai red-light district. Brought slum language into Marathi literature. "The most terrifying poetry written in any Indian language." Raw, furious, technically brilliant.

Novel

The Gypsy Goddess

Meena Kandasamy · 2014

A novel about the Kilvenmani massacre of 1968. Reconstructs the night 44 Dalits were burned alive. One of the most important books about caste violence ever written.

Autobiography

Baluta

Daya Pawar · 1978

First major Dalit autobiography in Marathi. Growing up Mahar in Mumbai's slums. Broke every convention of Marathi literary culture. The founding text of a new tradition.

Memoir/History

Ants Among Elephants

Sujatha Gidla · 2017

Dalit Christian family history, told by a New York City subway conductor. Brought Dalit reality to global English audiences. Essential and accessible for non-Indian readers.

Film

Fandry

Nagraj Manjule · 2013

Dalit boy catching pigs, dreaming of a higher-caste girl. Final scene — a stone thrown at the audience — is the most radical act in Indian cinema. It said: enough.

Film

Sairat

Nagraj Manjule · 2016

Inter-caste love story — and its violent destruction. Highest-grossing Marathi film ever. Its tragic ending made mainstream audiences confront what caste does to love.

Film

Jai Bhim

T.J. Gnanavel · 2021

Based on the real 1995 Rajakannu custodial torture case in Tamil Nadu. Amazon Prime's most-watched film globally for weeks. Rare mainstream film that names police caste violence directly.

Film

Article 15

Anubhav Sinha · 2019

Brahmin IPS officer investigates gang rape of two Dalit women. Unusually direct for Bollywood in naming caste as cause of violence. Its title is the constitutional article against caste discrimination.

Theory

No Nation for Women

Priyanka Dubey · 2018

Ground-level investigation of sexual violence focused on Dalit women. Documents cases where police refused FIRs, families were threatened, perpetrators walked free. Essential companion to the statistics.

Urtext

Annihilation of Caste

B.R. Ambedkar · 1936

Every book in this list exists in its shadow. Not just politics — a work of extraordinary philosophical depth on religion, reason, morality. The book the host refused to let be delivered.

→
❓
Frequently Asked Questions
The hard questions, answered honestly
Reference23
28 Q&A
▼
How to read this section

Every question below has been heard — in drawing rooms, comment sections, universities, corporate offices, and TV debates. They are not stupid questions. They are the questions upper-caste India has developed over generations to make the caste system seem inevitable, benign, or someone else's problem. Each one deserves a complete answer. These are the complete answers.

The "It Doesn't Exist Anymore" Arguments
COMMON CLAIM "Caste discrimination is illegal. It doesn't really happen anymore." +
THE REALITY

India registered 50,291 crimes against SC communities in 2022 alone — the highest ever recorded. This is after 75 years of it being illegal. The NCRB number is the undercount: most Dalits don't report because the police officer they'd report to is often from the perpetrator's caste. Rights organisations estimate actual crimes at 5–10x registered figures — approximately 250,000–500,000 per year. One crime against a Dalit person every 60–120 seconds. Every day. In the country where it is illegal.

Illegality and reality are different categories. Dowry is illegal. Murder is illegal. Rape is illegal. The question is not what the law says — it is what happens when you try to use the law if you are Dalit. The conviction rate under the SC/ST Atrocities Act is approximately 10%. 90% of registered cases produce no conviction. If you know the conviction rate is 10%, the police are hostile, and filing a case means your family faces economic boycott and counter-charges — you don't file. The system's failure to prosecute is indistinguishable, in outcome, from the system not existing.

COMMON CLAIM "I don't practice caste. My generation doesn't care about caste." +
THE REALITY

95% of Indian marriages are within caste. Not 95% of your parents' generation — 95% right now, including in cities, including among the educated, including among people who "don't care about caste." When you marry within caste, you inherit within caste, network within caste, recommend within caste. You don't have to believe in caste for caste to structure your life and your choices.

More precisely: upper-caste people who "don't practice caste" benefit from three thousand years of accumulated advantage — land, capital, social networks, institutional positions — without doing anything actively. You don't have to burn a Dalit's house to benefit from a system that burned Dalit houses for centuries. You just have to inherit. The beneficiary of a robbery doesn't have to be the robber to keep the goods. Not practicing caste today does not return what caste took. It just means you're comfortable with keeping it.

COMMON CLAIM "Urban India has moved beyond caste. This is a rural problem." +
THE REALITY

The Oxfam-Newslaundry 2019 survey of 121 major Indian media organisations — based in Mumbai, Delhi, Bengaluru, Hyderabad — found zero Dalit editors in senior leadership positions across all 121. Urban India's corporate sector has no caste non-discrimination policy in 98% of companies. IIT Bombay, IIT Delhi, IIM Ahmedabad — urban, elite, educated — have documented anti-Dalit discrimination affecting student dropout rates, mental health outcomes, and campus deaths.

In urban housing markets, "brahmin/upper-caste family preferred" and "vegetarian only" listings function as caste exclusion in secular language. Dalit applicants in Mumbai face rejection at 2–3x the rate of comparable upper-caste applicants in private rental markets. Silicon Valley — as urban and global as any place on earth — had Dalit engineers filing discrimination complaints against upper-caste Indian managers in 2020. The city doesn't erase caste. It just gives caste better vocabulary.

The "Merit" Arguments
COMMON CLAIM "Reservation destroys merit. IITs and IIMs are being diluted." +
THE REALITY

This argument assumes the current system produces merit. It does not. It produces upper-caste advantage dressed as merit. Brahmin and upper-caste families have had exclusive access to Sanskrit education, temple economics, trade networks, and professional employment for 3,000 years. OBCs and Dalits were legally forbidden from education — the Manusmriti prescribed pouring molten lead into the ears of a Shudra who heard the Vedas. You cannot exclude a community from education for 3,000 years and then call their lower test scores evidence of lower merit. You produced those scores. With policy. Maintained by violence.

The actual evidence: Studies of IIT Dalit graduates show no difference in career outcomes compared to general category graduates 10 years post-graduation. The "merit gap" at entry disappears at the workplace — because the entry gap was produced by unequal schooling, not by inherent difference. Meanwhile, the 49.5% of IIT seats filled by general category candidates include: legacy admissions (children of alumni), coaching industry access (₹1–5 lakh/year, accessible to whom?), and social capital in navigating the system. None of that is "merit." It is inherited advantage renamed.

COMMON CLAIM "Why should my child suffer for sins committed 1,000 years ago?" +
THE REALITY

Your child is not suffering. Your child is receiving a smaller share of an advantage they did not earn. These are different things. A Dalit child grows up in a village where they cannot drink from the same well, attend school without being humiliated, marry across caste without their family being killed, or enter certain temples. They are suffering. Your child did not get into IIT on a general seat. They are competing. The language of "suffering" for the reduction of unearned privilege is a category error that reveals how accustomed to that privilege you have become.

More precisely: the discrimination did not end 1,000 years ago. Kilvenmani was 1968. Belchi was 1977. Karamchedu was 1985. Laxmanpur Bathe was 1997. Khairlanji was 2006. Hathras was 2020. Una was 2016. The oppression is not historical. It is contemporary. The response — reservation — is proportional to an ongoing injury, not a historical one. The question "why should my child suffer for past sins" would make sense if the sins had stopped. They have not stopped. Your child's slight disadvantage in IIT admissions is the state's partial, inadequate response to an ongoing crime.

COMMON CLAIM "There are creamy layer Dalits taking all the reservation benefits. Poor Brahmins don't get anything." +
THE REALITY

The "creamy layer" for OBCs is ₹8 lakh annual family income — above which reservation is unavailable. No creamy layer exists for SCs and STs. The Dalit community does not have a "creamy layer" problem in any meaningful sense: the median SC household income in India is approximately ₹96,000 annually. The number of SC families above ₹8 lakh is a tiny fraction of the community — and even those families face caste discrimination in housing, marriage, and social life that income does not erase.

As for "poor Brahmins": the EWS (Economically Weaker Section) 10% reservation, added in 2019, was explicitly designed to give reservation benefits to poor upper-caste communities. It is now law. What the "poor Brahmin" argument actually does is conflate class poverty (which EWS addresses) with caste discrimination (which SC/ST reservation addresses). They are different injuries. A poor Brahmin is not barred from temples, not prevented from drawing water from the same well, not called by a slur that marks them as subhuman. They are economically disadvantaged — a real problem, addressed by EWS. A Dalit faces economic disadvantage plus caste-based dehumanisation. Reservation addresses the second. EWS addresses the first. Both exist.

The Culture / Religion Shield
COMMON CLAIM "Varna was originally about profession, not birth. It got corrupted later." +
THE REALITY

This is the most popular apologetics argument and the most historically dishonest. The Purusha Sukta in the Rigveda — the oldest text — places Brahmins from the mouth of the cosmic being, Kshatriyas from the arms, Vaishyas from the thighs, and Shudras from the feet. This is not a description of profession. This is a cosmological ranking of human beings by birth. The Manusmriti — which is not a "corruption" but the systematic legal codification of Brahminical society — explicitly states that the birth of a Shudra is determined by karma, that a Brahmin must not eat food touched by a Shudra, and that untouchables must announce their presence so upper castes can avoid contamination. None of this is about job function.

Even if the "originally about profession" claim were true — and the textual evidence does not support it — what matters is 3,000 years of actual practice. You do not get to claim the theoretical purity of a system whose entire recorded history consists of its application as birth-based hierarchy. The argument is: "what we did wasn't what we meant." But what you did is what you did. The Dalit boy who could not enter the school was excluded by what you did, not by what you meant.

COMMON CLAIM "Hinduism is being blamed unfairly. Every religion has problems." +
THE REALITY

Every religion does have problems. Caste exists inside Islam, Christianity, and Sikhism in India — documented thoroughly in this archive. This is not a defence of Brahminical Hinduism. The question is not whether other religions have failed on caste — they have — but whether the specific textual, institutional, and theological structure of Brahminical Hinduism produces caste hierarchy in a way that makes internal reform exceptionally difficult. Ambedkar argued yes, and the 600 years of Bhakti movement followed by zero structural change is his evidence. The saints challenged cosmic hierarchy. They were absorbed and their radicalism sanitised without altering the Brahmin control of temples, priestly roles, or religious economic structures.

Specifically: the karma doctrine converts Dalit suffering into cosmic justice. It does not just justify the hierarchy — it makes resistance to the hierarchy morally bad (defying your dharma, accumulating bad karma). No other major world religion has built into its core theology a mechanism that makes the oppressed person's resistance the sin and the oppressor's domination the virtue. This is not anti-Hindu. This is what Ambedkar documented in Annihilation of Caste in 1936. The document that the Indian National Congress refused to publish at the time.

COMMON CLAIM "Dalits are using caste as a political tool. They don't really face discrimination." +
THE REALITY

The people most likely to believe caste discrimination doesn't happen are people whose caste means they've never experienced it and never had to pay attention to it. This is definitionally the group whose testimony on the subject is least reliable. The evidence for caste discrimination is not testimony — it is experimental. Correspondence studies in India and abroad (sending identical CVs with Dalit vs upper-caste names) consistently show Dalit applicants receive fewer callbacks. Land access, water access, and school enrollment studies show persistent gaps that correlate with caste after controlling for income. The NCRB data is not "political tool" — it is filed police reports.

The "political tool" argument functions as follows: when Dalits don't organise, upper castes say "see, no problem." When Dalits do organise, upper castes say "political tool, exaggerating." There is no mode of Dalit existence that this argument accepts as evidence of discrimination. That's not analysis — it's a thought-terminating cliché designed to make the questioner feel sophisticated while requiring no engagement with data.

The Whataboutery Arguments
COMMON CLAIM "What about Muslim violence against Hindus? Why don't you talk about that?" +
THE REALITY

Hindu-Muslim violence and caste violence are both real and both documented. They are also structurally different. Hindu-Muslim violence is communal — between two groups. Caste violence is hierarchical — by dominant groups against subordinate groups within the same religious community. A Dalit victim of caste violence and a Hindu victim of communal violence are both victims. The question "what about X" when discussing Y does not make Y less real. It makes you less willing to discuss Y.

The specific relevance: upper-caste Hindus are both the primary beneficiaries of the caste system and the primary leaders of organisations that mobilise Hindus around Muslim threat. The mobilisation against Muslims is partly functional — it creates Hindu unity across caste lines that prevents Dalit and OBC political consolidation against upper-caste power. Hindutva and casteism are not opposites. Historically, organisations that mobilise most aggressively against Muslims have shown the least concern for Dalit rights. This is documented, not speculation.

COMMON CLAIM "Dalits commit crimes too. This isn't a one-sided issue." +
THE REALITY

Yes. Individual Dalits commit individual crimes — exactly as members of every community do. No one claims Dalits are incapable of crime. The argument against caste discrimination is a structural argument, not a claim about individual virtue. The structure: Dalits as a community face systematic discrimination in employment, housing, education, justice, and social life — producing conditions (poverty, stress, social exclusion) correlated with crime in every society globally. Upper-caste communities have benefited from systematic advantage. Neither fact is a moral claim about individuals. Both are empirical descriptions of a system.

The specific data that makes "Dalits commit crimes too" irrelevant as a counter-argument: 75%+ of India's undertrial prison population is from marginalised communities — Dalits, Adivasis, OBCs, Muslims. Upper castes: tiny fraction. This is not because upper castes don't commit crimes. It is because: upper castes have access to lawyers, bail money, social connections, and the ability to influence police and courts. Dalits, overwhelmingly, do not. Who gets arrested, who gets bail, who goes to trial, who gets convicted — every stage of the criminal justice system has documented caste and class bias. "They commit crimes too" describes individuals. The prison data describes a system.

Genuine Questions — Answered Honestly
Is caste the same as class?+
Class is primarily economic and can change with wealth. Caste is hereditary — determined at birth, unchangeable. A Dalit billionaire is still a Dalit. A Brahmin who becomes poor is still a Brahmin. They overlap massively — Dalits are disproportionately poor — but the mechanisms are distinct.
