Ambedkar argued caste is primarily about social exclusion enforced by religious ideology, not just economics. This distinction matters: class-based solutions (economic growth, poverty alleviation) do not address caste-based exclusion from temples, inter-caste marriage hostility, or the social humiliation that income does not erase.
Why did Ambedkar convert to Buddhism?+
Ambedkar believed caste was inseparable from Brahminical Hinduism — you could not remain Hindu and be free of caste because caste has divine sanction in Hindu scripture. He studied world religions for 21 years — considering Islam, Christianity, and Sikhism — before choosing Buddhism for its emphasis on rationality, equality, rejection of God-ordained hierarchy, and its Indian roots. He developed Navayana ("New Vehicle") — socially engaged Buddhism stripped of karma/rebirth metaphysics and centred on social liberation. October 14, 1956 in Nagpur: converted with ~600,000 followers. Died six weeks later. Today 7.95M+ Navayana Buddhists in India, concentrated in Maharashtra.
What is the difference between Dalit, SC, Harijan, and Bahujan?+
Scheduled Caste (SC) — official constitutional/legal term. A bureaucratic category listing 1,263 castes eligible for reservation benefits. Determined by the President's Lists.

Dalit — political, self-chosen identity. From Sanskrit/Marathi "broken" or "ground down." Popularised by the Dalit Panthers (1972). Carries political consciousness and reclaimed dignity. Preferred by most activists and writers.

Harijan — Gandhi's term, meaning "children of God." Widely rejected by the community as patronising — it was given to them by a non-Dalit, carries no political content, and softens the nature of the oppression with religious language.

Bahujan — "the many" — broader political term for SC + ST + OBC + minorities (the oppressed 85% majority). Kanshi Ram's term for potential coalition politics. Used by BSP and Ambedkarite movements.
What is manual scavenging and why does it still exist?+
Manual scavenging is cleaning human excreta from dry latrines and sewers by hand — without protective equipment. Practiced almost exclusively by Dalit Valmiki communities as a "hereditary occupation." Banned in 1955, 1993, and 2013 — three separate laws. Continues openly. Why? Because the people who enter sewers are the people with no alternative — economic desperation plus hereditary social assignment. And because there is no political constituency powerful enough to enforce the ban against the convenience of municipalities and property owners who benefit from cheap sewage disposal. The government pays ₹10 lakh per documented sewer death as "compensation." 282+ official deaths 2017–2020. Actual figure: far higher. The compensation is paid because the death is expected. The death is expected because the work continues. The work continues because the ban is not enforced. Because they are Dalit.
Does caste discrimination exist outside India?+
Yes — the UN estimates 260 million people globally face descent-based discrimination analogous to caste. Japan's Burakumin (~3M), West Africa's hereditary griot and artisan castes, Nepal's Dalit communities (~3.5M), Sri Lanka's Rodiya communities, Somalia's Madhiban — all face systems with the same structural logic: hereditary status, occupational confinement, enforced by social ostracism and violence. In diaspora: Dalit engineers in Silicon Valley face caste discrimination from upper-caste Indian managers. The 2020 Cisco lawsuit in California was the first US corporate caste discrimination case. Seattle became the first city globally to explicitly ban caste discrimination in law (2023). California attempted a statewide ban (SB 403) — it passed the legislature, was vetoed by Governor Newsom.
More Common Claims Refuted
COMMON CLAIM "Dalits have Ambedkar, reservation, political parties — they have enough representation and power now." +
THE REALITY

Representation in visible positions and actual power over the conditions of your life are different things. There are 84 Dalit MPs in the Lok Sabha — all on reserved seats, all bound to party whips controlled by upper-caste leadership, none able to challenge the party on caste without losing their ticket in the next election. The Supreme Court — which decides the constitutionality of reservation itself — has had approximately 3 Dalit judges in its entire history. Zero Dalit editors in 121 major media organisations. Zero Dalit CEOs in the BSE-500. These are the institutions that set the terms for everyone else.

Ambedkar was one person. His presence in the drafting of the Constitution was remarkable and consequential. It does not mean the system he fought is gone — it means one Dalit was allowed to write the document while the system continued. The BSP at its peak governed UP — and was systematically dismantled by upper-caste political mobilisation that used Hindutva to fracture the Bahujan coalition. "They have representation" describes a few visible positions. The data describes who controls land, courts, media, police, temples, and capital. Those are not the same picture.

COMMON CLAIM "My family never owned slaves or oppressed anyone. Why should I feel guilty?" +
THE REALITY

Nobody is asking you to feel guilty. Guilt is not the point and it is not useful. The point is to understand how a system works. Your family may have been entirely peaceful — and still have benefited from a system that excluded Dalits from education, land ownership, water access, and professional employment for generations. The wealth gap, the network advantage, the access to quality schools — these accumulated through the system whether or not your specific ancestors were personally violent.

The question is not guilt. It is accuracy. If you understand that your social position was partly produced by a system that disadvantaged others, you can engage with policy questions (like reservation) accurately — as a partial redistribution of systemically accumulated advantage, not as an unfair attack on innocent individuals. Guilt asks: am I a bad person? Accuracy asks: how did this system work and what does fairness require now? The second question is the useful one. You don't have to feel guilty to support justice. You just have to understand what happened.

COMMON CLAIM "Inter-caste marriages are increasing. This shows caste is dying naturally." +
THE REALITY

Inter-caste marriages are increasing from an extremely low base. NFHS-5 (2019–21) data: approximately 5% of Indian marriages are inter-caste. That is up from 3% twenty years ago. At current rates of change, endogamy will remain the overwhelming norm for generations. And the increase that exists is concentrated within broad Varna categories — upper castes marrying within upper castes, OBC communities marrying within OBC communities — not across the Dalit/non-Dalit line, which is where the most violent enforcement of endogamy occurs.

When inter-caste marriage does cross the Dalit/non-Dalit line, the response is frequently lethal. "Honour killings" for inter-caste marriages are documented at hundreds of cases per year — overwhelmingly targeting couples where one partner is Dalit. Khap Panchayats issue public diktats against such marriages, mobilise community economic boycotts, and in documented cases organise violence. The Supreme Court ruled Khap Panchayats have no legal standing in 2018. They continue operating. A phenomenon that requires murderous enforcement to prevent cannot simultaneously be described as "dying naturally."

COMMON CLAIM "Caste is just like class discrimination everywhere. Every country has inequality." +
THE REALITY

Every country has inequality — correct. Not every inequality is caste. The specific features of caste that distinguish it from generic economic inequality: it is hereditary (you cannot leave it by becoming wealthy), it is religiously sanctioned (divine text assigns your position), it is enforced by ritual pollution doctrine (your physical presence is considered contaminating), and it assigns hereditary occupation (your family's job is your job regardless of ability or preference). The combination of these four features — heredity + divine sanction + pollution ideology + occupational inheritance — is specific to caste systems. It is not generic class inequality.

The practical consequence: a poor person in Denmark can become wealthy and be fully socially integrated. A Dalit who becomes wealthy remains Dalit — cannot move into certain neighbourhoods without facing discrimination, cannot marry into upper-caste families without facing violence, cannot enter certain temple inner sanctums, is still addressed by caste slur when found assertive. Wealth changes class. It does not change caste. This is the distinction the "every country has inequality" argument erases — and erasing it conveniently removes the need to address what is specific about caste.

COMMON CLAIM "The media exaggerates caste violence. Most of it doesn't happen." +
THE REALITY

The evidence for caste violence is not the media. It is government data. The NCRB is compiled by the Ministry of Home Affairs — not by journalists or activists. It registers 50,291 crimes against SC communities in 2022 from FIRs filed with police across every state. The NFHS is a government household survey. The census is a government document. The Socio-Economic and Caste Census (2011) was conducted by the Government of India. All of this data converges on the same picture: persistent, documented, systematic disadvantage for SC communities across every measurable dimension.

What the media actually does — and this is documented — is underreport caste violence. The Oxfam-Newslaundry 2019 survey showed zero Dalit editors in 121 major news organisations. Upper-caste editorial teams routinely decide not to cover Dalit atrocity stories, downgrade their prominence, omit the caste of perpetrators, and use "clash" language that implies mutual violence. Hathras (2020) received a fraction of the coverage of the 2012 Delhi rape — despite the body being cremated at 2am by police without family consent. The media doesn't exaggerate caste violence. Upper-caste-controlled media systematically minimises it.

COMMON CLAIM "Gandhi worked for Dalits. He called them Harijans — children of God. He cared." +
THE REALITY

Gandhi opposed untouchability as a practice. He did not oppose the varna system that produced it. His 1921 statement: "Varnashrama is inherent in human nature and Hinduism has simply reduced it to a science." He believed the four-varna division of society was natural and divine. He opposed the worst excesses (denial of temple entry, physical untouchability) while insisting the underlying hierarchy was legitimate. This is the distinction Ambedkar made clearly: Gandhi wanted to reform the system from within while keeping its structure. Ambedkar wanted to annihilate the system entirely.

The specific political conflict: the 1932 Poona Pact. The British colonial government had granted Dalits separate electorates — the right to vote for their own representatives, giving them genuine political autonomy. Gandhi fasted unto death to block this, arguing separate electorates would divide Hindu society. Ambedkar, facing Gandhi's death and the resulting violence against Dalits that would follow, signed the Pact that removed the separate electorates in exchange for reserved seats within the Hindu fold. Ambedkar called this the worst moment of his political life. Gandhi's fast removed Dalit political autonomy using his own body as leverage. "Harijan" — a name Gandhi chose for them — was rejected by the community because they did not want a name chosen for them by a non-Dalit man who opposed their political independence. They chose "Dalit" themselves.

COMMON CLAIM "Brahmins are actually discriminated against now because of reservation. They are the new oppressed." +
THE REALITY

Brahmins represent approximately 4–5% of India's population. They hold approximately 37% of IAS positions, 50%+ of judiciary positions, the overwhelming majority of senior media roles, and disproportionate representation in academia, corporate leadership, and cultural institutions. Reservation has partially opened 22.5% of central government jobs and educational seats to SC/ST communities — leaving 77.5% open competition, in which Brahmins continue to win at rates far above their population share. The "Brahmin is oppressed" argument requires ignoring every structural indicator of power while focusing exclusively on the 22.5% they do not have automatic access to.

The comparison being made — between a community that controls temples, courts, media, education and IAS cadres, and a community that cannot safely enter certain temples, cannot draw from certain wells, and faces 50,000+ registered crimes per year — as equivalent forms of discrimination is not analysis. It is the claim that any reduction of overwhelming advantage is equivalent to systematic oppression. A community that had a 95% share and now has an 77% share has not been oppressed. It has been made marginally less dominant. These are not the same experience.

COMMON CLAIM "Why don't Dalits just work harder and pull themselves up? Individual effort matters." +
THE REALITY

Many do. The ones who do face specific, documented barriers that upper-caste people of identical effort do not face. Identical CVs sent to employers with Dalit-signalling names receive fewer callbacks than those with upper-caste names — this is experimental evidence, not anecdote. Dalit students in IITs face hostile environments — documented in multiple surveys — that produce higher dropout rates, higher mental health crisis rates, and in multiple documented cases, deaths. Dalit graduates with identical qualifications earn less than upper-caste counterparts in the same roles.

"Work harder" is advice that assumes the surface you're pushing against is equally hard for everyone. It is not. A Dalit child who grows up in a village where the school has upper-caste teachers who seat them separately, who faces economic boycott if their father asserts his legal right to minimum wage, who cannot easily access the coaching industry that prepares urban upper-caste students for competitive exams — this child works at the same absolute level as an upper-caste child in Delhi with access to elite coaching and parental professional networks, and produces worse outcomes. The "work harder" prescription mistakes a structural problem for an individual one. Individual effort matters. It does not operate in a vacuum. The vacuum is not equally distributed.

"The outcaste has no way of escape. He cannot go back to his caste. He cannot marry in another caste. He cannot get employment. All doors are closed against him. It is the cruelest form of social killing. It is murder while the victim is alive."
— Dr. B.R. Ambedkar, Annihilation of Caste, 1936
→
👩
Dalit Women — The Triple Burden
Caste + class + gender. The intersection every movement abandoned.
Society13
Full SectionNEW
▼

Dalit women exist at the intersection of the three most brutal hierarchies in Indian society: caste, class, and gender. Not sequentially — simultaneously, in every interaction, every day. The mainstream feminist movement in India has been dominated by upper-caste women who treat caste as a secondary, optional issue to be addressed after "women's issues" are resolved. The Dalit political movement has been dominated by men who treat gender as a secondary, optional issue to be addressed after "caste issues" are resolved. Dalit women fall through both gaps. They are not spoken for, not counted, not protected, and not led by either movement that claims to represent them. This section will not soften that. It will name the specific forms of violence unique to Dalit women, the women who fought back, and what fighting back cost them.

"I have a message for the women of the upper castes. You are fighting for your rights. We are fighting for our lives. These are not the same fight."
— Ruth Manorama, National Campaign on Dalit Human Rights

The violence Dalit women face is not merely the sum of caste violence against Dalits plus gender violence against women. It has a specific character that emerges from the intersection: upper-caste men rape Dalit women as a political act — to punish Dalit men who assert rights, to terrorise entire communities into submission, to demonstrate that Dalit bodies belong to upper castes whenever upper castes choose to make that point. This is not individual deviance. Human Rights Watch documented it systematically in their 1999 report Broken People. It has been documented in state after state since. The pattern is consistent enough that it is the only coherent explanation for the data.

The NCRB registers approximately 3,676 rapes of Dalit women per year — roughly 10 per day. Every rights organisation working at ground level estimates the actual figure is 10 to 20 times higher, because the barriers to reporting are extraordinary and deliberately structured. To file a rape FIR, a Dalit woman must: walk into a police station staffed by officers from dominant castes; report to an officer who may know the accused personally or be related to him; persuade him that her body's violation is worth paperwork; survive the standard response — "what were you doing there," "why were you alone," "are you sure this isn't a dispute about something else" — while in shock. If she insists, she then faces the near-certain reality that the accused's family will file a counter-case against her — false charges of theft, public nuisance, or prostitution — that will result in her own arrest. Most Dalit women do not report. The NCRB number is the floor, not the ceiling.

The conviction rate for rape under the SC/ST Atrocities Act is below 15% nationally and falls below 8% in UP and Rajasthan — the states with the highest reported rates. The Hathras case (2020) is the most visible illustration of the entire system working exactly as it is designed: the 19-year-old victim gang-raped by four upper-caste men, dying in hospital, her body cremated at 2am by UP Police without family consent to destroy forensic evidence, the CM's office denying the rape occurred, journalists arrested at district checkpoints, the accused eventually convicted — but not specifically for rape, because the body had been burned. This is not an aberration. This is how it works. Hathras was exceptional only in that the world was watching.

Beyond rape: the "double subordination" of Dalit women within their own families. In communities under sustained economic and social siege, Dalit men sometimes direct downward the violence and humiliation they cannot direct upward. Domestic violence rates in SC households, though not officially disaggregated from national data in most state surveys, are documented by women's organisations as high — and complicated by the reality that the same upper-caste-dominated police and legal system that refuses to prosecute Dalit rape cases is also the system a Dalit woman would need to access to report domestic violence. She faces the choice of reporting within a system that has never protected her, or not reporting. Most do not report. The political demand of the Dalit women's movement — for a state and a movement that takes them seriously simultaneously on both caste and gender — has not yet been met by either.

"When the entire system — police, courts, media, government — closes around you the moment you try to speak, silence is not cowardice. It is survival."
— NCDHR testimony, 2021
NCRB 2022

10 Rapes / Day — Official Undercount

3,676 registered rapes of SC women in 2022. Rights organisations estimate actual figure: 36,000–70,000 annually. The 10:1 to 20:1 gap exists because of police hostility, fear of counter-cases, and the near-certainty of no justice. UP, Rajasthan, MP account for over 60% of registered cases.

Conviction Rate

Below 15% — Below 8% in UP & Rajasthan

Cases that are registered, investigated, chargesheeted, tried in special courts, and still end in acquittal at rates that make reporting irrational from a purely strategic perspective. The system at every stage is designed and staffed by people whose communities produce the accused.

Maternal Mortality

2–3x the National Average

SC maternal mortality ratio in high-caste-concentration districts of UP, Bihar, and Rajasthan is estimated at 2–3x the national average. 59% of Dalit women are anaemic (NFHS-5). Institutional delivery rate for SC women: 78%, vs 90% for upper-caste women. The 12-point gap translates to preventable infant and maternal deaths every day.

HRW 1999 — "Broken People"

The Document That Named the Pattern

Human Rights Watch's landmark report documented rape as systematic political terror — not individual crime. Pattern documented across AP, Tamil Nadu, Bihar, UP: rape used as punishment when Dalit men demand wages, refuse humiliating labour, file police complaints, or contest elections. Changed how UN treaty bodies categorised Indian caste violence.

The devadasi system is the institutionalisation of child sexual abuse through religious ceremony. It functions through a precise choreography: a girl — almost universally Dalit, from the poorest households, typically between ages 6 and 14 — is brought to a temple. A ceremony is conducted that resembles a wedding. She is dressed as a bride. She is "married" to the goddess. From that moment she belongs to the temple and to the upper-caste men who pay the temple trustees for access to her. She cannot marry a human man. She cannot refuse patrons. She cannot leave. When she is no longer young enough to attract patrons — usually by her mid-twenties — she survives by begging near the same temple. Her children, fathered by multiple patrons, are registered without a father's name. They inherit her status. The cycle begins again.

The system operates under different names across states. Jogini in Telangana and Andhra Pradesh — the National Commission for Women documented 80,000+ Joginis in Telangana alone as of 2018. Basavi in Karnataka. Matangi in Maharashtra. Thevardiyar and Devaradiyar in Tamil Nadu. Murali in Goa. Different deities. Different ceremonies. The same girls. The same outcome. The National Commission for Women's aggregate estimate across all states: 450,000+ devadasis, 93% from SC/ST communities.

The system was banned in Karnataka in 1982, in Maharashtra and Andhra Pradesh in 1988, and nationally under the Immoral Traffic Prevention Act. It operates openly in 2024. The Yellamma temple complex at Saundatti, on the Maharashtra-Karnataka border, is the epicentre — an estimated 250,000 devadasis in the surrounding districts. The annual Yellamma fair draws hundreds of thousands of pilgrims. Rights organisations document new dedications of girls at each fair. The temple is administered by a government-recognised trust. Government officials attend the fair. The prohibition is on paper. The dedication ceremonies are in person.

The health consequences are documented in peer-reviewed medical literature going back to the 1990s. HIV prevalence in some Yellamma-belt devadasi communities: 30–40%, compared to India's national HIV rate of under 0.3%. The devadasi system is a vector for HIV that extends into the broader community through the men who pay for access — men who then return to wives and families. Government response: condom distribution campaigns targeting devadasi women. Not the abolition of the system that creates their exposure. Not prosecution of the trustees and patrons who profit from it. Condom distribution. To the victims. Who did not choose to be there.

Why does this system target Dalit girls specifically? Because their families are poor enough that the "honour" of goddess dedication — combined with the small payment to the family at the ceremony — can overcome resistance. Because their communities have the least political power to refuse pressure from temple trustees and landlords who want the system maintained. Because Brahminical theology designated their bodies as ritually impure — and upper-caste men's use of those bodies therefore carries no ritual consequence for the men. The girl is polluted in the theology. The man who uses her remains pure. The theology that structured her social death is also the theology that makes her abuse consequence-free for the abuser.

"I was nine years old. I did not know what married meant. By eleven, I knew. There is no word in any language for what it means to be a child and to know what I knew."
— Devadasi woman, Yellamma region, testimony to NHRC (anonymised)
Scale

450,000 Devadasis — 93% SC/ST

NCW national estimate. 250,000 near Maharashtra-Karnataka border alone. 80,000+ Joginis in Telangana. Karnataka government's own survey found 22,491 in just 6 districts. Banned since 1982–1988 depending on state. Continues in 2024 at temples administered by government-recognised trusts.

HIV Crisis

30–40% Prevalence — vs 0.3% National

Multiple peer-reviewed studies from Karnataka and Maharashtra since the 1990s. HIV transmission extends through male patrons into the general community. Government response: condom campaigns targeting victims. Zero prosecutions of trustees or patrons. The epidemic is documented. The cause is documented. The cause is not addressed.

The Legal Framework

Banned Three Times — Continues Openly

Karnataka 1982. Maharashtra 1988. AP 1988. Immoral Traffic Prevention Act nationally. Three decades of prohibition. Multiple Supreme Court observations calling it "national shame." The Karnataka High Court issued specific enforcement directions in 2019. Temple trustees have not been prosecuted. District officials who attend the Yellamma fair have not been prosecuted. Dedication ceremonies have not been stopped. The law is real. The enforcement is theatre.

Rehabilitation

Budgets Unspent. Lives Unhelped.

Government rehabilitation schemes for devadasi women exist in Karnataka, Telangana, and Maharashtra with official budget allocations. Rights organisations document year after year that the majority of the budget is unspent, the criteria for receiving support are inaccessible to women without documentation, and the amounts provided — where they do reach women — are insufficient to replace the income the system provided. Women are left with neither the system nor functional alternatives.

Witch-hunting — accusing a woman of being a dayan (witch) and killing her — is one of the most under-reported forms of violence against Dalit and Adivasi women in India. It is overwhelmingly concentrated in Jharkhand, Bihar, Odisha, Chhattisgarh, Assam, and Rajasthan. The NCRB documented 2,500+ women killed as witches between 2000 and 2016. Rights activists who work in the field estimate the actual figure at 10,000+ in the same period — because deaths are registered as murder without the "witch" motivation, as accidents, as suicides, or simply not registered at all in communities where police presence is minimal and landlord power is total.

What witch-hunting actually is, in the specific communities where it occurs: it is a mechanism for seizing land, silencing women who refuse sexual advances, punishing women who seek inheritance rights, removing widows who hold property, and destroying women whose visible competence or social connections threaten male authority in the community. The accusation of witchcraft is the trigger. The material interest beneath the accusation is the cause. A woman is identified as a witch — typically by a ojha (spirit healer, almost always male) who has been paid or pressured by the accuser — and the community's fear of supernatural harm is weaponised to do the accuser's bidding. Once accused, the woman has no defence. Her denial is proof. Her calm is proof. Her distress is proof. Everything is proof once the community has decided.

The specific violence is documented in NHRC testimonies and women's organisation case files: women stripped naked and paraded through the village; forced to eat human excreta; beaten by groups including women who participate under social coercion; hair shaved publicly; branded; in many cases killed — hacked with agricultural implements, burned alive, or killed by methods specifically designed to demonstrate that the death is communal punishment rather than individual murder, making prosecution impossible because there is no single killer to identify. The community is the weapon. That is also why accountability is almost structurally impossible — prosecuting a "witch-hunting" requires prosecuting a crowd, and the police and judiciary in these areas are drawn from the same communities and share the same beliefs.

Dalit and Adivasi women are targeted disproportionately because they have the least social protection — no dominant-caste family network, no political connections, no resources to hire a lawyer or pay police — and because their marginality makes them useful targets. Widows are the most vulnerable: women who hold property after their husbands' deaths but have no male family member to protect that claim are natural targets for witch accusations designed to remove them from the land. Jharkhand alone is estimated by activist organisations at 500+ cases annually. Jharkhand has an anti-witch-hunting law — passed in 2023 after decades of advocacy. The enforcement in the first year: minimal. The ojhas continue operating. The killings continue.

"She owned land. Her husband died. They called her a witch the following month. This is how land transfer works in our village when there is no man to protect a woman."
— Jharkhand women's rights activist, documented testimony 2021
NCRB 2000–2016

2,500 Official Deaths — 10,000+ Actual

NCRB records witch-hunting deaths under "murder" — the motivation is not separately coded. Activist organisations in Jharkhand document 500+ cases annually in that state alone. The 4:1 gap between official and estimated figures is consistent with every other category of violence against Dalit women: official data is what police choose to record, not what happens.

Geography

Jharkhand, Bihar, Odisha, Chhattisgarh, Assam

Five states account for the majority of documented cases. All five have: large Adivasi and Dalit populations; weak state presence in remote forest/hill areas; strong ojha (spirit healer) traditions that provide institutional infrastructure for witch accusations; and land scarcity pressures from encroachment by mining, plantation, and agricultural interests that have steadily reduced Adivasi land holdings across four decades.

The Real Cause

Land. Inheritance. Sexual Refusal.

Systematic analysis of documented cases by Birsa Agricultural University, Jharkhand (2019): over 70% of witch-hunting cases involve either: (a) widows or women with land inheritance claims; (b) women who refused sexual advances from the accuser or a male relative of the accuser; or (c) disputes over property between families in which the "witch" accusation is a cheaper and more effective tool than litigation. The supernatural is a weapon. The motive is material.

Legal Status

Most States: No Specific Law

Bihar 1999, Jharkhand 2001 (strengthened 2023), Chhattisgarh 2005, Rajasthan 2015, Odisha 2013 — each has some anti-witch-hunting legislation. No central law exists. Enforcement in all states: near-zero. Ojhas continue practising openly. The Jharkhand 2023 amendment increased penalties. It has not yet produced a single conviction in its first year of operation.

Between the 1970s and the 1990s, India's government conducted mass sterilisation campaigns under population control policy — and Dalit and Adivasi women were the primary targets. This is not speculation. It is documented in government records, court proceedings, and academic research. The Emergency period (1975–1977) under Indira Gandhi is the most documented phase: Sanjay Gandhi's informal population control programme set sterilisation quotas for states and districts. Local officials who met their quotas received rewards. Those who did not faced consequences. The quota was met by targeting the communities with the least political power to resist — which meant, systematically and with intent, Dalit and Adivasi women.

The specific documented mechanisms: women attending government hospitals for unrelated treatment were sterilised without consent while unconscious, with their post-operative records noting "voluntary sterilisation." Government health workers in SC-majority areas were given sterilisation targets for their catchment populations, creating direct personal incentive to pressure women. Women attending maternal health camps were told sterilisation was a condition of receiving the care they came for. In some districts of Rajasthan and Gujarat in the 1970s–80s, sterilisation was a de facto condition of access to Below Poverty Line rations for SC households. These practices are documented in state human rights commission reports, in the Peoples Union for Civil Liberties' investigations, and in parliamentary committee testimony — not in anti-government propaganda, but in official government-adjacent records.

The Emergency's mass sterilisations are relatively well-known — though their caste dimension is consistently underplayed in mainstream historical accounts, which focus on upper-caste urban men who were targeted and resisted, rather than on the Dalit and Adivasi women who were targeted and had no mechanism of resistance. Less well-known is that coercive sterilisation of Dalit women continued long after the Emergency ended. The 2014 Bilaspur (Chhattisgarh) mass sterilisation camp — where 83 women were sterilised in five hours in unsanitary conditions, 13 died, and investigation revealed all patients had been Dalit and Adivasi women from surrounding villages — is the most recent major documented case. A government doctor performed the sterilisations at an assembly-line pace that made individual consent physically impossible. He was initially arrested, then bail was granted, then proceedings stalled. As of 2024, no significant accountability has occurred.

The ideological underpinning of targeting Dalit and Adivasi women is rarely stated explicitly in policy documents, but it is the only coherent explanation for the consistent pattern: population control ideology combined with caste hierarchy produces a simple calculus — "too many" Dalits is a problem in ways that "too many" Brahmins is not. The reproductive bodies of Dalit women are treated as a policy variable to be managed by the state, not as sovereign persons whose bodily autonomy is constitutionally protected. This is the most complete expression of what it means to be simultaneously female and Dalit in a state that has never considered your body fully your own.

"They told me it was for my health. When I woke up they told me the operation was done. I did not know what operation. I did not know until three months later when my husband asked me why I had not conceived."
— Dalit woman, Rajasthan, testimony to PUCL investigation, 1978
The Emergency 1975–77

Sanjay Gandhi's Quota System — Who Paid the Price

Sanjay Gandhi's sterilisation programme set numeric targets for states and districts. The program's documented class and caste skew: urban upper-caste men who resisted were a political problem (and their resistance is what historians recorded). Dalit and Adivasi women who were sterilised without consent had no political infrastructure to document or resist. The history of the Emergency sterilisation programme is almost entirely written from the perspective of those who could write.

Bilaspur 2014

83 Women Sterilised in 5 Hours. 13 Dead.

November 2014. Government sterilisation camp in Bilaspur district, Chhattisgarh. One doctor sterilised 83 women in five hours in a school building, using rusty instruments, in conditions that made individual consent physically impossible. Thirteen women died. All 83 were Dalit or Adivasi. The doctor was paid on a per-operation basis. Initial arrest, then bail, then slow proceedings. No significant accountability as of 2024. The camp was part of a government public health programme with quota-based incentives for doctors.

The Pattern Across Decades

Consent as Optional for Dalit Bodies

Government records, PUCL investigations, state HRC reports, and parliamentary committee testimony document coercive and non-consensual sterilisation targeting SC/ST women from the 1970s through the 2010s — in Rajasthan, Gujarat, Chhattisgarh, MP, and Bihar. The pattern is not aberrant. It is the consistent output of a public health system that, at every level, treats Dalit women's bodily autonomy as subordinate to population policy targets.

International Law

Non-Consensual Sterilisation = Torture Under UN Convention

The UN Special Rapporteur on Torture has classified non-consensual sterilisation as a form of torture under the Convention Against Torture. The UN Committee on the Elimination of Discrimination Against Women (CEDAW) has specifically raised India's sterilisation camp programme in periodic reviews. India's official response: the programme is voluntary, oversight has been strengthened. The Bilaspur dead disagree.

Dalit women's intellectual tradition is one of the most extraordinary in the modern world — a tradition built by people who were denied education for centuries and who nevertheless produced, when education became partially accessible, some of the most searing, clear-eyed, and theoretically sophisticated accounts of oppression ever written. These are not victim narratives. They are analyses. They name the system. They describe its mechanisms with precision. They are also — this must be said — almost entirely absent from Indian university syllabi, from national literary canons, from the feminist reading lists of upper-caste women's studies departments, and from the Dalit reading lists of Dalit studies programmes dominated by male scholars. They have been doubly erased: by caste from the feminist tradition, and by gender from the Dalit tradition.

Savitribai Phule (1831–1897) is the origin point. India's first female teacher, trained by her husband Jyotirao, who walked through daily gauntlets of thrown dung and stones to teach untouchable girls, who brought a second sari to change into on arrival at school, who ran a refuge for rape survivors in 19th-century Maharashtra — and who, at the moment of her husband's death in 1890, lit his funeral pyre herself, refusing to allow Brahmin priests to take the ceremony. She then nursed plague victims — Dalit, Muslim, anyone — during the 1897 Pune epidemic, contracted plague herself, and died of it. She did not ask for recognition. She built what did not exist and walked into it every morning while people tried to stop her.

Baby Kamble (1929–2012) wrote Jenya Jivit (Marathi, 1982, published in English as The Prisons We Broke in 2008) — the first autobiography published by a Dalit woman in any Indian language. It is a document of extraordinary specificity: the internal world of a Mahar community in Maharashtra in the first half of the 20th century, the precise texture of untouchability in daily life, the sexual violence inflicted on Dalit women by upper-caste men as a matter of course, the relationship between caste and alcohol (upper-caste-run liquor as deliberate community destruction), and the transformation brought by Ambedkar's movement. "We had no rights," she wrote. "Not even to grieve our dead in public. Not even that." The autobiography was not widely reviewed in mainstream Marathi literary circles for two decades after publication.

Bama (born 1958, Tamil Nadu) — born Faustina Mary Fatima Rani into a Dalit Catholic family in Tamil Nadu — published Karukku in 1992, the first Tamil Dalit autobiography and the first Tamil Dalit woman's autobiography in the same gesture. It is structured as a winding, layered account of growing up Dalit Catholic in Tamil Nadu — experiencing caste discrimination from upper-caste Catholics (demonstrating definitively that caste does not disappear with conversion to Christianity), leaving a convent because the Church reproduced the same caste hierarchy it preached against, and finding in Ambedkar's thought and the Dalit movement a framework for understanding what she had always lived. Karukku was initially self-published after major publishers declined it. It is now taught in universities in the United States, Germany, and Japan — and is still not in the standard Tamil Nadu school curriculum.

Uma Chakravarti — not Dalit herself, but the scholar who provided the theoretical framework that Dalit feminist organising has built on — developed the concept of "Brahmanical patriarchy" in her 1993 paper of the same name. The argument: the caste system is reproduced biologically through endogamy (marriage only within caste), which means the reproductive bodies of upper-caste women must be controlled by upper-caste men to ensure "purity" of the caste line. Upper-caste women's oppression and Dalit women's oppression are therefore not separate systems — they are two functions of the same system of caste patriarchy, which controls upper-caste women's sexuality to maintain caste boundaries from the top, and exploits Dalit women's sexuality because their bodies have no caste value that requires protection. The framework transformed how Indian feminist theory understood the relationship between caste and gender.

Urmila Pawar (b. 1945, Maharashtra)

Aaydan — The Weave of My Life

Marathi Dalit woman's autobiography (2003, English 2008). Documents three generations of Mahar women — grandmother, mother, herself — in Maharashtra. Examines how Ambedkar's movement transformed women's self-understanding while simultaneously often expecting women to be support structures for male political activity rather than political actors themselves. One of the most honest accounts of the gender tensions within the Dalit liberation movement.

Sharmila Rege (1964–2013)

Sociology of Dalit Women's Knowledge

Pune sociologist who developed the framework of "Dalit feminist standpoint" — the argument that Dalit women, located at the sharpest intersection of caste, class, and gender, have an epistemological position that produces knowledge unavailable to those at any single axis of the intersection. Her collected essays and her work at Krantijyoti Savitribai Phule Women's Studies Centre built Dalit feminist sociology as a discipline. She died of cancer at 49. Her students continue her work.

Meena Kandasamy (b. 1984, Tamil Nadu)

Poet, Novelist, Activist

Tamil Dalit woman poet and novelist. Her poetry in English — Touch (2006) and Ms Militancy (2010) — is among the most formally ambitious and politically ferocious Dalit feminist writing in any language. Her novel When I Hit You (2017) — about domestic violence within a marriage — refuses to separate personal and political in ways that both feminist and Dalit movements find uncomfortable. She has faced sustained online harassment from both Hindutva networks and from Dalit political organisations that find her independence threatening.

Susie Tharu & K. Lalita (eds.)

Women Writing in India — The Recovery Project

Two-volume anthology (1991–1993) that recovered women's writing across Indian languages going back 600 BCE — including, critically, writing by women from low-caste and untouchable communities that had been systematically excluded from "Indian literature" as anthologised by upper-caste male scholars. The anthology changed what "Indian women's writing" is understood to include. It is still not comprehensively taught in Indian literature departments.

Dalit women have been fighting back since long before there was a movement to fight with. They fought with the only things available to them: their bodies, their refusal, their voice, and — when they could access it — their pen. What is remarkable is not merely the courage, which was immense. It is the precision of the analysis. Women who had no formal education, no institutional support, no political allies, and no media sympathetic to their cause looked at the system that was crushing them and described it with an exactness that scholars with all those advantages have spent decades catching up to.

Phoolan Devi (1963–2001) was born into the Mallah (boatman, low-caste) community in UP. She was child-married at 11, escaped, was gang-raped by Thakur men in Behmai village in 1979, survived, formed a bandit gang, and in February 1981 returned to Behmai with her gang and killed 20 Thakur men — specifically those involved in her rape. She surrendered to government in 1983 after negotiating terms. She was jailed for 11 years without trial. Released in 1994, she became a symbol of low-caste women's resistance, was elected to Parliament in 1996, re-elected in 1999. She was assassinated on July 25, 2001, shot outside her Delhi home by a Thakur man who said he was avenging the Behmai killings. She was 37 years old. Upper-caste India called her a bandit. Dalit and OBC women called her the only kind of justice they had ever seen.

Sampat Pal Devi (b. 1960) founded the Gulabi Gang — the Pink Gang — in Banda district, UP, in 2006. A group of women, many Dalit and OBC, who wore pink saris and carried bamboo sticks and intervened physically in domestic violence cases, child marriage cases, and corruption cases where police refused to act. When a dominant-caste landlord was beating a Dalit woman in her village, Sampat Pal went to the police. The police refused to act. She went back with 400 women in pink saris and besieged the police station until the landlord was arrested. The Gulabi Gang has thousands of members across UP. It operates where the state does not — which in Dalit areas is most of the time.

CK Janu (b. 1970, Kerala) — Adivasi activist from Wayanad — led the Adivasi Gothra Maha Sabha's land occupation campaigns demanding the return of forest land promised to Adivasi communities. In January 2001, she led thousands of Adivasi people to occupy the Muthanga forest reserve in Wayanad. Police fired. Two people died. She was arrested, tried, and acquitted. She ran for Parliament twice. She is the most prominent Adivasi woman political figure in Kerala, in a state that congratulates itself constantly on its progressive credentials while its Adivasi communities remain landless. Her existence is the contradiction that Kerala's self-congratulation cannot absorb.

Mayawati (b. 1956) — Dalit, Jatav caste, from Badalpur, UP — became Chief Minister of Uttar Pradesh four times, governing 200 million people, the most powerful Dalit political leader in world history by population governed. She was mentored by Kanshi Ram. She renamed districts, built Ambedkar parks, increased SC welfare budgets, and proved that Dalit political power at the state level was possible when Dalit organisations built their own political machinery rather than depending on upper-caste-led parties. She is not without controversy — her governing style has been called authoritarian, and allegations of corruption during her terms have been made. She is also the woman who, at 38, became Chief Minister of India's largest state by population, having come from a family with nothing, in a system designed to ensure she would remain with nothing. The upper-caste response to her success — the ferocity of the personal attacks, the sexualised abuse, the "statues scandal" coverage — tells you more about her opponents than about her.

"They gave us the right to vote. But when we vote for ourselves, they call it caste politics. When they vote for themselves, they call it merit."
— Mayawati, campaign speech, 2007 UP election
Sengeni — The Real Jai Bhim

17 Years for Her Husband's Justice

Sengeni is the Dalit tribal woman whose husband Rajakannu was arrested by Tamil Nadu police in 1995 on fabricated theft charges and tortured to death in custody. She fought for justice for 17 years. Advocate Chandru — later a Madras High Court judge — took her case pro bono. He won. Officers were convicted. Her story became the film Jai Bhim (2021). What the film does not show: that for every Sengeni who found an advocate Chandru willing to fight, there are 10,000 Dalit women whose husbands died in custody and whose cases were never filed.

National Federation of Dalit Women

Organised Since 1995 — Still Fighting for Both Recognition

Founded 1995. Represents Dalit women's organisations across 22 states. Primary demands: (1) accountability for crimes against Dalit women in the criminal justice system; (2) implementation of reservations for Dalit women within women's political reservation, not just Dalit reservation; (3) recognition that Dalit women's issues require Dalit women's leadership, not representation through male Dalit politicians or upper-caste women's organisations. All three demands remain substantially unmet.

AIDMAM

All India Dalit Mahila Adhikar Manch

National Dalit women's rights network. Documents crimes against Dalit women systematically — filling the gap between what the NCRB records and what actually happens. Their annual reports are among the most comprehensive accounting of Dalit women's experiences in India. They have produced detailed documentation of the Hathras case, the devadasi system, witch-hunting patterns, and forced sterilisation. The documentation exists. The accountability does not.

The Dalit Women's Movement's Core Demand

"We Are Not a Subset. We Are a Subject."

The fundamental political demand of Dalit women's organising since the 1990s: to be treated as political subjects in their own right — not as a subset of "Dalits" (who default to male in most political discourse) and not as a subset of "women" (who default to upper-caste in most feminist discourse). To have their specific experiences, their specific violence, their specific knowledge, and their specific leadership recognised by both movements that have for decades asked for their votes and their bodies without returning commensurate power.

→
🏫
Caste in Education — Where Each Generation Learns Its Place
From village primary schools to IITs. The system that converts birth into destiny.
Society14
Full SectionNEW
▼

The school is where the caste order explains itself to the next generation. Not through a lesson. Through the arrangement of bodies in the room — who sits on a bench, who sits on the floor; whose lunch is accepted, whose is refused; whose raised hand is called on, whose is ignored. Every one of these practices has been documented in government surveys, NHRC reports, and academic research in the 21st century. None of them appear in any official account of what Indian education does. They are invisible to the people who design policy and visible to every Dalit child who lives them. Ambedkar walked four miles to school and was not allowed to sit inside the classroom. The classroom has since been built. The arrangement inside it has not changed nearly as much.

"Ambedkar walked miles to a school that seated him outside the room. We got inside the room. They seated us at the back. Our children are still at the back. This is what 70 years of progress looks like."
— Dalit teacher, UP, interview with ASER field researcher, 2019

The practices documented below are not historical. They are documented in field surveys, NHRC inspection reports, and journalistic investigations conducted between 2010 and 2023. They are happening now, in government schools that are legally required under the Right to Education Act 2009 to provide equal treatment. The law exists. The enforcement does not. And the teachers, administrators, parents, and district officials who sustain these practices are not prosecuted — because they are operating within a social consensus in their communities that the law has not reached.

Seating segregation. Dalit children forced to sit separately from upper-caste children — on the floor while others sit on benches, in the back rows, or in a physically separate area of the classroom — has been documented in government primary schools in Rajasthan, UP, Bihar, MP, Gujarat, Tamil Nadu, and Andhra Pradesh in surveys conducted since 2010. In some Rajasthan schools, separate seating arrangements have persisted for decades. When mid-level education officials are asked about it, the standard response is that it reflects "community preference" or "family wishes." It is not community preference. It is the dominant-caste community's preference, enforced through the same social mechanisms — threat of violence, economic boycott, social ostracism — that enforce untouchability outside the school gate.

Mid-day meal segregation. The mid-day meal scheme — free cooked meals in government schools — was expanded nationally in 2001 specifically to incentivise SC/ST and OBC attendance. Within a year of expansion, reports of segregated eating began accumulating. Navsarjan Trust surveys (Gujarat, 2010–2016): Dalit children eating separately in over 60% of sampled schools. Multiple state surveys document: Dalit children served last, after upper-caste children have eaten; Dalit children eating outside the school building while upper-caste children eat inside; Dalit children prohibited from using the same plates and cups as upper-caste children; upper-caste children refusing to eat food cooked by Dalit cooks appointed under the scheme. In 2020, a teacher in Rajasthan was caught on video making Dalit children clean the school premises while upper-caste children sat inside. The video circulated briefly. No prosecution followed.

Water — the primary vector. In caste geography, water is the sharpest marker. Dalit children denied access to common water taps — required to use separate vessels, prohibited from touching the shared water pot, or provided no water access at all — has been documented in government school surveys in six states as recently as 2022 by the RTE Forum. In 2016, a Dalit boy in Rajasthan was beaten by his teacher for touching a water vessel used by upper-caste children. He was eight years old. He died of his injuries. The teacher was arrested. He was out on bail within weeks. The case has not concluded as of 2024.

Teacher negligence as caste violence. Teachers refusing to evaluate Dalit students' work — marking their papers absent without grading them, refusing to correct answers on the blackboard when a Dalit student answers, calling Dalit students by caste epithets rather than names, assigning Dalit children to cleaning duties while upper-caste children study — has been documented in MP, Gujarat, and Tamil Nadu. In several documented cases in Bihar and UP, the Dalit-majority hamlet school simply has no teacher present on most days: the government teacher assigned to the post uses political connections to avoid posting and receives their salary regardless. ASER surveys consistently show teacher absence rates in SC-majority schools are 2–3x the rate in upper-caste-majority schools. This is not coincidence. It is the state's caste hierarchy expressing itself through the management of its own institutions.

"My son came home and said the teacher told him Dalits are dirty and should not touch the food of others. My son was seven. He already knew what he was, because the teacher told him."
— Father of SC child, Bihar, NHRC testimony 2018
Segregation Survey

11 States — Meal Segregation Documented Post-2010

Surveys by Navsarjan Trust (Gujarat), ASER, NHRC inspection reports, and NGO ground teams document mid-day meal segregation in Rajasthan, UP, Bihar, MP, Gujarat, Tamil Nadu, AP, Karnataka, Jharkhand, Odisha, and Haryana. The RTE Act prohibits discrimination in schools. The National Commission for Protection of Child Rights has issued repeated advisories. The practice continues.

2016 Rajasthan

8-Year-Old Beaten for Touching Water Pot — Died

Rajasthan, April 2016. A Dalit child, aged 8, beaten by his upper-caste school teacher for touching the clay water pot from which upper-caste children drank. He died from his injuries. Teacher arrested. Bail granted within weeks. Case pending eight years later. No conviction. The water pot is still separate in the same school, according to local reporting in 2023.

Teacher Absence

2–3x Higher Absence in SC-Majority Schools

ASER (Annual Status of Education Report) data consistently shows teacher absence rates in schools located in SC-majority hamlets are roughly 2–3x the rate in general-category schools. Government teachers use political connections to remain in urban postings or to avoid remote postings entirely. The constitutional right to education is meaningless in a school where the teacher assigned to the post is physically absent 40–60% of the time.

Legal Framework

RTE 2009 — The Law That Changed Nothing Enough

The Right to Education Act 2009 mandated free, compulsory elementary education with non-discrimination provisions. It produced infrastructure — more school buildings, more enrolled children. It did not produce the enforcement mechanism needed to change how caste operates inside school buildings. There is no criminal provision for caste discrimination in schools. The NCPCR can issue advisories. It cannot prosecute. The gap between the law and its enforcement is measured in the daily humiliation of Dalit children across 600,000 villages.

The dropout data tells a precise story about where the educational system breaks the Dalit child's trajectory. It does not break it uniformly. It breaks it at specific transition points — from primary to upper primary (Class 5 to 6), from upper primary to secondary (Class 8 to 9), and from secondary to higher secondary (Class 10 to 11) — and it breaks Dalit children's trajectories at each point at measurably higher rates than children from upper-caste households. Each transition point is where the cost of continuing school rises (uniforms, textbooks, examination fees, transport to schools that may be farther away), the opportunity cost rises (a 14-year-old Dalit boy can earn agricultural wages; an 8-year-old cannot), and the social pressure to conform to hereditary occupation increases. Every transition point is a test of whether the family's commitment to education can survive the economic and social forces pushing against it. For Dalit families — poorer, in more economically precarious agricultural relationships, with less political power to resist landlord pressure — the test is harder at every stage.

The numbers. SC secondary-level dropout rate (Class 9–10): 17.5% nationally, vs 10.2% for general category (UDISE+ 2022–23). In Bihar and UP specifically, SC primary-level dropout exceeds 30%. SC gross enrolment ratio at higher education level: approximately 23% (2021–22), compared to 28% nationally and over 35% for upper-caste households. These gaps have narrowed since 1950 — but have not closed, and in some states the gap in higher education is wider now than it was in 2010, as private higher education expands in ways that SC reservation provisions do not reach.

Why girls drop out. SC girl dropout rates are consistently higher than SC boy dropout rates. The primary documented causes: absence of toilets in schools (or toilets that are not maintained) makes attending school after puberty unsafe and socially impossible for girls; distance to secondary school (which is often in a different, dominant-caste village rather than the Dalit hamlet) exposes girls to risk during transit; social norms in many SC communities around girls' education being less urgent than boys' — norms that have been narrowing but not eliminated; and the specific vulnerability of Dalit girls in upper-caste-dominated school environments, where they face both caste discrimination and sexual harassment from male students and teachers. The toilet problem is documented in ASER every year. Every government since 2009 has announced toilet construction drives. As of 2023, functional, maintained girls' toilets remain absent in a significant percentage of government schools in UP, Bihar, Rajasthan, and MP.

Economic pressure as educational exclusion. Bonded and semi-bonded agricultural relationships — in which Dalit families owe debts to landlords that are repaid in labour — directly translate into children being pulled out of school to work. ASER surveys document this in UP, Bihar, Rajasthan, and MP: children from families in debt-bonded agricultural relationships attend school significantly less regularly than children from families not in such relationships. The landlord who holds the debt does not need to formally say "take your child out of school." He can simply make it clear — through reduced rations, through accelerated debt calls, through other forms of pressure — that a family whose children are in school rather than in the field is a family whose debt obligations are not being taken seriously. The educational exclusion is economic coercion at one remove.

UDISE+ 2022–23

SC Secondary Dropout: 17.5% vs 10.2% General

At the Class 9–10 level, SC students drop out at 70% higher rates than general category students. The gap is largest in Bihar (SC dropout ~26%, general ~12%), UP (~22% vs ~11%), and Rajasthan (~20% vs ~10%). These are not legacy numbers from a generation ago. They are the current state of India's constitutional guarantee of equal education.

Higher Education

SC Gross Enrolment: 23% — Gap Widening at Top

SC gross enrolment ratio in higher education: ~23% (2021–22). National average: ~28%. Upper-caste household estimated: 35%+. Private higher education — which accounts for the majority of expansion in Indian higher education since 2000 — is almost entirely outside the reservation framework. The more education privatises, the smaller the protected space in which SC students have guaranteed access.

Toilets

Girls Drop Out Because Schools Are Not Safe for Girls

ASER surveys document year after year that absence of functional girls' toilets is the single most consistently cited reason for SC girl dropout at the upper primary and secondary level in UP, Bihar, Rajasthan, and MP. Government toilet construction drives have been announced under every government since 2009. Maintenance of constructed toilets remains inadequate. The result: built but non-functional. Announced but not counted by the girls who stopped coming to school.

Private Education Expansion

The New Exclusion — Legal, Invisible, Growing

India's private school sector has expanded dramatically since 2000. Private schools — which can charge fees SC families cannot afford, which have no reservation requirements in admissions, and which are informally sorted by caste and class in ways that produce upper-caste-majority environments — now educate over 30% of Indian children. They will educate more. As the private system grows, the reservation framework covers a shrinking share of education. The next generation's educational exclusion will be economic and private rather than legal and public — which makes it harder to fight, not easier.

The Indian Institutes of Technology — the most prestigious engineering institutions in India, the gateway to the global tech industry, the symbol of Indian intellectual excellence in every international context — are places where Dalit students are systematically told they do not belong. Not once, not by accident, not by a few bad actors. Systematically. As a matter of documented institutional culture. IIT Bombay's own internal Caste Discrimination Awareness Committee report (2023) — not an activist document, an internal document obtained through RTI — found that Dalit students entering through SC reservation face consistent hostility from faculty and from upper-caste peers, are routinely told that they are "taking someone else's seat," have lower rates of mentorship access, and fail examinations at higher rates specifically because of inequalities in pre-admission coaching access — not because of inherent intellectual differences. The report was not released publicly. An RTI had to be filed to obtain it.

The hostile environment operates through mechanisms that are individually deniable and collectively crushing. A professor who grades Dalit students' work more harshly — deniable as subjective assessment. A study group that forms organically along caste lines, from which Dalit students are excluded — deniable as personal preference. An upper-caste peer who tells a Dalit student in a corridor "you only got in through reservation, you shouldn't be here" — deniable as one person's bad behaviour. A department head who never considers a Dalit student for a research assistant position — deniable as merit-based selection. Individually, each is deniable. Together, they form a total environment in which a Dalit student at IIT is working against structural headwinds at every point of their academic career. The IIT Bombay report found this environment exists. It did not find it exceptional. Multiple other institutions have not investigated at all.

Faculty representation. SC/ST representation among IIT faculty: below 5% across all IITs combined, against a mandated 22.5% (15% SC + 7.5% ST) under reservation provisions. IIT administrations explain this consistently as "no suitable candidates found" — meaning that SC/ST candidates who apply for faculty positions are evaluated and found not to meet the bar. This explanation is used year after year, across decade after decade, without the institutions asking the prior question: why, after 70 years of reservation in higher education, is the pipeline of SC/ST PhD graduates not producing faculty candidates? The answer is the one these institutions are least equipped to examine: the pipeline is thin because the graduate experience at these very institutions is designed — not consciously, but structurally — to thin it.

The "merit" argument — what it actually means. The argument that reservation places "unqualified" students in IITs is empirically testable. It has been tested. Studies of IIT alumni outcomes — patent filings, research publications, entrepreneurship rates, earnings — show no significant performance difference between students who entered through the general category and students who entered through reservation, when tracked over careers rather than measured by a single entrance examination. What the entrance examination measures is not potential — it measures access to expensive coaching (the top IIT coaching centres in Kota, Hyderabad, and Patna charge fees equivalent to a Dalit family's annual income for two years of preparation). The "merit" that the entrance examination selects for is, in large part, the merit of having parents who could afford the coaching. The reservation system partially corrects for this. The "meritocracy" argument defends a system in which inherited wealth is the primary determinant of exam performance and calls it natural talent.

IIT Bombay Internal Report 2023

Hostile Environment — Documented, Not Released

The Caste Discrimination Awareness Committee report found: SC students face consistent peer hostility; "you don't belong here" messaging from faculty and peers; lower mentorship access; higher examination failure rates traceable to pre-admission coaching inequality rather than inherent ability. The institution did not release the report. A journalist filed an RTI. The report became public. No institutional reform followed. Darshan Solanki died the same year.

Faculty Representation

SC/ST Faculty at IITs: Below 5% — Mandated 22.5%

Mandatory representation: 15% SC, 7.5% ST = 22.5%. Actual combined SC/ST faculty across IITs: approximately 3–5% per parliamentary committee data (2022). Consistent explanation given: "no suitable SC/ST candidates found." Consistent follow-up question never asked by IIT administrations: why is the pipeline this thin after 70 years? The answer is in the experience of the students currently at these institutions.

The Coaching Economy

Two Years of Kota Coaching = Dalit Family's Annual Income

Top IIT-JEE coaching centres in Kota (Rajasthan) charge ₹2–4 lakh per year for two-year programmes — comparable to or exceeding the annual household income of the median SC family. The entrance examination selects heavily for students who had this preparation. The reservation system attempts to correct for this. The "merit vs reservation" debate consistently ignores that the examination itself is measuring something other than raw intellectual potential.

Alumni Performance Studies

Reservation vs General: No Significant Outcome Difference

Studies examining IIT alumni career outcomes (patents, publications, entrepreneurship, earnings) find no statistically significant performance difference between general-category and reservation-category students over career timelines. What differs is the entrance score — which measures coaching access. What does not differ is what the institutions exist to produce. The "merit" argument for which reservation students allegedly displace better performers is not supported by post-graduation data.

Since 2007, at least 24 Dalit students have died by suicide at Indian universities — in suicide notes, in final WhatsApp messages, in institutional complaints filed in the weeks before their deaths — citing caste discrimination as the cause. The actual number, accounting for cases where the suicide note was confiscated by police, where deaths were classified as accidents, where families were pressured not to file discrimination complaints, and where the institution's response was to suppress rather than investigate, is certainly higher. These are the documented cases. Each one follows a pattern so consistent it has become a template: a promising Dalit student; an institution that tells them they do not belong; a bureaucratic process that exhausts them without resolution; a final letter that names the system precisely; and an institutional response, after the death, that focuses on procedural compliance rather than structural accountability.

Rohith Vemula (1989–2016) was a PhD student in the University of Hyderabad's Department of Science and Technology Studies. He was a brilliant writer — his doctoral work and his public essays on science, caste, and identity were of a quality that his supervisors acknowledged. He was a member of the Ambedkar Students Association. The ABVP — the student organisation affiliated to the RSS/BJP — filed a complaint against him and four other Dalit students, accusing them of assault. The Union Minister for Human Resource Development wrote to the university. The Vice-Chancellor responded by suspending five Dalit students and removing them from their hostel rooms — denying them campus residence while leaving them enrolled, which had the specific effect of removing their living stipend while leaving them unable to leave. They lived in a tent outside the administration building for weeks. On January 17, 2016, Rohith Vemula went into that tent and hanged himself. His final letter — which circulated globally within hours — contained the line: "My birth is my fatal accident." He was 26. The enquiry commission appointed after his death concluded there was no caste angle. Five enquiry commission members were later found to have connections to ABVP and BJP organisations.

Darshan Solanki (2004–2023) was a first-year BTech student at IIT Bombay, having entered through SC reservation. He was 18 years old. He jumped from the seventh floor of a hostel building on February 12, 2023 — three weeks after his IIT Bombay internal committee report documented hostile caste environments. His classmates told investigators he had been repeatedly told by peers that he "didn't deserve to be there." IIT Bombay's initial response: a statement expressing condolence and listing available mental health resources. The institution's mental health resources were available. The problem was not a lack of counsellors. The problem was that the student was told daily by the social environment around him that he was an interloper whose presence was illegitimate. Counselling cannot fix that. The institution's structure would need to change. It has not changed.

Payal Tadvi (1991–2019) was a Muslim-Adivasi (Bhil community, OBC) doctor completing her MD at TN Topiwala National Medical College, Mumbai. Three upper-caste senior doctors — her supervisors — subjected her to sustained casteist and communal abuse over months: denied her the clinical procedures required to complete her degree, called her by caste and community slurs in front of patients, threatened her, and obstructed her academic progress. She had filed internal complaints. Nothing was done. She died by suicide in her hostel room. She left a WhatsApp voice message to her mother saying she could not take any more. Her phone was allegedly wiped before police arrived. The three doctors were arrested. One was eventually convicted of abetment of suicide. The other two were acquitted. Payal's mother Abeda Tadvi has continued fighting the case — and continues to document how the medical establishment has closed ranks to protect those responsible.

"My birth is my fatal accident. I never had a self of my own. A glorious thing to be made out of star dust."
— Rohith Vemula, final letter, January 17, 2016
Documented Cases Since 2007

24+ Dalit Students — All Named the System

Rohith Vemula (Hyderabad, 2016). Darshan Solanki (IIT Bombay, 2023). Aniket Ambekar (IIT Bombay, 2023). Faizan Ahmed (HCU, 2022). Muthu Krishnan (JNU, 2017). Senthil Kumar (AIIMS Delhi, 2014). Each left documentation — notes, WhatsApp messages, prior complaints — citing caste. Each institution's post-death response: grief counselling announcement, procedural review, no structural change.

Rohith Vemula Act

Demanded 2016. Not Passed as of 2024.

Following Rohith Vemula's death, student and civil society organisations demanded the "Rohith Act" — legislation specifically making caste discrimination in educational institutions a criminal offence with mandatory prosecution provisions. Eight years later, no such legislation has been passed. The UGC has guidelines. Guidelines are not law. The absence of a criminal provision means every institution can respond to documented discrimination with an enquiry committee rather than a prosecution. Enquiry committees protect institutions. They do not protect students.

Institutional Response Pattern

Condolences → Committee → Closure → Repeat

Documented across every case: death occurs; institution expresses condolences; committee appointed; committee either finds no caste angle or finds minor procedural failures; no senior official is held accountable; mental health resources are announced; case fades from coverage; next death occurs 6–18 months later at a different institution. The pattern is mechanical. It is not accidental. It is the institutional management of accountability without accountability.

Payal Tadvi (1991–2019)

The Medical Establishment Protects Its Own

Muslim-Adivasi (Bhil) MD student, TN Topiwala National Medical College, Mumbai. Three senior doctors: sustained casteist-communal abuse, denial of required clinical procedures, slurs in front of patients. Filed complaints. Nothing done. Died by suicide May 22, 2019. Phone allegedly wiped before police arrived. One conviction, two acquittals. Mother Abeda Tadvi continues fighting. The Indian Medical Association's initial response was to defend the accused doctors, not to express solidarity with the dead student's family.

Educational reservation — the constitutional provision guaranteeing 15% of seats in government-funded higher education institutions for SC students and 7.5% for ST students — was designed by Ambedkar as a transitional mechanism: a correction for centuries of deliberately imposed educational exclusion, intended to create the critical mass of educated Dalit professionals who would then transform their communities from within. It was not designed to be transitional in the sense of temporary. It was designed to persist until the conditions that made it necessary — the exclusion of Dalit students from educational access — no longer existed. Those conditions still exist. The provision has been in place for 75 years. This section examines what 75 years of educational reservation has and has not produced, and why the gap between what it promised and what it delivered is as large as it is.

What reservation produced. The growth of a Dalit professional middle class that did not exist in 1950. The first generation of Dalit doctors, engineers, lawyers, civil servants, academics, and politicians — people whose existence is itself a refutation of the ideology that Dalit communities are constitutionally incapable of these roles. The transformation of political possibility: Dalit communities with educated members have political advocates in ways that pre-reservation Dalit communities did not. The Dalit intellectual tradition — Ambedkar's own intellectual formation was made possible by Baroda State scholarship, the earliest form of educational reservation. Every Dalit academic, every Dalit journalist, every Dalit politician who reads and writes and argues today is, in some chain of causation, a product of the reservation provision's expansion of educational access. This is what is at stake when reservation is attacked. Not the "unfairness" to individual upper-caste applicants who narrowly missed a seat. The entire existence of Dalit public life.

What reservation has not produced — and why. Reservation applies to government-funded institutions. Approximately 30% of Indian higher education is now private, with no reservation requirement. Central institutions — IITs, IIMs, AIIMS, central universities — do have reservation requirements, but fill SC seats at below-mandated levels in PhD and faculty positions. The reservation mechanism does not address the conditions that make Dalit students less competitive at the entrance stage — the pre-admission educational environment, the coaching access gap, the primary and secondary school quality gap. It is a mechanism that addresses the door of the institution without addressing the road that leads to the door. Most Dalit students do not reach the reservation door because they do not survive the road to it.

The private sector gap. The 93rd Constitutional Amendment (2005) allowed private unaided colleges to maintain reservations as per their own policies — which in practice means most private colleges have no SC reservation. The Supreme Court upheld this in the P.A. Inamdar case (2005), holding that private unaided institutions have autonomy in admissions. The result: as India's higher education system privatises, the guaranteed access space shrinks. This dynamic has been made worse by government policy choices that have encouraged private expansion without requiring private participation in affirmative action. The Dalit student who survives primary school, survives secondary school, survives the coaching economy — then faces a higher education landscape in which the expanding private sector is not required to admit them.

What Reservation Created

A Dalit Professional Class That Did Not Exist in 1950

The most powerful argument for reservation is the existence of the people it produced. Dalit doctors. Dalit engineers. Dalit judges — few, but some. Dalit journalists, academics, politicians. A Dalit professional middle class that has transformed political possibility and created the intellectual infrastructure of Dalit rights organising. Before 1950, educated Dalit individuals existed — Ambedkar being the supreme example — but required extraordinary individual heroism to achieve what reservation makes structurally possible. That is what is being defended when reservation is defended.

IIM SC Faculty Seats

Unfilled Decade After Decade — "No Suitable Candidates"
