India's premier management institutions report SC/ST faculty representation well below mandated levels every year. The explanation given annually: "no suitable SC/ST candidates found" at the faculty recruitment stage. The prior question never asked: why, after 70 years of educational reservation producing SC/ST graduates, is the PhD pipeline not producing faculty candidates at the rate that mandated representation requires? The answer requires examining the graduate experience — which these institutions are institutionally motivated not to examine.

Private Education Gap

30%+ of Higher Ed is Private — Zero Reservation Required

PA Inamdar Supreme Court ruling (2005): private unaided institutions have admissions autonomy. As a result: the expanding private higher education sector — which now accounts for over 60% of enrolment growth since 2000 — has no mandatory SC reservation. Government policy has actively encouraged private expansion. The share of higher education seats under the reservation framework is shrinking as a proportion of total seats. This dynamic is structurally equivalent to the retreat of affirmative action — and is happening without formal legislative change.

Rohith Act — Still Pending

No Criminal Law on Caste Discrimination in Education

Eight years after Rohith Vemula, there is no specific criminal provision for caste discrimination in educational institutions. UGC guidelines exist. They are advisory. Institutions respond to discrimination complaints with internal committees whose findings are not publicly disclosed and whose members are appointed by the same administration being investigated. The absence of a criminal law means the incentive structure for institutions is: investigation is costly, prosecution is impossible, so minimize investigation. Students die. Committees convene. No one is prosecuted. The next student dies.

The history of Dalit education is not primarily the history of institutions that reluctantly admitted Dalit students. It is the history of people — mostly Dalit women and men, some upper-caste allies — who built educational access from nothing, often literally constructing schools in the face of violent opposition, and who understood clearly that controlling who could be educated was the single most effective tool for maintaining caste hierarchy. Take away the education and you take away the ability to read the law, to file a complaint, to write about what is happening to you, to become the doctor or lawyer or official who can refuse the system's terms. The fight for Dalit education is the fight for Dalit existence as full citizens.

Savitribai and Jyotirao Phule. In 1848, Jyotirao Phule — Shudra-caste, Pune — opened the first school for untouchable girls in India, in the Bhidewada locality of Pune. His wife Savitribai Phule became the teacher. The school was not unopposed: Brahmin community leaders pressured Jyotirao's father, who asked them to leave the family home. They did. The school continued. Savitribai walked through daily crowds who threw dung and stones and verbal abuse, changing into a second sari she carried once she arrived. By 1851, the Phules had opened three schools for girls including Dalit and Shudra students. By 1852, they had opened a school specifically for Mahars (the untouchable caste), which the British colonial administration formally recognised. In 1852, Jyotirao Phule received a public honour from the British administration for his educational work. The Brahmin press called him a traitor and an agent of Christian missionaries. He was neither. He was a man who looked at a system that deliberately kept human beings ignorant and decided to build schools.

Ambedkar's own educational battle is inseparable from his politics. He was admitted to Elphinstone High School in Bombay in 1900 — one of the first untouchable students in the school's history — only because a Brahmin teacher, Krishnaji Arjun Keluskar, personally advocated for him. At school, he sat separately on a gunny sack; teachers would not touch his work. At Sydenham College, Bombay, professors refused to share a water jug with him. The Baroda State scholarship that funded his Columbia education came from Gaekwad of Baroda, an enlightened Maratha king — without it, the intellectual career that produced India's constitution would not have existed. When Ambedkar returned from Columbia with a PhD and took up his post in Baroda, he could not find a single landlord willing to rent him a room because he was Dalit. He slept in a Parsi inn under a false name. Every credential he carried was accompanied by a housing crisis. Merit did not protect him. Only the political transformation he spent his life building could do that.

Ambedkar Students Associations (ASAs) — student organisations built on Ambedkarite principles, present in virtually every major Indian university — are the living continuation of this tradition. They provide peer support networks for Dalit students navigating hostile institutional environments, document discrimination cases, and build the political consciousness that the institution will not provide. Rohith Vemula's Ambedkar Students Association at the University of Hyderabad was the organisation for whose membership he was punished. The ASA was not a radical organisation by any reasonable measure: it organised cultural events, reading groups, film screenings, and discussions. Its radical act was existing — as a named Dalit political space, in an institution that preferred its Dalit students invisible and individually tolerated rather than collectively organised. The suppression of the ASA was the suppression of the idea that Dalit students have the right to organise as Dalit students. Rohith Vemula's death was the consequence of that suppression.

1848 — The First School

Savitribai Phule Walked Through the Gauntlet Every Day

Pune, 1848. First school for untouchable girls in India. Savitribai walked through crowds throwing dung and stones daily. She carried a second sari to change into on arrival. By 1852, the Phules had three schools. The British administration gave Jyotirao an award. The Brahmin press called him a traitor. Within five years of opening, the schools had educated hundreds of girls from communities that had never been permitted to read. The stone-throwers are not remembered. The students are.

Ambedkar's Formation

Every Step Opposed — Every Step Taken Anyway

Gunny sack at Elphinstone. Professor refusing water jug at Sydenham. Baroda State scholarship from an enlightened king — without which Columbia does not happen. PhD from Columbia, law degree from London, PhD from LSE — and still cannot rent a room in Baroda because he is Dalit. His intellectual formation was a sustained fight against exclusion at every stage. The constitution he wrote guarantees what he was denied. That is not irony. That is intention.

Ambedkar Students Associations

Organising as a Radical Act

Present in virtually every major Indian university. They document discrimination, provide peer networks, build political consciousness. Rohith Vemula's ASA at Hyderabad organised film screenings and reading groups. The ABVP complaint that led to his suspension accused the ASA of assault. The university responded not by investigating the assault claim but by suspending five ASA members. The ASA's crime was organised Dalit presence. In an institution that preferred Dalit students individually tolerated rather than collectively empowered, organised presence was provocation enough.

The Demand Going Forward

Not Charity. Constitutional Debt.

The Dalit education movement's core argument, from Ambedkar to the present: reservation and educational access are not charity extended by a generous state to unfortunate communities. They are partial repayment of a constitutional debt owed to communities from whom education was systematically stolen for three thousand years. The state that maintained the system of exclusion owes the communities it excluded the means of inclusion. This is not a generous policy option. It is the minimum that justice requires. And after 75 years, the minimum has not yet been fully delivered.

→

The single most revealing number about caste in Indian higher education is not the student dropout rate. It is the faculty composition. Who teaches determines what is considered knowledge, which questions are considered worth asking, which students are treated as having intellectual potential, and whose discomfort counts as a problem worth solving. Every IIT, IIM, AIIMS, and central university in India is led, intellectually governed, and daily administered by overwhelmingly upper-caste faculty — in institutions constitutionally mandated since 1950 to be otherwise. The mandate is not met. The shortfall is not coincidental. It is the product of recruitment processes that give committees with no diversity requirement the discretion to declare, year after year, that no suitable SC/ST candidates were found. The candidates exist. The committees are not looking.

The IIT system — the hard data. Parliamentary Standing Committee on Education's 2022–23 report: SC/ST combined faculty across all 23 IITs — approximately 3.8%. Mandated by reservation provisions: 22.5% (15% SC + 7.5% ST). The gap is not a few percentage points. It is a gap of 18+ percentage points that has persisted for decades despite formal legal mandates, parliamentary committee scrutiny, and ministry circulars. IIT Bombay's SC faculty as of 2022: approximately 2.1%. IIT Delhi: approximately 1.9%. IIT Madras: approximately 2.4%. The institutions producing India's engineering elite have faculties that are structurally incapable of providing SC/ST students with senior academic mentors who share their social position. The pipeline argument — "no qualified SC/ST candidates" — is deployed every recruitment cycle by the same institutions whose hostile internal environments have been producing the thin pipeline for sixty years.

AIIMS — the medical establishment's numbers. All India Institute of Medical Sciences Delhi, the most prestigious medical institution in India, trains the doctors who train other doctors, produces the professors who sit on national health policy boards, and supplies the faculty to every AIIMS subsequently established. SC/ST faculty at AIIMS Delhi: below 5% in most departments as of available RTI data (2021–22). In the Surgery and Medicine departments — the apex of medical hierarchy — SC/ST faculty presence is near zero. The consequence is structural: SC/ST medical students at AIIMS navigate their entire training in an environment in which no one in a position of institutional authority shares their social background. The senior faculty who control residency placements, research opportunities, prize nominations, and recommendation letters are drawn almost entirely from communities that have always been at the top of the caste hierarchy. This is the institutional context in which Payal Tadvi died. It is not background. It is cause.

Delhi University — the scale of the vacancy. Delhi University has over 600 departments and colleges. A 2019 RTI filed by journalist Dilnoza Yusupova revealed: across DU's colleges, SC faculty vacancies — positions mandated to be filled by SC candidates under reservation — had been unfilled for periods ranging from 3 to 17 years. The explanation given consistently: "no suitable SC candidate found." What this means in practice: the selection committee — composed of faculty who are themselves upper-caste in most departments — reviews SC candidates and finds them below the bar. The bar is set by, and the evaluation is conducted by, people with zero institutional incentive to find SC candidates suitable and strong structural incentive not to — because an SC faculty hire changes the social composition of a department that has been homogeneous for decades. A 2023 Delhi High Court order specifically directed DU to fill reserved vacancies within a timeframe. Compliance: partial, contested, ongoing.

The promotion barrier — the second gate. Even where SC/ST faculty are hired, promotion to Associate Professor and Professor levels is controlled by university committees whose composition has no diversity mandate. Parliamentary committee data (2022): while SC/ST representation at the Assistant Professor level is 8–10% in central universities (below mandated 15% but closer than at the IITs), representation at the Professor level falls to 3–5%. The system produces a career ceiling: SC/ST faculty enter at the bottom of the hierarchy and disproportionately remain there. Senior positions — which carry influence over admissions, fellowships, publications, and institutional policy — remain upper-caste territory at the exact same institutions that formally committed to reservation in 1950. The commitment is to entry. The hierarchy is protected at every step above entry.

"I have been an Assistant Professor for 14 years. I have published more than colleagues who were promoted 8 years ago. The selection committee has twice found me 'not yet ready.' The committee members do not know my name. They know my caste certificate."
— SC faculty member, central university, testimony to Scheduled Castes Commission 2022 (anonymised)
IITs — Parliamentary Data 2022–23

SC/ST Faculty 3.8% — Mandated 22.5%

Across all 23 IITs. The gap: 18.7 percentage points. IIT Bombay ~2.1%, IIT Delhi ~1.9%, IIT Madras ~2.4%. Institutions that train India's engineering elite have faculties nearly devoid of SC/ST representation. "No suitable candidates found" is the standard explanation. The explanation has been offered every year for 70 years. In year 71 it is still being offered.

AIIMS Delhi — RTI Data 2021–22

Surgery & Medicine: SC/ST Faculty Near Zero

AIIMS Delhi's apex clinical departments have near-zero SC/ST faculty presence. The institution that sets standards for Indian medical education, produces national health policy, and trains the trainers of doctors is almost entirely upper-caste in its intellectual leadership. This is the environment in which resident doctors from SC communities learn. The power differential in a department where your supervisors are from communities that have historically controlled, humiliated, and excluded your community — and where those supervisors control your grades, your placements, and your future — cannot be described in policy language. It can be described in Payal Tadvi's WhatsApp voice message to her mother.

Delhi University — RTI 2019

SC Faculty Vacancies Unfilled 3–17 Years

Across 600+ DU departments and colleges. Vacancies mandated by reservation left open for up to 17 consecutive years. "No suitable SC candidate found" repeated for 17 years in the same position. Delhi HC issued compliance orders in 2023. Partial compliance, ongoing contestation. The university that produces a significant share of India's civil servants, journalists, lawyers, and academics has a faculty that has spent 17 years being unable to find a suitable SC candidate for its reserved positions.

The Promotion Ceiling

SC/ST Professor Level: 3–5% in Central Universities

SC/ST assistant professors in central universities: 8–10% (below mandated 15%, but substantially better than IITs). SC/ST full professors: 3–5%. The career ceiling is not a metaphor. It is a measurable number that shows the hierarchy is protected most fiercely at its senior levels — where institutional power (over admissions, fellowships, publications, policy) actually sits. Entry is partially conceded. Power is not.

Three mechanisms operate at the level of individual students to ensure that SC/ST students who survive the entry barriers — who pass the entrance exam, who pay the fees, who arrive at the institution — are degraded, economically choked, or academically destroyed before they can complete what they came to do. These are not isolated incidents of bad individual behaviour. They are documented patterns consistent enough across institutions and decades to constitute systemic practice: answer sheet manipulation that destroys academic careers through mark sabotage, specific targeted physical and verbal abuse in medical institutions where hierarchy is enforced through the bodies of junior doctors, and scholarship delays that use the state's own treasury as a weapon to starve SC students into dropping out. Each operates deniably. Together they form a complete system for reversing reservation's effects without formally abolishing it.

Evaluation sabotage — marks as a weapon. Multiple High Court cases across India have heard SC/ST students' allegations that professors deliberately under-marked their answer sheets, assigned failing grades to work that passing grades had been given in prior semesters from the same student, or simply marked answer sheets as absent when they had been submitted. The pattern documented in High Court proceedings: the student files an RTI for their answer sheet. The answer sheet is returned with marks inconsistent with the level of work done — in some cases, blank sheets are returned for papers the student demonstrably sat. The student seeks re-evaluation. Re-evaluation committees are composed of faculty from the same department — the same professional community as the evaluator being challenged. The Madras High Court (2019) ordered re-evaluation of a PhD student's thesis viva after finding unexplained inconsistency between the internal examiner's marking and external examiner's marking; the internal examiner was from the same dominant-caste community as the panel majority that had failed the student. The Bombay High Court (2021) directed re-evaluation of a postgraduate medical examination after documented discrepancy between two rounds of marking for an SC student at a Mumbai medical college. The Allahabad High Court has received multiple such petitions. In each case, the institutional response follows the same template: the faculty are respected academics exercising academic discretion, the student's complaint is unfounded, re-evaluation is an attack on academic freedom. Courts have, in documented cases, disagreed.

AIIMS and medical college abuse — the residency system as caste enforcement. The medical residency hierarchy — in which junior doctors (residents and interns) are entirely subordinate to senior doctors (consultants, professors) — is one of the most extreme institutional hierarchies in India, and it maps with extraordinary consistency onto the caste hierarchy. Multiple documented cases across AIIMS Delhi, AIIMS Bhopal, King George's Medical University (Lucknow), Grant Medical College (Mumbai), and Osmania Medical College (Hyderabad): SC/ST resident doctors assigned the most physically demanding and professionally unrewarding duties while upper-caste residents are given the procedures that build clinical skills; denied letters of recommendation that are essential for fellowship applications; publicly humiliated in front of patients with casteist comments that are deniable as "tough teaching style"; and physically assaulted — slapped, pushed — in cases where the reporting doctor is Dalit and the senior doctor knows that no institutional mechanism will act on a complaint against a senior faculty member of the dominant community. Payal Tadvi's case is the most documented. It is not the most extreme. It is the one that ended in a death visible enough that a complaint could not be suppressed.

The Internal Complaints Committee problem. Every university is required under UGC guidelines to have an Equal Opportunity Cell and mechanisms for SC/ST students to report discrimination. The composition of these committees: appointed by the university administration — which means appointed by the same upper-caste academic establishment whose conduct the committees are meant to investigate. The Equal Opportunity Cell at the University of Hyderabad — the institution where Rohith Vemula died — had an SC/ST Studies Centre that produced scholarship on caste discrimination. The same institution's administration suspended Rohith Vemula and four other Dalit students at the request of a Union Minister connected to the ruling party, in response to a complaint filed by the ABVP. The Equal Opportunity Cell's complaint mechanism was available to Rohith Vemula. He had used it. It had not protected him. The committee investigating his death was constituted by the administration being investigated for his death. The findings: no caste angle. This is not a failure of the system. It is the system working as designed.

Scholarship delay — the treasury as weapon. SC/ST students in government higher education are entitled to Post-Matric Scholarships — funded by the Ministry of Social Justice and Empowerment — which cover tuition, hostel fees, and a maintenance allowance. The scheme is budgeted. The money exists. In UP, Bihar, MP, Rajasthan, and Odisha, the scholarship has been documented arriving 6 to 18 months late, or not at all for the academic year. This is not administrative inefficiency. It is a choice: the state government receives the central grant, holds it in its treasury, and does not disburse it. The interest on unspent scholarships accrues to the state. The Dalit student who does not receive the scholarship faces a binary: borrow to stay enrolled, or drop out. The borrowing is from moneylenders or from the same landlord networks whose control of the student's family constitutes the economic basis of caste subordination. The scholarship delay is a mechanism for ensuring that the promise of education — the principal tool through which Dalit communities have been building independence from those landlord networks — is systematically sabotaged by the state that made the promise. A 2020 Supreme Court order directed all states to disburse Post-Matric Scholarships within 30 days of the academic year beginning. UP's compliance in 2021: partial. In 2022: partial. In 2023: partial. The Supreme Court's order is clear. The state's implementation is deliberate defiance.

"The money is there. It is in the government's account. My son stopped eating lunch to save fees. The government collected interest on the money that was meant to feed him while he studied."
— Father of SC student, Varanasi, affidavit to Allahabad HC scholarship petition, 2022
High Court Cases — Evaluation Fraud

Madras HC 2019 · Bombay HC 2021 · Allahabad HC Multiple

Madras HC ordered PhD thesis re-evaluation after documented discrepancy in examiner marking; internal examiner from same dominant community as failing-vote majority. Bombay HC directed medical PG examination re-evaluation after SC student's paper marked drastically differently in two rounds. Allahabad HC has received multiple petitions alleging marks sabotage in DU-affiliated and UP state university examinations. Courts have found merit in these petitions. Institutions have appealed and delayed. The academic career ends while the litigation continues.

Medical Residency Caste Abuse

AIIMS Delhi · KGMU · Grant Medical · Osmania

Documented across five major medical institutions: SC/ST residents assigned disproportionate menial duties; denied fellowship recommendation letters; publicly humiliated with casteist language; physically assaulted in cases where reporting mechanisms are controlled by the same senior faculty community. The medical hierarchy — in which junior doctors have no recourse against senior doctors who control their careers — amplifies caste power into its most concentrated institutional form. Payal Tadvi is the documented fatality. She is not the only victim.

Post-Matric Scholarship Delays

UP, Bihar, MP, Rajasthan — 6 to 18 Months Late

Supreme Court 2020 order: disburse within 30 days of academic year. UP compliance 2021, 2022, 2023: partial. Students borrow, drop out, or go hungry. State governments earn interest on withheld scholarship funds. The scholarship scheme's budget exists. The disbursement is delayed by choice. An estimated 1.5–2 crore SC/ST students are affected annually by scholarship delays across the country — the largest single mechanism for reversing the educational access that reservation is meant to provide.

The Committee Capture Problem

Watchdog = Dog Being Watched

Equal Opportunity Cells, Internal Complaints Committees, Anti-Discrimination Officers — mandated by UGC, appointed by university administrations that are overwhelmingly upper-caste. The institution investigates itself. The mechanism is designed to produce outcomes that protect the institution's reputation rather than the student's rights. Rohith Vemula filed complaints through official channels before his death. The channels were constituted by the administration that was targeting him. The outcome: his suspension was upheld, not reversed. The committee mechanism is not a safety net. It is a documentation trail that the institution uses to demonstrate procedural compliance while doing nothing.

National Crime Records Bureau data 2015–2022. Every graph the government prints but does not explain.

The NCRB publishes crimes against Scheduled Castes annually. The numbers are underregistrations — police hostility, fear, and counter-FIR threats mean most crimes never become data. What reaches the NCRB is the floor. What these charts show is the minimum scale of what India inflicts on its Dalit citizens every year.

NCRB 2015–2022
Annual Crimes Against SCs — 8-Year Trend
38,670 → 50,291. Every year higher than the last.
+30.1% increase 2015–2022. The SC/ST Atrocities Act was strengthened in 2018. Crimes went up, not down. The law did not deter. The law was never the binding constraint.
NCRB 2022
State-Wise Crimes — Top 10
UP alone: 26% of all crimes against SCs in India.
UP + Rajasthan + MP = 58% of India's registered SC crimes with ~25% of India's SC population. These are not just "high crime" states. They are high-impunity states.
NCRB 2022 — Crime Breakdown
What Kind of Crimes — Full Taxonomy
Assault & hurt dominate. Every category is higher than the previous year.
3,676 rapes = 10/day official. Actual: est. 50–100/day.
1,040 murders. Less than 30% ever result in conviction.
8,900+ outraging modesty — groping, stripping, parading.
930+ kidnapping & abduction. Most are women and children.
The Justice Funnel
From FIR to Conviction — What Survives
100 crimes registered. 9.7 end in conviction.
From 50,291 crimes → 41,557 chargesheets → 17,100 trials completed → ~4,900 convictions. 9.7% end-to-end conviction rate. 90.3% of registered crimes produce no conviction. These are the registered crimes.
Rate per 1 Lakh SC Population
Crime Rate — Controlling for SC Population Size
Rajasthan overtakes UP when you control for population. Telangana in top 5.
Raw counts favour large states. Per-lakh rate reveals intensity of targeting. Rajasthan's SC population faces nearly 4x the national average crime rate per lakh SC persons.
NCRB 2015–2022 — SC Women
Rapes of Dalit Women — Annual Count
Steady rise. 2020 dip = COVID lockdown suppression of reporting, not reduction in rapes.
Rights groups estimate actual rapes at 10–20x registered cases. 2020's apparent decline corresponds exactly to COVID-period restrictions on movement — when victims couldn't reach police stations, not when perpetrators stopped.
State-Wise Justice 2022
Conviction Rate Under SC/ST POA Act — By State
States with most crimes have lowest conviction rates. This is not coincidence.
UP: 8% conviction rate. Rajasthan: 11%. Bihar: 6%. These are the three states with the most registered crimes. The inverse correlation between crime volume and conviction rate is not statistical noise. It is the political economy of impunity.
Pendency — The Backlog Crisis
Cases Pending at Each Stage — 2022
470,000+ cases pending trial. At current disposal rate: 15+ years to clear backlog.
Special courts under the SC/ST POA Act were mandated specifically to speed trials. As of 2022, only 14 states have set up functional Special Courts. At current disposal rates, the pending backlog will never clear — new cases pile in faster than old ones are resolved.
Police Stage — Where Cases Die First
Chargesheet Rate vs National Average — 2022
SC/ST cases have lower chargesheet rates than general IPC cases in most high-crime states.
A lower chargesheet rate for SC/ST cases than for general IPC cases means: police are less likely to file a chargesheet — to formally accuse someone — in atrocity cases than in general crimes. This is the first gate of impunity. Before the judge sees the case, the police have already been given it and declined to push it forward.
Why Cases Fail — Court Stage
Disposition of Completed Trials — 2022
Of completed trials: convicted, acquitted, compounded, or withdrawn.
"Compounded" = settled out of court, typically under pressure on the victim. "Withdrawn by prosecution" = government lawyer drops the case. Both are mechanisms that convert formal legal action into informal suppression. Combined with acquittals, over 74% of completed trials end without a single conviction.
Violence Peaks Where Progress Peaks
SC Literacy vs Crime Rate — State Correlation
Violence against Dalits intensifies as Dalit communities become more educated and mobile — not less.
Tamil Nadu and Maharashtra — states with highest Dalit education and mobility — also have high violence rates. The pattern across India: rising Dalit assertion provokes rising upper-caste violence. The violence is a response to progress, not evidence of its absence.
2012 → 2022 Decade Comparison
Crime Categories — Ten Years of Change
Which crimes grew fastest. Rape up 47%. Murder up 22%. No category fell.
Across every crime category, 2022 numbers are higher than 2012. India has had 10 years of economic growth, constitutional amendments, Supreme Court orders, and government schemes. No category declined. The trend is unbroken. The trajectory is upward.
Constitutional Mandate vs Ground Reality
Special Courts for SC/ST Cases — Mandated vs Established vs Functional
POA Act 2018 amendment mandated Special Courts in every district. Reality: wildly different by state.
Mandated
766
One per district across India. Required under POA Act 2018 amendment.
Established
~194
Only 14 states have any. Most are "designated" courts — existing courts given the label without additional resources.
Functional with Exclusive Jurisdiction
<50
Fewer than 50 courts in all of India handle only SC/ST cases. In UP, with 13,000+ SC crimes/year: 0 exclusive Special Courts as of 2023.
SC/ST POA Act — Most Used Sections
Which Legal Provisions Are Actually Invoked
NCRB section-wise breakdown — what acts are charged.
Section 3(1)(r)&(s) — insult/humiliation in public — is the most invoked provision but has the lowest conviction rate. Section 3(2)(v) — offences under IPC committed against SC — is the highest-penalty provision and is charged least often. Police charge the easiest provisions, not the strongest ones.
Police Force Composition
SC/ST Representation in Police — Who Registers the FIR
The person at the FIR counter determines whether your crime becomes a statistic.
SC officers in police: ~14–16% (near mandated), but concentrated in constable and sub-inspector ranks. In Inspector and above — who control investigations and chargesheets — SC representation drops to under 8% nationally. The seniority gap means SC FIRs are investigated by upper-caste officers.
What These Numbers Actually Mean
The Floor, Not the Ceiling

Every NCRB number is a registered crime. Rights organisations estimate actual violence is 10–20x higher across all categories. The NCRB data is what police chose to record — after the victim navigated hostility, after the counter-case threat, after the landlord's men told the family what would happen if they filed. What you see here is what survived all of that.

The Inverse of Progress

Numbers go up as Dalit communities become more educated, more mobile, more politically organised. This is not a paradox. It is the mechanism: upper-caste violence increases when Dalit communities threaten the hierarchy, not when they are fully subjugated. Rising crimes in Tamil Nadu and Maharashtra — states with the most Dalit assertion — confirm this. Subjugation is peaceful. Resistance is violent. The NCRB data measures the violence of the resistance moment.

The System Measuring Itself

The NCRB is a government body. The police who register crimes are government employees. The courts that produce conviction rates are government courts. The government publishes data showing 9.7% end-to-end conviction rates, 470,000+ pending cases, and crimes rising every year — and continues to describe the SC/ST Atrocities Act as "robust." The data and the description cannot both be true. One of them is the government's evidence. The other is the government's claim. Read the evidence.

🧠
The Ideology — How Caste Justifies Itself
Karma. Dharma. Pollution. Harmony. Four pillars that turned a system of exploitation into divine order.
Foundation03
Full SectionNEW
▼

Every system of sustained oppression requires an ideology — a framework of ideas that makes the oppression appear natural, deserved, inevitable, and even sacred. Without ideology, raw power must be enforced visibly every day, which is expensive and unstable. With ideology, the oppressed internalise their own subordination. They police themselves. They explain their suffering as cosmic justice. They teach their children that this is how the world is and must be. Caste's ideology is among the most sophisticated and durable ever constructed — it has survived colonialism, industrialisation, mass literacy, constitutional abolition, and sixty years of state welfare. Understanding each pillar of this ideology — precisely, mechanically, with specific texts and specific refutations — is the prerequisite for understanding why the legal abolition of untouchability has not abolished untouchability.

The karma doctrine as applied to caste is this: your birth caste is the precise consequence of your moral conduct in previous lives. You were born untouchable because of sins committed before this existence. Your suffering is not injustice — it is accounting. It is the universe settling scores with your soul. The child born into a family that manually removes human excrement was morally inferior in a previous life and is now repaying the cosmic debt through hereditary sewage work in this one.

This is not a fringe interpretation. It is stated explicitly in the Manusmriti (Manu 10.5, 12.40–68) and elaborated across the Puranic literature: low birth is the result of specific sins — disrespect for Brahmins, violation of ritual purity, arrogance. High birth is the reward for piety and deference in past lives. The doctrine converts the entire social hierarchy into a moral ledger: the Brahmin at the top earned it. The Valmiki at the bottom deserved it. Both current positions are the output of previous choices, made in lives neither party remembers.

The ideological function is precise: it removes the possibility of social critique. If my low birth is the cosmic consequence of my own previous conduct, then the upper-caste person who humiliates me is not committing an injustice. They are simply occupying the position their karma earned. If I resist my position — if I demand dignity, if I refuse hereditary occupation, if I organise politically — I am not asserting my humanity. I am defying the cosmic moral order and accumulating bad karma for the next life. Resistance is converted into sin. Endurance is converted into virtue. The ideology does not just justify the hierarchy. It immunises it against the only weapon the oppressed have: the moral claim that what is happening to them is wrong.

"The Hindu must be a slave not only in his economic life but in his spiritual life as well. The very God he worships has commanded him to remain in servitude."
— B.R. Ambedkar, Annihilation of Caste, 1936
Ambedkar's Demolition

Karma Applied to Birth Position Is Not Moral — It's Absurd

If karma justifies inherited caste position, then the child born into a sweeper family was morally inferior in a previous life. The child. Who has done nothing yet in this life. Who cannot remember the previous life. Who cannot challenge the verdict. Who cannot appeal. The doctrine requires you to punish a person for conduct they cannot recall, in a court they cannot access, under a sentence with no defined end. This is not a moral system. This is inherited punishment dressed in cosmic language.

Who Benefits from Karma Ideology

The Doctrine Is Not Impartial — It Always Favours the Current Winner

Any ideology that says "your current position reflects your moral worth" is, by definition, an ideology that benefits whoever currently has the best position. In a society where upper castes hold power, karma ideology becomes a divine endorsement of their power. It does not challenge the powerful to justify themselves morally. It tells them their power is the moral justification. The doctrine could only have been constructed by, and for, those who were already at the top.

The Persistence Mechanism

Internalised by the Oppressed — The Ideology's Greatest Triumph

Studies of Dalit communities by sociologists including Gopal Guru and Suraj Yengde document the degree to which karma ideology has been internalised by members of oppressed communities themselves — explaining their own suffering as cosmic consequence, discouraging their children from asserting rights by warning of worse karma in the next life. This internalisation is the ideology's greatest achievement: it converts the oppressed into the most reliable enforcers of their own subjugation.

Dharma, as applied to caste, is the doctrine that each caste has a specific sacred duty — a svadharma — which includes its hereditary occupational assignment. The Brahmin's dharma is to study, teach, and perform religious ritual. The Kshatriya's dharma is to fight and govern. The Vaishya's dharma is to trade and farm. The Shudra's dharma is to serve the three upper varnas. The untouchable's dharma — not even assigned a varna in the original scheme — is to perform the tasks so ritually polluting that no caste person can perform them: remove dead animals, clean human waste, tan leather, wash the dead.

The critical word in the Gita's formulation is svadharma — one's own dharma. In the Bhagavad Gita (18.47), Krishna tells Arjuna: "Better is one's own dharma, though imperfect, than the dharma of another, though well performed." The verse is directed at Arjuna — a Kshatriya who wants to refuse battle. But its application to caste is systematic and explicit in Brahminical commentary: a Shudra who performs a Brahmin's duties — studying, teaching, performing ritual — is not fulfilling dharma well. He is violating cosmic order. The content of the occupation matters less than the caste assignment of the occupation. Excellence within the wrong caste duty is worse than mediocrity within the right one.

The ideological function: dharma converts economic exploitation into religious virtue for all parties. The Brahmin who receives the Valmiki's sanitation service is not exploiting him. Both are fulfilling their divine function. The relationship is not one of extraction — it is one of complementary dharmic roles. Each needs the other. The whole is sacred. Resistance to this arrangement is not just economically threatening — it is religiously wrong. When a manual scavenger refuses to clean sewage — when Bezwada Wilson and the Safai Karmachari Andolan organise to demand mechanisation — they are not just demanding labour rights. They are, in the Brahminical framework, violating divine order. The state's slow enforcement of the Manual Scavenging Act, the police hostility toward manual scavenging abolition movements — these are downstream effects of an ideology that frames the work as sacred assignment.

"Manu's code prescribed that a Chandala's occupation shall be to carry out the corpses of persons who have no relatives; he shall execute those condemned to death... His wealth shall be the clothes of the executed, their personal ornaments, and the iron of their weapons."
— Manusmriti 10.55–56. The "wealth" of execution work is defined as its refuse. This is not work. It is punishment assigned as occupation.
Manual Scavenging Today

The Dharma Doctrine's Living Consequence — 58,098 Manual Scavengers 2022

The Prohibition of Employment as Manual Scavengers and their Rehabilitation Act was passed in 1993, strengthened in 2013. Manual scavenging continues because the ideology that assigns Valmiki/Balmiki communities to this work predates the law by 3,000 years and is reproduced in family pressure, community expectation, and municipal employment practices. When panchayats refuse to hire Dalit sanitation workers for mechanised work while continuing to rely on Valmiki families for manual sewage removal, they are enacting dharma ideology through municipal administration.

Bezwada Wilson — Safai Karmachari Andolan

The Man Fighting a 3,000-Year-Old Job Description

Bezwada Wilson, born into a manual scavenging family in Kurnool district, AP, founded the Safai Karmachari Andolan in 1994. His work: documenting that manual scavenging continues illegally, filing RTIs to expose municipal employers, organising affected families to refuse the work, demanding rehabilitation under the 2013 Act. He received the Ramon Magsaysay Award in 2016. The Indian government's response to his documentation: denial that manual scavenging exists at the scale he documents. The 2022 census found 58,000+ manual scavengers. The government's official figure: much lower. The ideology that says this work is dharmic makes it harder to admit, document, and end.

Svadharma in Education

Why Dalit Education Was Specifically Prohibited — Not Just Discouraged

Manusmriti (2.16–2.24, 10.4): Shudras may not study the Vedas. If a Shudra listens to the recitation of the Vedas, his ears shall be filled with molten lead and lac. If he recites Vedic texts, his tongue shall be cut out. If he memorises them, his body shall be split. Education is the Brahmin's svadharma. A Shudra who acquires it is not ambitious. He is transgressing cosmic order. Every village that burned a Dalit school, every school that seated Dalit children outside, every professor who refused to grade a Dalit student's work — all of them were implementing a 3,000-year-old job description that said knowledge is not this person's dharma.

Ritual pollution — the doctrine that contact with certain bodies, certain substances, and certain occupations creates spiritual contamination that spreads through touch, proximity, shadow, and even gaze — is the mechanism through which untouchability is operationalised in daily life. It converts the social exclusion of Dalits from an act of cruelty into an act of hygiene. The upper caste who refuses to share a water vessel with a Dalit is not harming the Dalit. He is protecting himself from contamination. The logic of avoidance is the logic of self-protection, not aggression. This rhetorical conversion — from perpetrator to victim, from violence to defence — is the ideology's most brilliant move.

The pollution hierarchy is precise and graduated. The Brahmin's body is maximally pure. Contact with lower castes reduces purity in proportion to caste distance. The untouchable body is maximally impure — not merely at the bottom of the scale but in a qualitatively different category. Contact with an untouchable is not merely a minor pollution requiring minor ritual correction. It requires elaborate purification — bathing, discarding clothes, sometimes leaving and re-entering the house. In Kerala's documented history, this reached its extreme form: untouchables had to maintain specific physical distances from upper castes (Nambudiri Brahmins required untouchables to stay 64 feet away), and the shadow of an untouchable falling on a Brahmin was considered polluting. The shadow. Not the touch. The shadow.

The physical incoherence of the doctrine is its tell. Periyar posed it directly: if a Brahmin performs a fire ritual for several hours — handling sacred substances, invoking divine presence — and an untouchable simply walks on the same road, who pollutes whom when they are both in the same river afterwards? The Brahmin's ritual purity cannot be physical, because physically the Brahmin is sweating and the untouchable is bathing. The purity is social, not physical. It is a status, not a state. The pollution doctrine is not a theory of hygiene. It is a theory of hierarchy dressed as hygiene — in which the hierarchy is maintained by making its violation disgusting rather than merely forbidden.

This is why the pollution doctrine is so resistant to legal abolition. You cannot pass a law against disgust. You cannot make untouchability illegal and thereby stop upper-caste people from feeling that Dalit bodies are contaminating. The Constitutional abolition of untouchability (Article 17) abolished the legal infrastructure. It did not touch the psychological infrastructure — the generations of conditioning that make Dalit contact feel viscerally wrong to upper-caste bodies. Changing the law changed the official position. It did not change the nervous system that the ideology built over three thousand years.

Kerala — 64-Foot Rule

The Shadow Was Pollution — Documented in Living Memory

Early 20th century Kerala: Pulayas (untouchables) required to maintain 64 feet from Nambudiri Brahmins. Iravas: 32 feet. Nairs: 12 feet. The specific distances were not arbitrary — they were calibrated to the caste hierarchy. The breast cloth controversy (1820s–1915) in Travancore: lower-caste women were prohibited from covering their upper bodies — covering was a symbol of higher-caste status, and their bodies being visible was the physical expression of their available-for-use status in the caste order. When Nangeli cut off her own breasts in protest (c. 1803), she was resisting the embodied pollution doctrine — the doctrine that her body belonged to the display requirements of the caste hierarchy.

Water — The Primary Battleground

Every Village Has a Water Story — Most Villages Still Do

Water is where the pollution doctrine is most consistently and violently enforced because water is the most obvious daily necessity. Separate wells, separate taps, separate vessels — documented across every state from Independence to 2024. The 2016 Rajasthan water vessel murder (8-year-old beaten to death) is the most recent documented fatality. The mechanism: if a Dalit touches the shared water vessel, the water — and everyone who drinks it — becomes polluted. The child's touch is an act of aggression against the community's purity. The teacher's violence is self-defence. The ideology makes the child the perpetrator of the act that kills him.

Inter-Caste Dining — Still Contested

Upper Castes Will Not Eat Food Cooked by Dalits in 2024

Mid-day meal segregation (documented in 11 states 2010–2023) is the pollution doctrine in school cafeterias. Upper-caste parents lobbying to remove Dalit cooks from school kitchens — documented in Rajasthan, UP, Gujarat — are applying the pollution doctrine to the government's welfare scheme. The food cooked by a Dalit hand is polluted food. The child who eats it becomes polluted. The parent who allows this is failing in their caste duty. The RTE Act says nothing about this. The pollution doctrine is older than the RTE Act by three thousand years and is currently winning.

The harmony argument is the ideology's contemporary form — what karma, dharma, and pollution look like when they have been stripped of their explicit religious content and repackaged for a liberal, educated audience. It operates through a set of connected claims: every community has its traditions, which deserve respect; the caste system historically provided social organisation and division of labour; outsiders who seek to "impose" inter-caste marriage or reservations are disturbing a "natural" social order; change must come from within, gradually, through education and goodwill rather than through legislation and political pressure. This argument is made by middle-class upper-caste people who would never state the karma doctrine explicitly but who arrive at the same political conclusion through apparently secular reasoning.

The harmony argument has one empirical problem: the society it describes as "harmonious" has a 9.7% conviction rate for crimes against Dalits, 50,291 registered crimes in 2022, and a documented 3,000-year history of systematic violence against communities that resist their assigned position. This is not harmony. It is a particular kind of peace — the peace of the defeated, who have been made to understand that resistance is more costly than endurance. Calling this "harmony" requires looking at the silence of the crushed and hearing music.

Ambedkar on harmony, from Annihilation of Caste: "The Hindu social order is a system of rights. But it is a system of rights for some and a system of duties — uncompensated duties — for others. It is not a system of rights for all. It is not a system where those with duties have rights, and those with rights have duties. The Brahmin has rights without duties. The Shudra has duties without rights. This is not social organisation. It is social exploitation. Calling it harmony is like calling slavery a labour contract."

The political function of harmony ideology in 2024: it converts every Dalit demand for rights into "disturbing communal harmony." A Dalit student who organises is "creating caste tension." A Dalit woman who files an FIR is "destabilising the community." A Dalit politician who wins is "playing caste politics." In each case, the person asserting a right is framed as the aggressor, and the person defending a privilege is framed as the defender of peace. The ideology converts the demand for equality into a threat, and the maintenance of inequality into a virtue.

The Khap Panchayat

Honour Killing as Harmony Enforcement

Khap panchayats — caste councils in Haryana, Rajasthan, UP, and western UP — issue orders prohibiting inter-caste and same-gotra marriages and organising violence against couples who violate these prohibitions. Their explicit justification: protecting community honour and social harmony. The Supreme Court (Shakti Vahini v. Union of India, 2018) ruled that khap diktats have no legal standing and that honour killing is murder. Khap panchayats continue to operate. The harmony they protect is the harmony of endogamy — which is also the reproductive mechanism of caste.

Village Social Boycott

Harmony Means: Know Your Place or Be Destroyed Economically

Social boycott — the collective refusal by dominant-caste villagers to engage economically or socially with a Dalit family that has violated caste norms — is documented across India as a tool of caste enforcement. Prohibited under the Maharashtra Prohibition of Social Boycott Act (2016) and theoretically actionable under SC/ST POA Act provisions, social boycotts are rarely prosecuted. The village that boycotts a Dalit family that filed a police complaint calls itself peaceful. The family that filed the complaint has disturbed the harmony. The ideology makes the victim responsible for the violence directed at them.

RSS/Hindutva and Varnashrama

Harmony Ideology in Political Form

The RSS position on caste has officially opposed untouchability while defending varnashrama dharma — the system of caste-based duties — as the organic social organisation of Hindu society. This allows simultaneous condemnation of "abuses" (untouchability) while defending the structure that produces them (hereditary varna). The BJP's political management of caste: OBC communities absorbed through Hindutva nationalism; SC communities managed through welfare schemes; varnashrama ideology defended as Hindu culture against "anti-Hindu" Ambedkarite critique. The harmony is maintained while the structure is defended.

The most powerful refutations of caste ideology were not written by outsiders. They were written by people who lived inside the system — who knew it from the inside out and dismantled it with surgical precision. Ambedkar, Periyar, Phule, Kabir — each attacked a different pillar of the ideology, and together they leave nothing standing.

Ambedkar's refutation of karma: In Annihilation of Caste (1936) and The Buddha and His Dhamma (1956), Ambedkar argued that the karma doctrine as applied to birth caste is not a moral system — it is an immunisation of current privilege against moral challenge. A genuine moral system requires that the person being punished have performed the act being punished, have the capacity to understand the connection between act and consequence, and have the theoretical possibility of behaving differently. Birth-karma satisfies none of these conditions. The child cannot remember the previous life, cannot understand the supposed connection, and cannot change their birth. Punishment without understanding, without memory, and without possibility of different conduct is not justice. It is magic dressed as ethics — and its only consistent effect is to make the privileged feel righteous and the oppressed feel guilty.

Periyar's refutation of pollution: E.V. Ramasamy Periyar (1879–1973, founder of the Self-Respect Movement, Tamil Nadu) attacked the pollution doctrine through what he called "rational enquiry" — applying consistent logic to its claims. His argument: if Brahmins are ritually pure because they perform Vedic ritual, and if untouchables are ritually impure because they handle death and human waste, then what happens when a Brahmin handles a cow's dung — which is considered sacred and purifying in Hindu practice? The doctrine requires that handling cow dung purifies while handling human waste pollutes. What is the chemical or spiritual difference between bovine and human waste that produces this reversal? There is none. The purity/pollution distinction is not based in any consistent principle. It is based in social hierarchy: the things associated with upper-caste activity are pure because upper castes defined purity; the things associated with Dalit activity are impure because upper castes defined impurity.

Jyotirao Phule's refutation of dharma: Phule's Gulamgiri ("Slavery," 1873) made the argument that Brahminical dharma ideology is not ancient divine revelation — it is recent political construction by a conquering group. His argument: the Aryans were invaders who conquered the indigenous peoples of the subcontinent (the Shudras and Ati-Shudras, ancestors of today's OBCs and Dalits). The varna system was not cosmic ordering — it was the administrative structure of conquest, in which the conquered were assigned permanently subordinate positions and given religious sanction for their subordination. Dharma is the ideology of conquest calling itself divine order. The refutation of dharma requires recognising not its imperfect implementation but its illegitimate origin.

Ambedkar — Annihilation of Caste (1936)

The Speech That Was Never Delivered — Still the Most Radical Text in Indian Political History

Ambedkar was invited to deliver the presidential address to the Jat-Pat-Todak Mandal conference in 1936. The organisers received the written text in advance and cancelled the conference — they could not endorse what it said. Ambedkar published it himself. It contains the argument that caste cannot be reformed — it must be annihilated, because its foundation is the Brahminical scriptures that give it religious sanction. "You must destroy the authority of the Shastras. You must have the courage to tell the Hindus that what is wrong with them is their religion." The speech that was too radical to be delivered in 1936 is still the most comprehensive critique of caste ideology in any language.

Periyar — Self-Respect Movement

Tamil Nadu's Rationalist Revolution — Still Ongoing

Periyar founded the Self-Respect Movement (1925) and later the Dravidar Kazhagam (1944) on the premise that Brahminism is a political ideology masquerading as religion, and that its demolition requires rational critique, not devotional counter-practice. He organised self-respect marriages — conducted without priests, without Brahminical ritual, by the couple themselves — as a direct assault on the ceremony that reproduced caste hierarchy through marriage. Tamil Nadu today has the most developed Dravidian political tradition in India partly because of Periyar's 50-year project of dismantling the ideological infrastructure of caste, rather than simply building political coalitions around it.

Buddha's Original Teaching

The First Anti-Caste Philosopher — Ambedkar's Recovery

Ambedkar's The Buddha and His Dhamma (1956, completed days before his death) argues that the historical Siddhartha Gautama's original teaching was specifically directed against the Brahminical varna order — that birth does not determine spiritual worth, that the only hierarchy that matters is moral conduct rather than heredity. "Not by birth is one a Brahmin, not by birth is one an outcast. By deed is one a Brahmin, by deed is one an outcast." Ambedkar's conversion to Buddhism — 14 October 1956, Nagpur, with 600,000 followers — was not religious escapism. It was the construction of an alternative ideological foundation for Dalit identity, one that did not require acceptance of the karma-dharma-pollution framework.

The four classical pillars of caste ideology — karma, dharma, pollution, harmony — have not disappeared in modern India. They have mutated into secular-sounding forms that are more defensible in liberal discourse and more difficult to challenge because they no longer explicitly claim divine sanction. Understanding the modern mutations is essential for understanding why contemporary conversations about caste so frequently go wrong — why educated, English-speaking, professedly secular people end up defending the same positions that the Manusmriti defended, through apparently different arguments.

Karma → Meritocracy. The karma doctrine said: your high position reflects your moral worth. The meritocracy doctrine says: your high position reflects your ability and effort. Both convert current privilege into deserved outcome. Both place the responsibility for low position on the person occupying it — not on the system that placed them there. The specific mechanism differs (past-life conduct vs. present-life performance) but the ideological function is identical: the hierarchy is moral, not political; natural, not constructed; the result of individual differences, not historical injustice. The meritocracy argument is karma for people who have gone to secular school.

Dharma → Cultural Identity. The dharma doctrine said: your caste occupation is sacred duty. The cultural identity argument says: our community's traditions — including occupational traditions, marriage practices, and social boundaries — are our heritage and deserve respect. Both protect hereditary practice from challenge by converting the practice from economic arrangement to identity. When dominant-caste communities organise to prevent Dalit entry into their temples, their festivals, their water sources — and frame this as "protecting our traditions" — they are applying dharma ideology through the language of multiculturalism. The doctrine has learned to speak the language of the people who challenge it.

Pollution → Social Preference. The pollution doctrine said: Dalit bodies are ritually impure. The social preference argument says: people prefer to associate with members of their own community — this is natural, universal, not specifically casteist. When upper-caste parents remove their children from schools with Dalit cooks — when upper-caste tenants in Mumbai refuse to rent to Dalits (documented consistently in housing surveys) — when upper-caste professionals in tech companies exclude Dalit colleagues from social networks — they describe this as preference, not prejudice. The disgust response that the pollution doctrine spent three thousand years cultivating now presents itself as free association.

Harmony → Anti-Reservation. The harmony argument said: asserting Dalit rights disturbs social peace. The anti-reservation argument says: reservation creates divisions, perpetuates caste consciousness, and prevents the "natural" dissolution of caste through market integration and modernisation. Both blame Dalit assertion for social tension. Both locate the problem in the demand for equality rather than in the system that makes equality necessary. The anti-reservation argument is harmony ideology expressed as economic policy.

The Meritocracy Myth

India's "Merit" System Selects for Coaching Access, Not Talent

The Thorat-Attewell CV experiment (2007): identical qualifications, different names — upper-caste names get significantly more callbacks. Kota coaching cost = Dalit family's annual income. IIT entrance exam selects heavily for 2-year specialised coaching. Studies of IIT alumni outcomes show no performance difference between general and reservation entrants over career timelines. The "merit" that reservation supposedly displaces is itself caste-contaminated — it measures access to preparation, not native intelligence. Meritocracy is karma with a JEE rank.

Housing Discrimination Mumbai 2015

"Vegetarian Only" = Upper-Caste Only in Indian Housing Markets

Multiple studies of Mumbai housing markets document that "vegetarian only" and "brahmin/upper-caste family preferred" listings — technically illegal under Fair Housing principles — function as caste exclusion in secular language. A 2015 ICRW study found Dalit applicants faced rejection at 2–3x the rate of comparable upper-caste applicants in Mumbai's private rental market. The pollution doctrine — Dalit bodies contaminate living spaces — operates through "preference" language in the real estate market. No divine sanction required. The conditioning is sufficient.

The Language of Caste Erasure

"I Don't See Caste" — The Most Effective Caste Statement

The claim "I don't see caste" made by upper-caste individuals in professional contexts serves a precise ideological function: it makes caste discrimination impossible to name or challenge without the person naming it being accused of "creating" the division. If I don't see caste, then you — the Dalit person describing your experience of caste discrimination — are the one introducing caste into a caste-free space. The ideology's final form is the denial of the ideology's existence. "I don't see caste" is the harmony argument for people who have read enough liberal theory to be embarrassed by the harmony argument.

💰
Economy & Labour — The Wealth Apartheid
Land, bonded labour, private sector discrimination, and the gap that is widening — not closing.
Data10
Full SectionNEW
▼

The economic data on caste in India describes not a gap that is gradually closing through development and education — but a structured, self-reproducing system of exclusion that is widening in absolute terms as India grows. SC/ST communities — 25% of India's population — own 9% of agricultural land, supply the majority of its manual labour, and are almost entirely absent from its capital ownership class. In liberalised India since 1991, the communities that owned land, capital, and professional credentials benefited from economic growth. Dalit communities, owning almost none of these, did not. The liberalisation that produced India's billionaire class produced a new mechanism of Dalit exclusion: the private sector, which employs 90%+ of India's workers and is explicitly exempt from constitutional reservation.
