Source: All India Debt and Investment Survey (AIDIS) 2019. Mean assets include land, buildings, livestock, agricultural equipment, non-farm business assets, financial assets. The upper-caste household has 3.3x the assets of the SC household. In 1950, the gap was smaller in rupee terms. In 2024, after 30 years of liberalisation, it is larger. This is not convergence. This is divergence.

Land is the foundation of the Indian rural economy — the primary source of income, the primary collateral for credit, the primary basis of social power in village India, and the primary target of every upper-caste violence documented in this archive. SC/ST communities constitute 25% of India's population. They own approximately 9% of agricultural land. The distribution is not merely unequal — it is inverse to need and historic injustice. The communities that performed agricultural labour for three thousand years without compensation own the least agricultural land in India.

State-level data sharpens the picture. Bihar — where the Ranvir Sena conducted 22 documented massacres of Dalit agricultural labourers — has SC communities comprising 16% of the population owning less than 3% of agricultural land. Punjab — where SC communities are 32% of the population — own less than 3% of agricultural land, with Jat Sikh landlords holding the vast majority. Rajasthan — where manual scavenging is most prevalent — has SC communities at 18% of population owning under 4% of land. In each case, the violence documented elsewhere in this archive is violence defending this distribution — ensuring that Dalit communities that demand better wages, that contest elections, that send children to school, do not accumulate the land that would give them independence from the landlord class.

The constitutional promise and its failure. The Indian Constitution empowered state governments to enact land reforms — placing ceilings on landholding and redistributing surplus land to landless communities. Between 1950 and 1975, most states passed land ceiling legislation. The implementation was determined by the same landlord class that controlled state politics. Ceiling legislation allowed exceptions that swallowed the rule: land was transferred to family members, split into technically separate holdings, registered under benami (false-name) ownership, or simply never surveyed. The National Land Records Modernisation Programme — launched 2008, ongoing — has digitised land records but has not altered their content. The land that was not redistributed in 1955 is still not redistributed in 2024, and now has three more generations of landlord inheritance making the claim more legally complex.

Panchayat land — the stolen allocation. The 73rd Constitutional Amendment (1992) required village panchayats to allocate one-third of common land to SC households. This is a constitutional requirement. It has been implemented in approximately 10% of villages nationally — by activist organisation estimates derived from RTI data, village surveys, and state government compliance reports. Thirty-two years. 10% compliance. No panchayat member has been prosecuted for non-compliance. The land sits in panchayat records as SC-allocated. Dominant-caste families cultivate it. When Dalit families try to claim it — with documents — they are beaten, socially boycotted, or killed. The Karamchedu massacre (1985, Andhra Pradesh) was triggered by a Dalit family asserting their right to use a public pond. The connection between land claims and violence against Dalits is not metaphorical. It is causal and documented.

Agricultural Land Ownership

SC+ST = 25% Population, 9% Land

NSSO 77th Round (2019): SC households own 4.3% of agricultural land (16.6% of population). ST households own 4.7% (8.6% of population). Combined: 9% of land for 25% of people. Upper-caste Hindus (~15% of population): estimated 45–50% of agricultural land. The gap between population share and land share is the material foundation of every other inequality documented on this site.

Bihar State

22 Ranvir Sena Massacres — All Defending This Distribution

Bhumihar and Rajput landlords in Bihar hold 60–70% of agricultural land in districts where SC communities are 30–40% of the population. The Ranvir Sena — Bhumihar landlord private army — conducted 22 documented massacres of Dalit agricultural labourers between 1995 and 2000. Every massacre was triggered by Dalit demands: higher wages, land rights, political assertion. Every massacre defended the land distribution. Zero Ranvir Sena commanders have served significant prison time.

Panchayat Land — 32 Years, 10% Compliance

Constitutional Mandate, Not Implemented

73rd Amendment (1992): 1/3 of village common land to SC households. Survey data from RTI-based activist research (2020–23): ~10% of villages have implemented this. Dominant-caste families cultivate the allocated land. When Dalit families assert their claims, they face violence, boycott, or legal counter-cases. The panchayat system — which the 73rd Amendment also strengthened — is in most villages controlled by the same dominant-caste families whose land dominance the Amendment was meant to correct.

Urban Land — Slum Demolition

Dharavi, Mumbai — The Urban Land Question

Urban Dalit and migrant communities live overwhelmingly in informal settlements without formal property rights. Dharavi — Asia's largest urban settlement, ~1 million people, majority Dalit and migrant — faces ongoing redevelopment pressure. Offered "rehabilitation" housing in the same redevelopment projects is consistently inaccessible: too expensive for informal workers, documentation requirements exclude long-term residents without papers, and distance from livelihoods makes replacement housing functionally uninhabitable. Pa. Ranjith's Kaala (2018) literalised this: a Dalit slum leader fighting demolition. It is not fiction. It is the present.

The International Labour Organization estimates India has the world's largest bonded labour population — approximately 40 million people, the majority of them Dalit and Adivasi. The Bonded Labour System (Abolition) Act was passed in 1976. It is law. It is enforced almost nowhere. The reason is structural, not incidental: Labour Inspectors, District Magistrates, and police officers are appointed by state governments. State governments are controlled by political parties whose core electoral base includes the landlord and industrial class that benefits from bonded labour. The enforcement mechanism is constituted by the people the law is meant to prosecute. This is not a failure of will. It is the system working as designed.

How bonded labour works in 2024. The mechanism is debt: a Dalit family in agricultural distress — a medical emergency, a wedding, a drought — borrows from a landlord or labour contractor. The loan is structured so that it cannot be repaid at the wages the lender offers. The family's labour is pledged against the debt. The debt persists across generations — children inherit the obligation. The family cannot leave the land without repaying the debt. They cannot repay the debt at the wages being paid. They are bonded. The bond is enforced not primarily by physical chains but by the social and physical infrastructure of the village: the landlord controls access to the market, to other employment, to water, and to physical safety. Leaving is theoretically possible. Practically, it means leaving with a debt, no money, no alternative employment, and an angry landlord who may have political connections to the police.

Documented industries. NHRC (2012): UP brick kilns — 300,000+ bonded workers in a single state. Anti-Slavery International: Mirzapur-Bhadohi carpet belt — child and adult bonded labour producing carpets exported to the UK, US, and EU. The carpets sold in Western furniture chains have documented supply chains passing through bonded-labour workshops. ILO: Tamil Nadu's Sumangali Scheme — young women (predominantly Dalit and OBC) recruited from villages for 3-year textile mill contracts under which they receive a lump sum "marriage payment" at the end; during the contract they live in supervised hostels, cannot leave, and are paid below market wages. The "marriage payment" framing makes the arrangement appear benevolent. It is a bonded contract with a dowry wrapper.

ILO Estimate

40 Million Bonded Workers — World's Largest Such Population

India's bonded labour population is the world's largest. SC and ST communities make up the overwhelming majority. The Bonded Labour System (Abolition) Act 1976 has been in force for 48 years. The ILO estimate has not substantially decreased in that time. The number has been relatively stable because the Act abolished the legal infrastructure of bondage without abolishing the economic infrastructure — the debt relationships, the landlord power, and the absence of alternative livelihoods — that sustains it.

UP Brick Kilns — NHRC 2012

300,000 Bonded Workers in One State's One Industry

The National Human Rights Commission's 2012 investigation of UP brick kilns documented 300,000+ bonded workers in UP alone — advance-paid workers who could not leave the kiln before the season ended without repaying the advance, which was structured to be irrepayable. Kilns operate October–June. Workers are brought from Bihar, MP, and Odisha by recruiters who advance money to families. During the season: no freedom of movement, substandard food, no medical access. Children work alongside parents. The 2012 NHRC report was published. It produced zero prosecutions of kiln owners as of 2020.

Tamil Nadu Sumangali Scheme

Dowry Framing for a Bonded Labour Contract

Young women (predominantly Dalit, 14–25) recruited from rural Tamil Nadu, Andhra, Karnataka for 3-year contracts at garment/textile mills. Premise: lump sum "marriage dowry" payment at end of contract. Reality: locked hostels, no freedom of movement, wages paid partially in locked accounts, contact with family monitored. Anti-Slavery International, Human Rights Watch, and Tamil NGO reports document this system supplying garments to European and American brands. The lump sum at the end means workers cannot access their wages to leave. The "marriage payment" framing makes Western buyers comfortable. The workers remain.

The Enforcement Gap

Labour Inspectors Investigate the Landlord's Employee

Labour Inspectors are state government employees. In states where bonded labour is most prevalent — UP, Bihar, Rajasthan, MP — the state government's political constituency includes the landlord class. A Labour Inspector who diligently prosecutes bonded labour contractors is threatening the economic base of the ruling political party's major donors. Inspectors who do not prosecute face no consequences. Inspectors who do prosecute face transfers, harassment, and career difficulty. The enforcement system is staffed by people with structural incentives not to enforce. This is the design. It is not a malfunction.

The constitutional reservation system covers government employment — approximately 9–10% of India's total workforce. The private sector employs the remaining 90%+. It is explicitly and deliberately outside the reservation framework. The Supreme Court's PA Inamdar ruling (2005) confirmed that private unaided institutions have admissions autonomy. The BJP-led government's consistent position has been that private sector reservation is not constitutionally mandated and would be economically harmful. The result: the expanding, dynamic, high-wage sectors of the Indian economy — IT, finance, pharmaceuticals, media, professional services — are entirely exempt from any diversity requirement and in practice are overwhelmingly upper-caste in their composition.

The Thorat-Attewell experiment (2007) — the most rigorous empirical evidence of private sector caste discrimination in India. The design: identical CVs, different names. Upper-caste Hindu names (Sanjay Kumar Sharma, etc.), Dalit names (Sanjay Kumar Dhobi, etc.), Muslim names. Same qualifications. Sent to identical private sector job postings in Delhi NCR. Result: CVs with upper-caste Hindu names received callback rates 33% higher than CVs with Dalit names and 66% higher than CVs with Muslim names. The differences persisted when education level was controlled, when experience was controlled, when employer type was varied. Caste discrimination in private hiring is not anecdotal. It is measurable, consistent, and statistically significant. The paper was published in Economic and Political Weekly. It did not change government policy on private sector reservation. It was cited in academic papers. The discrimination continued.

The Indian tech industry — caste's most lucrative contemporary form. Equality Labs' 2018 survey of South Asian Americans: 67% of Dalit respondents experienced workplace caste discrimination, with the tech industry having the highest documented rates among industries. The mechanism: Indian-origin hiring managers who are disproportionately from upper-caste communities recruit through alumni networks (from IITs and NITs that themselves have near-zero SC/ST faculty and hostile environments for SC/ST students), social networks that are caste-stratified, and "culture fit" assessments that are proxies for caste-community membership. The result is that the Indian-origin tech workforce in the US — and in India — is heavily skewed toward upper-caste communities in ways that are not random and are not explained by qualification differentials. The Cisco case (California, 2020–2023) made this judicially visible for the first time.

Thorat-Attewell 2007

Identical CV, Different Name — 33% Fewer Callbacks for Dalit Names

Published: Economic and Political Weekly, 2007. Method: 548 applications to private sector jobs in Delhi NCR. Identical qualifications. Three name conditions: upper-caste Hindu, SC/Dalit, Muslim. Upper-caste callbacks: baseline. SC callbacks: 33% lower. Muslim callbacks: 66% lower. The caste discrimination in private hiring is not claimed — it is measured. No Indian government has since used this study to justify private sector reservation policy changes. The study is in academic literature. The discrimination is in the labour market.

Who Owns Indian Business

Forbes India Billionaires — Upper Caste Dominance

Multiple analyses of Forbes India's 100 richest individuals: upper-caste Hindus account for approximately 80–90% of named billionaires. Baniyas (merchant caste): ~25–30%. Brahmins: ~20–25%. Marwaris, Patels, Khatris: ~20–25%. SC/ST billionaires: approximately 1–2 individuals over any five-year period. The Indian capital class is not merely upper-caste — it is Brahmin-Bania dominated to a degree that exceeds even their already disproportionate education and professional representation. Capital, like land, is not just unequally distributed — it is almost entirely outside the reach of the communities whose labour has historically produced it.

Cisco Case 2020–2023

First Corporate Caste Discrimination Case in US History — Settled

California DFEH filed against Cisco in 2020: two upper-caste Indian managers systematically harassed, undermined, and professionally sabotaged a Dalit Indian engineer — denying him promotions, undermining his work, outing his caste to colleagues. Cisco settled in 2023. Settlement terms: caste sensitivity training, policy changes. The case established that caste discrimination is legally actionable in the US workplace. It opened the door. The Indian tech corridor — where upper-caste hiring, promotion, and social network advantages are documented — has not yet been systematically litigated. The Cisco case is the precedent.

Gig Economy

Zomato, Swiggy, Ola — Caste-Neutral Platform, Caste-Sorted Workforce

Platform gig economy presents itself as caste-neutral: the algorithm assigns jobs, not the human. Reality: delivery work, domestic work, and logistics — the lowest-paid gig categories — are disproportionately filled by SC/OBC workers. High-paying gig categories (freelance tech, consulting, design) are disproportionately filled by upper-caste workers. The sorting is not the algorithm's doing — it reflects pre-existing educational and social capital differentials. The "caste-neutral platform" inherits and reproduces the caste hierarchy of its user base. The algorithm is innocent. The inputs are not.

Every year in India, men die inside sewers. They descend into underground chambers to manually clear blockages, without protective equipment, without oxygen monitoring, without safety harnesses, and with the knowledge that the gases inside — hydrogen sulphide, methane, carbon dioxide — can kill within minutes. Between 2017 and 2022, at least 351 such deaths were officially recorded by the National Commission for Safai Karamcharis. Rights organisations estimate the actual figure is higher by a factor of 2–3, because many deaths are recorded as "accidents" without the sewer entry context being noted. Almost every one of these men is Dalit — specifically from the Valmiki/Balmiki community, which has been assigned sewage work as hereditary dharmic occupation.

The Employment of Manual Scavengers and Construction of Dry Latrines (Prohibition) Act was passed in 1993. It was strengthened in 2013 as the Prohibition of Employment as Manual Scavengers and their Rehabilitation Act 2013. Both laws are in force. Manual scavenging continues because the municipalities and private contractors that hire for this work face no meaningful prosecution, the workers have no alternative income, and the state rehabilitation programme — which is legally mandated to provide alternative livelihood training and a one-time payment — has disbursed its mandated support in a fraction of documented cases. The 2022 national survey by the Social Justice Ministry identified 58,098 manual scavengers. The government's previous official figure: zero, because the practice was "abolished" in 1993. These are not the same number. One of them is wrong. Both are government documents.

The sewer death pattern is precise: a municipal corporation receives a complaint about a blocked drain or sewer. A contractor is called. The contractor sends workers — Valmiki community men, who have no other available employment in the community, who were doing this work as children alongside their parents, who cannot access municipal sanitation workers' permanent employment because the permanent positions require educational qualifications they could not access. The workers descend without equipment. They die. The contractor pays a small settlement to avoid prosecution. The dead man's son begins doing the same work because the family needs income. The cycle repeats.

"My husband told me every time he went down: 'If I don't come back, tell the children I died for their food, not for their dignity. Tell them to get out of this work.' He didn't come back. His children are still doing the work. There is nothing else for them."
— Widow of sewer death victim, Delhi, testimony to NCSK 2021
NCSK Data 2017–2022

351 Official Sewer Deaths — Actual: 700–1,000+

National Commission for Safai Karamcharis: 351 sewer/septic tank deaths officially recorded 2017–2022. Safai Karmachari Andolan (Bezwada Wilson's organisation): estimates 2–3x higher based on ground documentation. Multiple deaths not recorded as sewer deaths when employers and police record the cause as "accident" without specifying the sewer entry. Almost all victims: Valmiki/Balmiki community men. Average age: 30–45. Almost all: fathers. Almost all: their sons are doing the same work.

Legal Status

Banned 1993, Banned Again 2013, Still Happening 2024

1993 Act. 2013 Act (with criminal provisions). Supreme Court order 2014 directing identification and rehabilitation of all manual scavengers. 2022 government survey: 58,098 active manual scavengers. Zero prosecutions of municipal corporations for employing them. The 2013 Act makes employment of manual scavengers a criminal offence for the employer. The only people prosecuted under the 2013 Act as of 2023: a small number of individual contractors. Municipal corporations — the largest employers of manual scavenging through contractor systems — have not been prosecuted anywhere in India.

Rehabilitation Scheme

Mandated Support Disbursed in ~20% of Cases

The 2013 Act mandates: ₹40,000 one-time payment, skill training, subsidised loans for alternative livelihood. Activist RTI data (2019–22): approximately 20% of identified manual scavengers have received the one-time payment. Skill training: even lower uptake, because training for "alternative livelihoods" in villages where the only available work for Valmiki community members is sanitation work does not produce alternative livelihoods. The scheme exists as legislation. It functions as documentation of good intent. The workers remain in the sewers.

Bezwada Wilson

Ramon Magsaysay 2016 — Fighting a 3,000-Year Job Description

Born into a manual scavenging family in Kurnool, AP. Founded Safai Karmachari Andolan in 1994. Work: RTI filings exposing municipal employers, survivor documentation, organising Valmiki families to refuse the work and demand rehabilitation. Government response: denial of scale, slow rehabilitation, refusal to prosecute municipalities. His 2016 Ramon Magsaysay Award speech: "The government of India celebrates our Constitution. The Constitution abolished untouchability. Manual scavenging is untouchability with a broom. Which of these two facts should we believe?"

The Dalit capitalism debate is the most consequential strategic disagreement within the Dalit rights movement. It concerns whether economic empowerment through entrepreneurship — building Dalit-owned businesses, accumulating Dalit capital, creating Dalit employment — can serve as a parallel or alternative path to the political and social liberation that Ambedkar's movement sought. The debate has been sharpened by India's economic liberalisation since 1991, by the emergence of a Dalit middle class through reservation, and by the specific frustration that reservation — which has produced Dalit civil servants, Dalit academics, and Dalit politicians — has produced no Dalit industrialists and almost no Dalit capital owners.

The case for Dalit capitalism — Milind Kamble and DICCI. Milind Kamble founded the Dalit Indian Chamber of Commerce and Industry (DICCI) in 2005, based in Pune. The argument: if upper-caste economic power was built through caste-based business networks — Marwari trading networks, Patel co-operative networks, Brahmin professional networks — then Dalit communities must build equivalent economic networks. Political power without economic power is fragile — it depends on the goodwill of the bureaucracy and the electoral cycle. Economic power is more durable. DICCI organises Dalit entrepreneurs, connects them with government procurement opportunities (where SC reservation exists), lobbies for private sector participation, and attempts to build the mutual-support networks that upper-caste business communities have always had. By 2022, DICCI claims a membership of 3,000+ Dalit entrepreneurs. Their most visible success: Rajesh Saraiya, a Dalit billionaire in the steel industry — one of fewer than five identifiable SC/ST billionaires in India.

The case against — Anand Teltumbde, Suraj Yengde. The critique operates on two levels. First, empirical: Dalit businesses face caste discrimination in exactly the markets where they need to operate — credit markets where banks and informal lenders are less likely to extend credit to SC borrowers (documented in multiple RBI-adjacent studies), supplier networks where upper-caste suppliers refuse or overcharge Dalit business owners, customer markets where caste-community brand loyalty disadvantages Dalit brands, and contract markets where Dalit businesses face exclusion from the informal networks through which most Indian business is actually conducted. A Dalit entrepreneur cannot simply "compete" in these markets as if caste were absent. Caste operates in every transaction. Second, structural: even if Dalit capitalism succeeds in producing a Dalit billionaire class, it leaves the 200 million Dalits who will never be entrepreneurs exactly where they are. Capitalism produces a few wealthy individuals from any community. It does not produce collective liberation. Ambedkar's critique was not of individual economic failure — it was of a system that converts collective labour into collective dispossession. Dalit capitalism addresses individual success within that system. It does not address the system.

"Dalit capitalism is not the answer to Brahminical capitalism. It is participation in a game whose rules were written to exclude you, using pieces that were designed for someone else. You may win occasionally. The game does not change."
— Suraj Yengde, Caste Matters, 2019
DICCI — Founded 2005

3,000+ Dalit Entrepreneurs — One Documented Billionaire

Dalit Indian Chamber of Commerce and Industry. Milind Kamble, Pune. Government procurement focus — where SC reservation provides some protection. 3,000+ members. Most successful member: Rajesh Saraiya (steel). Challenge: government procurement is ~10% of India's economy. The other 90% — private sector contracts, export markets, retail — has no reservation protection and full caste discrimination. DICCI is building within the protected zone. Outside it, the barriers are intact.

Credit Discrimination

SC Borrowers Pay Higher Rates, Get Less Credit — RBI Data

Multiple RBI-adjacent studies document that SC borrowers: receive smaller loan amounts relative to collateral compared to upper-caste borrowers; pay higher interest rates on informal credit (where the lender knows the borrower's caste); face more collateral demands for equivalent formal credit amounts; and are more likely to be rejected in discretionary lending decisions. The credit market that Dalit entrepreneurs need to grow their businesses is itself caste-discriminatory. This is the market that DICCI's members must navigate. The market is not neutral. It never was.

Gujarat Model — Patel vs Dalit Business

The Network Economy That Dalit Entrepreneurs Cannot Join

Patel (Patidar) businesses in Gujarat operate through dense intra-community networks: Patel suppliers give credit to Patel businesses on trust; Patel customers prefer Patel brands; Patel banks (co-operative, informal) finance Patel expansion at below-market rates; Patel political connections protect Patel interests. A Dalit entrepreneur in Gujarat cannot access any of these networks. He faces the full market rate on every input and the full market competition on every output. The Patel business operates with community subsidy. The Dalit business operates without it. This is not competition. It is a handicapped race called a fair race.

The Ambedkar Position

Economic Emancipation Requires Political Power First

Ambedkar's position, consistently held: economic emancipation for Dalits cannot precede political emancipation — it requires it. Without political power, economic gains by individual Dalits can be reversed by state violence, market discrimination, and social pressure. The history of prosperous Dalit communities being destroyed by upper-caste violence (Tsundur 1991: Dalit families who had accumulated land and education were massacred; Khairlanji 2006: a family that owned land and had educated children) confirms this. Economic success without political protection does not produce economic security. It produces a target.

→
⚔️
Counter-Arguments — Every Objection, Answered with Data
The 8 strongest arguments against Dalit rights. Steel-manned, then refuted.
Reference21
Full SectionNEW
▼

These are the eight arguments most commonly made against Dalit rights — in newspaper comment sections, in upper-caste dinner table conversations, in Supreme Court briefs, and in government policy documents. Each is stated here in its strongest form, the way a thoughtful person who believes it would state it. Then each is answered — not with emotion, but with data, logic, and the same intellectual rigour the argument demands. Read these if you came here with doubts. Read them especially if you came here already convinced — because these are the arguments you will need to answer.

⚔️
Argument 1
"Caste discrimination is illegal. The problem is being solved. Why keep talking about it?"
The Argument (Steel-Manned)

Article 17 of the Constitution abolished untouchability in 1950. The SC/ST Prevention of Atrocities Act (1989, strengthened 2018) criminalises caste violence with mandatory prosecution provisions. Reservation has been in place for 75 years. The legal infrastructure is comprehensive. Progress is slow but real — literacy rates, life expectancy, and political representation for SC communities have all improved. Continuing to describe caste as an unsolved crisis is either outdated or politically motivated to perpetuate division.

The Refutation

50,291 registered crimes against SC communities in 2022 — up 30.1% from 2015. 9.7% end-to-end conviction rate. 470,000+ cases pending trial. Mid-day meal segregation documented in 11 states in 2023. SC faculty at IITs: 3.8% against mandated 22.5%. SC/ST land ownership: 9% for 25% of population. The law exists. The data shows the law is not working. "Being solved" requires evidence of solving. The evidence points the other way. Describing an ongoing crisis as "being solved" is not optimism. It is a failure to read the data.

⚔️
Argument 2
"Reservation is reverse discrimination. It hurts meritocracy and punishes people for their ancestry."
The Argument (Steel-Manned)

Reservation allocates positions on the basis of birth — the exact criterion that caste itself uses. If caste discrimination is wrong because it judges people by birth, reservation is wrong for the same reason. The child of a Dalit civil servant should not receive the same reservation benefits as a child of a landless agricultural labourer. And a talented upper-caste candidate who narrowly misses a competitive examination because of a reserved seat has been punished for something their generation did not do.

The Refutation

Reservation does not punish upper-caste applicants for their ancestors' conduct. It corrects for advantages those applicants currently carry — the educational infrastructure built on exclusion, the land inherited from systems that denied SC communities land, the coaching access that IIT entrance selects for. The Thorat-Attewell experiment proves private hiring is already caste-discriminatory without reservation. "Meritocracy" in a caste-discriminatory system is not meritocracy — it is caste privilege with a neutral name. As for the "creamy layer" argument: the SC child of a civil servant still faces housing discrimination, social exclusion, and a 9.7% conviction rate for crimes against them. Wealth within a caste does not remove caste.

⚔️
Argument 3
"Dalit politicians play caste politics for votes. They keep their own communities backward to stay relevant."
The Argument (Steel-Manned)

Dalit political leaders — BSP, VCK, RPI — win elections by mobilising caste identity. If caste-based politics is wrong, it is wrong for everyone. And there is evidence that some Dalit political organisations have used welfare claims for political fundraising without delivering welfare outcomes. The solution to caste is integration, not further caste-based political mobilisation.

The Refutation

Upper-caste political formations have mobilised caste identity for their entire history — the Congress's Brahmin-Bania coalition, the BJP's upper-caste base, state Congress parties controlled by Reddy, Maratha, or Patidar communities. They are not described as "playing caste politics." Caste politics becomes a criticism only when the caste doing politics is Dalit. Furthermore: the political mobilisation of Dalit communities is what produced the 73rd Amendment, the strengthening of the SC/ST Act, and the political pressure that has partially implemented reservation. Without political pressure, the law on the books produces nothing. Mayawati governing UP produced more SC welfare implementation than 40 years of Congress government. The evidence does not support "keeping communities backward."

⚔️
Argument 4
"Poverty, not caste, is the real problem. Class-based policy would help Dalits more than caste-based policy."
The Argument (Steel-Manned)

Poor upper-caste families also struggle. Class-based poverty programmes — universal health coverage, employment guarantee schemes, subsidised education — would help all poor people including Dalits without reinforcing caste categories. Caste-based policy keeps caste visible and therefore keeps caste-based discrimination alive. A genuinely class-blind welfare state would be more effective and more equitable.

The Refutation

Controlling for income, SC communities have worse health outcomes, lower conviction rates for crimes against them, and higher discrimination in hiring than upper-caste communities at the same income level (NFHS-5 data; Thorat-Attewell). Caste discrimination is not reducible to class — a wealthy Dalit still faces housing discrimination, social exclusion, and violence. The class-blind solution treats the symptom (poverty) while leaving the cause (caste) intact. Moreover: "class-blind" welfare is not class-blind in implementation — government welfare schemes in UP and Bihar are documented to be implemented in caste-skewed ways even without explicit caste targeting. The solution to caste-biased implementation is not to remove caste targeting — it is to demand caste-disaggregated monitoring of implementation.

⚔️
Argument 5
"SC/ST Atrocities Act is misused. False cases are filed to harass upper-caste people and extort them."
The Argument (Steel-Manned)

The SC/ST Act has mandatory arrest provisions that can be misused. Multiple Supreme Court orders have noted concerns about misuse. If the Act is routinely misused against innocent upper-caste people, its credibility is damaged and genuine victims are harmed by the association with false cases. The 2018 Supreme Court dilution order (in Subhash Kashinath Mahajan) was based on this concern.

The Refutation

NCRB data: 9.7% end-to-end conviction rate for SC/ST Act cases. This is presented as evidence of misuse. It is evidence of the opposite: a system so biased against Dalit complainants that 90%+ of registered cases fail to produce a conviction — because of police hostility, evidence destruction, witness intimidation, and court delays. If the Act were being used to "harass" upper-caste people, we would expect high FIR rates and low conviction rates as genuinely innocent people are acquitted. This is exactly what we see — but the cause is not fabricated FIRs; it is the complete failure of prosecution. The 2018 Supreme Court dilution order prompted the largest Dalit protest in post-independence history — the April 2 Bharat Bandh — because Dalit communities understood that the "misuse" argument was being used to gut the one legal protection they had.

⚔️
Argument 6
"Comparing caste to race is a Western imposition. Caste is different — it's being misrepresented internationally."
The Argument (Steel-Manned)

Race and caste are different systems. Applying racial frameworks — developed in the US context of transatlantic slavery and Jim Crow — to India's caste system distorts both. The international campaign to classify caste as "descent-based discrimination" equivalent to racial discrimination imposes Western analytical categories on a complex South Asian social reality and gives foreign organisations leverage over Indian domestic affairs.

The Refutation

The comparison to race is not made by Western scholars imposing categories. It is made by Dalit scholars. Ambedkar himself, in his address to a 1946 conference of Negro Americans, explicitly compared his situation to theirs. The UN Special Rapporteur on descent-based discrimination's framework was developed with extensive input from Dalit organisations. The argument that comparing caste to race is "Western imposition" is made by upper-caste Indians who want to insulate caste from international scrutiny — not by Dalit communities who experience the system. The people who live inside the system are allowed to describe it using frameworks they find useful. The people who benefit from the system do not get to veto their choice of language.

⚔️
Argument 7
"Things are getting better. Young people don't care about caste. The market and urbanisation are dissolving it."
The Argument (Steel-Manned)

Urban young people from all castes work together, socialise together, and many marry inter-caste. Economic development is reducing the relevance of hereditary occupation. Education is reducing the relevance of caste identity in professional settings. The trend is clearly toward dissolution — slower than reformers want, but real. The crisis framing is outdated.

The Refutation

NCRB data 2015–2022: crimes against SCs rose 30.1% in the same period that India's urban young were supposedly dissolving caste. Matrimonial websites continue to list caste as a primary filter — and the market for them grows, not shrinks. IIT Bombay's own 2023 internal report documents systematic caste hostility among today's student body — the most educated young people in India. Equality Labs documents caste discrimination in Silicon Valley — the most economically integrated international professional environment. The "dissolution" narrative is based on the experience of upper-caste urban professionals whose social networks became more diverse. It is not based on the experience of the Dalit communities who continue to be murdered, segregated, and exploited. Whose "getting better" are we measuring?

⚔️
Argument 8
"Ambedkar is being misused. He was a constitutional patriot, not a radical revolutionary."
The Argument (Steel-Manned)

Ambedkar was a democrat and a constitutionalist who believed in parliamentary methods, rule of law, and national integration. Contemporary Ambedkarite activists who use his image for radical anti-state politics, separatist demands, or violent protest are misappropriating his legacy. The BJP has honoured Ambedkar — his statue stands in the Parliament complex. His legacy belongs to all Indians.

The Refutation

Ambedkar in Annihilation of Caste (1936): "I tell you very specifically, you must destroy the authority of the Shastras." In What Congress and Gandhi Have Done to the Untouchables (1945): a systematic, documented indictment of the Congress and its leadership. On the Constitution: "On January 26, 1950 we are going to enter into a life of contradictions... In politics we will have equality, in social and economic life we will have inequality." He described the constitution he wrote as a document that could be used against the nation if the nation did not change its social character. The BJP's installation of Ambedkar statues while opposing the SC/ST Act's enforcement, cutting Dalit welfare budgets, and governing in alliance with the caste-landowning class Ambedkar spent his life opposing — is not honouring his legacy. It is using his image to neutralise his politics. Ambedkar anticipated this. He called it "hero worship."

→

A Note on Data Methodology

All NCRB crime data cited is from the published Crime in India annual reports, available at ncrb.gov.in. Conviction rates are calculated from NCRB's own "trials completed" and "persons convicted" figures — the calculation is straightforward and verifiable. Estimates that exceed NCRB figures (e.g., actual rape vs registered rape at 10–20x) are from rights organisations (NCDHR, AIDMAM, HRW) who document the gap between reporting and occurrence. Population percentages are from Census 2011. Wealth data is from AIDIS 2019. Where specific states or numbers are cited without inline source, the source is documented in the category above. If you find a specific figure you cannot verify, email a correction — this archive is only useful if it is accurate.

Reservation Wars — 75 Years of a Contested Right
What reservation is, what it produced, who fights it and why, and what the data says about every claim made against it.
Resistance18
Full SectionNEW
▼

Reservation is the most debated policy in independent India. It is also the most lied about. The lies come from both sides. What the debate almost never contains is the actual data: what reservation has produced, where it has failed, what the alternatives proposed by its critics would actually deliver, and what the Constitution's framers understood they were doing. This section contains that data.

Mandated vs Actual — Central Govt Seniority
SC Representation by Rank — Where Power Lives
The gap widens as seniority increases. The building lets Dalits in. The rooms keep them out.
At Secretary level — the people who draft policy — SC representation is under 5% against a 15% mandate. Reservation gets Dalits into the building. The promotion system keeps them out of the rooms where decisions are made.
SC Reserved Posts Vacant — Ministry-Wise 2022
4.1 Lakh Vacancies — Reservation on Paper, Vacancy in Practice
Posts left unfilled for years rather than lowering qualification bars the system itself created.
Private vs Public — SC Representation 2019-20
Reservation Covers 10% of Jobs. The Other 90% Is This Chart.
SC in senior private sector roles: under 3%. Board level: near zero. No reservation. No remedy.
SC Literacy 1951-2011
What 60 Years of Educational Reservation Produced
10.3% → 66.1%. Fastest literacy growth of any social group. The gap remains. Both facts are true.

Reservation is not welfare. Not charity. Not affirmative action in the American sense. It is a constitutional right — the product of a specific historical argument by Ambedkar in the Constituent Assembly, accepted as the only mechanism to make formal equality meaningful in a society structured by three thousand years of deliberate exclusion.

Article 15(4): The state may make special provision for the advancement of any socially and educationally backward class or for SCs and STs. Article 16(4): The state may reserve appointments in favour of any backward class not adequately represented in services under the state. Article 46: The state shall promote with special care the educational and economic interests of SCs and STs and protect them from social injustice and all forms of exploitation.

Ambedkar's logic was explicit: equality of opportunity is meaningless when starting positions are radically unequal. If some runners begin a kilometre behind the starting line — held there by law for centuries — the formally open race produces substantive unfairness. Reservation does not create unequal treatment. It compensates for unequal treatment that has already occurred and continues to occur.

On time limits: Ambedkar expected social transformation sufficient to make reservation unnecessary within a generation. The first Constitutional review was in 1960. They extended it — because every decade of data showed the transformation had not occurred. The extension of reservation is not political opportunism. It is the response to data. When the data changes, the policy can change. The data has not changed.

"Give me control over the executive and I do not care for your laws. What good is law if those who implement it are themselves prejudiced against it?"
— B.R. Ambedkar, Constituent Assembly Debates, 1949
Poona Pact 1932 — The Original Compromise

Separate Electorates Traded for Reserved Seats — Under Duress

Ambedkar's original demand: separate electorates — constituencies where only Dalits vote, electing representatives accountable exclusively to Dalit communities. Gandhi fasted unto death against this. Ambedkar, knowing Gandhi's death would trigger anti-Dalit pogroms, signed the Poona Pact: reserved seats within joint electorates instead. This meant Dalit representatives must win upper-caste approval. Every criticism of "vote-bank politics" by Dalit politicians operates within this constrained space that Ambedkar was forced to accept at risk of his community's physical safety.

Constituent Assembly 1949

The Same Arguments Were Made Then. The Assembly Rejected Them.

Upper-caste Assembly members argued in 1949: reservation violates equality, it is temporary but will become permanent, it lowers efficiency, it rewards birth not merit. These are the same arguments made in 2024. The Assembly debated them for weeks and rejected them — because Ambedkar and others documented that formal equality without compensatory provision produces substantive inequality. The arguments have not become stronger in 75 years. The conditions that produced the original answer have not changed either.

Adequate Representation — The Test

SC Communities at 16.6% of Population. 5% of Group A Services. Test Satisfied.

Article 16(4) triggers when a backward class is "not adequately represented." SC: 16.6% of population. Group A civil services: ~5%. IIT faculty: 3.8%. Private sector senior roles: ~3%. Capital class: ~1%. By any definition of adequate representation, the Article 16(4) trigger is continuously and massively satisfied. The debate about whether reservation is still needed is answered by these numbers. The numbers are government data.

The honest accounting must include both what reservation has produced and where it has failed. Opponents talk only about the former framed as problems. Supporters talk only about the latter framed as progress. Both miss the full picture.

What reservation has demonstrably produced: A Dalit professional and middle class that would not exist without it. SC literacy: 10.3% (1951) → 66.1% (2011) — fastest literacy growth of any social group in independent India, directly correlated with educational reservation. A Dalit civil service presence that changed — partially, imperfectly — what the state delivers to SC communities in districts where SC officers hold relevant positions. Political representation: 84 reserved Lok Sabha seats producing Dalit politicians whose presence is now irreversible. Mayawati's four UP governments produced measurable welfare delivery gains for SC communities — documented in scheme uptake data and NCRB filing pattern changes in her tenure periods.

Where reservation has structurally failed: It covers government employment, which is shrinking as privatisation accelerates — from 70%+ of formal jobs in 1990 to under 20% today. It does not reach private sector, capital ownership, or informal employment — where 93% of India's workers, including the majority of Dalit workers, actually work. Even within covered sectors, implementation is so incomplete (4.1 lakh vacant posts) that the policy on paper and in practice are radically different.

The IIT outcome data — the most important number in the merit debate: Bertrand et al. (MIT, 2010) tracked IIT graduates across career trajectories comparing entry categories. Finding: 10–15 year income and professional achievement outcomes are statistically indistinguishable between general and reserved-category entrants, controlling for field and institution. The entrance exam score gap did not translate into a career performance gap. The exam measures preparation access. Talent distributes equally across castes. Access to preparation does not.

SC Literacy 1951-2011

10.3% → 66.1% — Fastest Growth of Any Social Group

The growth is real. The remaining gap is also real: 66.1% vs national average 74% in 2011, and the quality gap (learning outcomes, access to higher education) remains larger than the enrollment gap suggests. Both facts are the story — neither the "reservation worked" nor the "reservation failed" framing alone captures it.

Bertrand et al. — MIT 2010

Reserved-Seat IIT Graduates: Identical Career Outcomes to General Entrants

Study: IIT graduates 1994–2006, comparing career trajectories by entry category. Result: no statistically significant difference in 10–15 year income or professional achievement between general and reserved-category entrants. The entrance exam score gap — used to argue reserved entrants are "less qualified" — does not predict career performance. This is the data that the anti-reservation "merit" argument has to defeat. It has not defeated it.

Mayawati — 4 UP Governments

Political Power → Welfare Delivery. Its Loss → Reversal.

BSP-led UP governments: increased SC welfare budget allocation, faster implementation of land allocation, improved police responsiveness (documented in NCRB filing patterns), Ambedkar Village Scheme infrastructure. After BSP 2012 loss: multiple schemes wound down, SC welfare budgets reduced. The evidence: political power produces welfare delivery. Its absence reverses it. No abstract argument about "merit" can answer this documented relationship.

Privatisation — The Silent Reservation Killer

Each Privatisation Eliminates Reserved Posts Without a Parliamentary Vote on Reservation

Air India (sold 2021): thousands of reserved posts converted to private employment with zero reservation. LIC, BPCL, HPCL partial divestments: same. Since 1991, covered employment (government + PSU) shrank from 70%+ to under 20% of formal employment. Privatisation is the most effective anti-reservation policy ever implemented. It is never described that way. That framing choice is itself a political choice.

The anti-reservation movement has three streams: upper-caste student agitation (1990 Mandal protests, 2006 medical quota protests), judicial restriction (Supreme Court rulings narrowing scope since 1992), and the intellectual/media argument (journalists, economists, policy analysts framing reservation as inefficient and divisive). All three have upper-caste composition that is not coincidental.

The 1990 Mandal agitations. When VP Singh announced OBC reservation implementation, upper-caste students self-immolated across North India. Rajeev Goswami set himself on fire. 200+ upper-caste self-immolations. The media covered them as tragedy — sympathetically, with photographs and individual stories. In the same period, HRW and PUCL documented anti-Dalit violence in UP and Bihar — attacks on communities who stood to benefit from Mandal. The anti-Dalit violence received a fraction of the coverage of upper-caste self-immolations. The coverage asymmetry is documented and is a data point about who controls the narrative.

The efficiency argument. The claim that reservation reduces organisational efficiency rests on two assumptions: exam scores predict job performance, and the alternative is genuinely performance-based. Bertrand et al. (2010) refutes assumption 1. Thorat-Attewell (2007) refutes assumption 2 — private hiring without reservation already produces caste-based outcomes. The efficiency argument is not economic. It is caste-privilege defence in economic language.

Who controls the narrative. Oxfam-Newslaundry 2019: zero Dalit editors across 121 Indian media organisations. The reservation debate is narrated almost entirely by people who benefit from its absence in private sector employment. This does not make anti-reservation arguments automatically wrong. It explains why those arguments receive sophisticated elaboration and prominent placement while the data-based case for reservation is systematically underrepresented in the same outlets.

1990 — Mandal Protests

200+ Upper-Caste Self-Immolations. Anti-Dalit Violence Unreported.

August 1990: VP Singh announces Mandal implementation. Upper-caste student protests, Delhi to Patna. Rajeev Goswami self-immolation: front-page, weeks of sympathetic coverage. HRW/PUCL documentation of anti-Dalit attacks in same period: inside-page, law-and-order framing. 1990 anti-reservation protests produced India's first clear documentation of who the national media considers a sympathetic victim.

2006 — Medical Quota Protests

Doctors Struck. Patients Died. Media Sided With Doctors.

UPA proposes expanding OBC reservation in medical colleges. Resident doctors at AIIMS and major government hospitals strike for weeks. Patients died for lack of emergency care — documented with names. Media framing: students fighting for merit. Dalit and OBC communities demanding the reservation — the other side — received minimal sympathetic coverage. Doctors who withheld emergency care from patients to protest a policy they disagreed with were treated as civil society heroes. The deaths of their patients were treated as collateral.

Jat/Patel/Maratha Demands 2015-18

Anti-Reservation Communities Demand Reservation — The Contradiction Revealed

Jats (Haryana 2016), Patidars (Gujarat 2015), Marathas (Maharashtra 2017–18): dominant landowning communities — who led anti-reservation agitations in 1990 — demand OBC reservation for themselves. The contradiction: the anti-reservation movement was never about the principle of reservation. It was about who benefits from it. When reservation could benefit their own communities, the principled objection evaporated entirely.

Oxfam-Newslaundry 2019

0 Dalit Editors. 121 Organisations. The Narrator Problem.

Survey of editorial leadership in 121 Indian media organisations: 88% upper-caste Hindu. 0 Dalit. The people who frame the reservation debate in national media are almost entirely people who benefit from reservation's absence in their own professional sector. The data does not tell us what is true about reservation. It tells us why certain arguments about reservation dominate the public conversation regardless of their empirical merit.

The Mandal Commission (Backward Classes Commission, 1979–80, chaired by B.P. Mandal) is the most important and most vilified policy document in post-independence India. Its mandate: identify OBCs — non-SC, non-ST communities socially and educationally backward — and recommend measures for advancement. Key finding: OBCs constitute approximately 52% of India's population, underrepresented in government employment and higher education. Recommendation: 27% reservation for OBCs in central government services and higher education.

The report was submitted in 1980. Shelved for ten years — successive governments feared confronting OBC political power against fierce upper-caste resistance. VP Singh announced implementation in August 1990 — partly sincere, partly aimed at outflanking BJP's Ram Mandir mobilisation. The political calculation was correct. The social consequence was the worst upper-caste mobilised violence in North India since Partition-era riots in many cities.

Indra Sawhney v. Union of India (1992). 9-judge bench. Court upheld OBC reservation. Imposed 50% ceiling on total reservation. Introduced creamy layer exclusion for OBCs (not SC/ST). Held reservation cannot apply to promotions — only initial appointments. The Sawhney judgment simultaneously validates OBC reservation and constructs the architecture for restricting its growth. Every subsequent legal challenge to reservation works within and against this framework.

The caste census suppression. Last full caste enumeration: 1931 British census. Mandal's OBC estimate (52%) based on 1931 data plus projections. Government conducted Socio-Economic Caste Census (SECC) in 2011 but suppressed the caste data from public release. Bihar's own 2023 caste census: OBCs at 63% of Bihar's population — far higher than Mandal's 52% estimate. Suppressing caste data prevents accurate policy targeting and — not coincidentally — prevents accurate documentation of how severely OBCs are underrepresented. The missing data is a policy choice.

Mandal Commission Findings — 1980

52% of India Is OBC. Government Representation: Under 12%.

OBCs in central government employment: 12.5%. OBC population: ~52%. The gap is larger than the SC/ST gap at the time. OBCs had no constitutional protection equivalent to SC/ST. The Commission used 11 social, educational, and economic indicators to identify OBC communities. The core finding — massive underrepresentation — was never seriously challenged. The methodology was — but the methodology critics proposed no alternative methodology and no counter-data.

OBC Political Transformation

Lalu, Mulayam, Deve Gowda — Mandal Created Indian Coalition Politics

OBC political mobilisation after Mandal: Lalu Prasad Yadav's Bihar, Mulayam Singh's UP, Deve Gowda's Karnataka, and the emergence of regional OBC-identity parties changed Indian politics structurally and permanently. The 1996–2004 coalition governments were built on OBC political power that Mandal created. Upper-caste political forces have exploited SC/ST vs OBC divisions to counter this — dividing communities that share structural disadvantages. This division management is itself a caste-politics strategy.

Bihar Caste Census 2023

OBCs at 63% in Bihar. National SECC Data Suppressed Since 2011.

Bihar CM Nitish Kumar conducted Bihar's own caste census in 2023. Result: OBCs 63% of Bihar's population, backward castes 36%, upper castes 15.5%. The national SECC conducted in 2011 collected caste data for 840 million people — and the central government has refused to release the caste enumeration component for 14 years. The suppression prevents evidence-based OBC policy and — by coincidence — prevents documentation of exactly how large the reservation demand relative to current allocation actually is.

Indra Sawhney 1992 — The 50% Architecture

The Ceiling That Constrained SC/ST Was Breached for EWS. Same Court. Different Result.

The Sawhney 50% ceiling was applied as absolute principle for 27 years — preventing any expansion of SC/ST/OBC reservation beyond 49.5%. In 2019, the EWS amendment added 10% for upper castes, bringing total reservation to 59.5%. The same Court that spent 27 years enforcing the ceiling upheld its breach in 2022. The ceiling constrained the communities the Constitution intended to benefit. It did not constrain the addition for the communities it was designed to balance against.

The 103rd Constitutional Amendment (January 2019) introduced 10% reservation for "Economically Weaker Sections" — defined primarily as upper-caste Hindus with income below ₹8 lakh per year without SC/ST/OBC status. Passed in 48 hours by both Parliament houses. Upheld 3-2 by the Supreme Court in November 2022. It represents a fundamental departure from the constitutional basis of reservation.

The constitutional problem. The basis for SC/ST reservation is social discrimination — state-enforced exclusion from education, employment, and civic participation based on birth identity, over generations. EWS introduced reservation based purely on economic criteria — income below ₹8 lakh — without a finding of historical discrimination, social exclusion, or inadequate representation. The original framework said: reservation is justified when a group has been systematically excluded by the state over generations. EWS says: reservation is justified when a group is poor. These are different arguments. The second has no limiting principle.

The ₹8 lakh threshold reality. India's median household income: approximately ₹1.5–2 lakh per year. The ₹8 lakh EWS threshold covers the vast majority of upper-caste families — including those with significant land, ancestral property, and social capital who are not poor in any meaningful sense but whose declared income is below ₹8 lakh. An upper-caste family in UP that owns 10 acres of land but declares agricultural income of ₹3 lakh qualifies for EWS. A landless Dalit agricultural labourer with a similar income does not — because he is SC. Income threshold without a discrimination criterion selects for upper-caste underclass rather than genuine deprivation.

The 3-2 Supreme Court split. Janhit Abhiyan v. Union of India (November 2022). 3-judge majority upholds EWS. Justice Bhat (dissent, joined by Justice Trivedi): the amendment excludes SC/ST/OBC from EWS benefits while breaching the 50% ceiling that had constrained their reservation — violating the equality principle. The amendment uses economic criteria to benefit exactly the communities that the Constitution's reservation framework was designed to balance against. The dissent is constitutionally serious. The majority won. The dissent is constitutional history.

103rd Amendment — 48 Hours

No Standing Committee. No Debate. Constitutional Change Overnight.

Introduced Lok Sabha: 8 January 2019. Passed Lok Sabha: 8 January. Passed Rajya Sabha: 9 January. Presidential assent: 12 January. Four months before the 2019 general election. Constitutional amendments normally require standing committee review, multi-round debate, structural consistency examination. This one received none. The speed of passage is its own statement: electoral positioning, not constitutional deliberation.

The Exclusion Within the Inclusion

SC/ST/OBC Explicitly Excluded From EWS — Despite Overlapping Poverty

SC/ST/OBC communities that are below ₹8 lakh income — i.e., economically weak — are explicitly excluded from EWS reservation. They already have their own reservation, is the justification. But the logic means: upper-caste poor get a new 10% category above the 50% cap. SC/ST/OBC poor get no expansion of their existing category despite the same economic criterion. The amendment is not poverty reservation. It is upper-caste reservation justified with poverty language.

EWS Implementation 2019-2023

Low Uptake. High Symbol Value. Seats Often Unfilled.

EWS reservation uptake across central institutions: lower than the 10% quota in most cases — because income documentation requirements (income certificate below ₹8 lakh) require bureaucratic access that genuinely poor families lack, and because seats remain unfilled even as SC/ST reserved seats face demand backlogs. The policy has high political value as a gesture and low implementation reach as a welfare measure. Its primary function is electoral. Its secondary function is constitutional precedent: reservation can be designed for upper-caste communities.

The Double Standard — Documented

50% Cap: Absolute for SC/ST. Breachable for Upper Castes.

Indra Sawhney 1992: 50% ceiling is constitutionally required to prevent reservation from becoming the rule not the exception. Applied strictly for 27 years against any SC/ST/OBC expansion. EWS 2019: ceiling breached for upper castes. Supreme Court 2022: upheld. The ceiling that constrained the communities the Constitution intended to benefit did not constrain the addition for the communities it was meant to balance against. This is the documented record. It is not a characterisation. It is the sequence of events.

Three internal debates within the reservation framework matter more than most of the external debate: reservation in promotions, creamy layer for SC/ST, and sub-categorisation within SC communities. Each is genuinely complex. Each is also used in ways that are not always in good faith.

Promotions. Indra Sawhney (1992) held reservation cannot apply to promotions — only initial appointments. Result: SC/ST employees hired through reservation face an unprotected seniority system at every promotion level where evaluating officers are predominantly upper-caste. Parliament amended the Constitution four times (77th, 81st, 82nd, 85th amendments) to restore promotion reservation. Courts struck down or limited these amendments repeatedly. M. Nagaraj (2006), Jarnail Singh (2018), B.K. Pavitra (2019) produced a complex, state-by-state, court-order-by-court-order patchwork. SC/ST employees enter through reserved appointments and hit a glass ceiling at each promotion stage where reservation does not apply.

Creamy layer for SC/ST. The creamy layer exclusion applies to OBC reservation (since Sawhney 1992) but not to SC/ST. Courts have repeatedly been asked to apply it to SC/ST. The argument: affluent SC families should not benefit when poorer SC families need it more. The counter-argument: SC/ST reservation is based on caste discrimination, not poverty — a wealthy SC family continues to face housing discrimination, social exclusion, and violence at rates the NCRB documents. The 2024 sub-categorisation ruling by a 7-judge bench (overturning E.V. Chinnaiah 2004) allows states to allocate SC quota shares among SC sub-communities — partially addressing this concern without applying the creamy layer to SC as a whole.

Sub-categorisation. The SC list contains 1,200+ communities with vastly different current positions. In many states, one or two communities (Mahars in Maharashtra, Chamars/Jatavs in UP) dominate SC reservation utilisation while smaller sub-groups (Valmikis in UP, Madigas in Andhra) receive far less. The 2024 Supreme Court ruling — one of the most significant SC rights decisions in recent years — is almost entirely unreported in mainstream media. The Madiga Reservation Porata Samithi, which has agitated for sub-categorisation since 1994, won their 30-year case. The victory was not news.

The Promotion Glass Ceiling

Enter Through Reservation. Hit the Ceiling at Every Promotion.

SC employee enters Group C through reservation. Promotion to Group B: evaluated by a predominantly upper-caste board. Promotion to Group A: same. Senior Group A positions: same. At each stage, reservation does not apply to the promotion decision. SC employees are hired at entry level in mandated proportion and then promote at far lower rates than upper-caste peers. The glass ceiling is invisible in hiring data and visible only in seniority data — which is rarely reported by the media that does report on reservation extensively.

12 Years of Legal Confusion — M. Nagaraj to Jarnail Singh

Parliament Adds Promotion Reservation. Court Strikes Down. Repeat.

Parliament: 77th Amendment (1995). Court: upholds but requires states to demonstrate "backwardness" with quantifiable data — creating a data-collection burden that has tied up cases ever since. Jarnail Singh (2018): removes "backwardness" requirement, retains "inadequacy" requirement. States produce data. Courts dispute methodology. Cases accumulate. Promotion reservation remains legally unstable 30 years after Parliament first tried to establish it. The legal uncertainty is itself an implementation barrier — government departments use it as grounds for not implementing promotion reservation while the legal question is "pending."

Madiga Reservation Porata Samithi

30-Year Agitation for Sub-Categorisation. Won in 2024. Unreported.

Andhra Pradesh/Telangana: the SC quota is dominated by Mala and Madiga communities, with Malas historically better placed to access educational and government positions. MRPS has agitated since 1994 for Madiga-specific allocation within SC quota. The August 2024 Supreme Court 7-judge bench ruled states can sub-categorise. This is 30 years of political struggle producing a constitutional victory that affects millions of people. It received inside-page or no coverage in the major outlets that have published hundreds of articles on reservation.

The "Creamy Layer" as Weapon

Used to Exclude the Successful Rather Than Protect the Vulnerable

The creamy layer principle — reservation should reach those who most need it — is reasonable in theory. Its application in anti-reservation advocacy: argue for creamy layer exclusion for SC/ST to exclude the most successful SC/ST people from reservation, reducing coverage, without addressing why SC/ST people at every income level still face caste discrimination. The principle is used selectively: never applied to argue that upper-caste communities who benefit from inherited privilege should have their benefits reduced as their privilege increases. Selectively applied principles are not principles. They are preferences.

The most common anti-reservation argument ends with a proposal: instead of reservation, implement better schools, better coaching, better opportunity at entry level so competition is genuinely fair. This is the "road not taken" argument — the claim that a better alternative exists. It sounds most reasonable and collapses most completely when you look at what happened when it was tried.

The Pre-Matric Scholarship — the alternative tried first. Pre-matric scholarship for SC students has been implemented since the 1950s — 70+ years. Result: SC secondary dropout rate is still 17.5% against 10.2% for general category (UDISE+ 2022–23). SC children with scholarships are still beaten for touching the water vessel. The scholarship scheme addresses whether SC children have money to attend school. It does not address why they leave. Money is not the primary barrier.

The Navodaya Vidyalaya model — what actually reduces caste's educational effects. JNVs: residential central government schools in rural areas with SC/ST quotas. Students who attend JNVs show markedly lower caste-discrimination effects in educational outcomes. The residential environment removes the daily caste hierarchy of the village school. There are 649 JNVs serving approximately 240,000 students. India has 1.5 million schools. The alternative that demonstrably works has been implemented at 0.04% of scale. Expanding it would cost money. The government that cut Post-Matric Scholarship disbursements in UP, Bihar, MP, and Rajasthan has not expanded JNVs to scale.

The Kerala model. Kerala has the highest SC literacy rate (83.7%), lowest caste-based crime rate (18.2 per lakh SC population), and most functional reservation implementation of any large state. Kerala also has strong reservation implementation AND strong public education investment. The "either/or" framing — social investment instead of reservation — is refuted by the state with the best outcomes, which did both. Not either.

"You cannot ask a drowning man to wait for swimming lessons. The better alternative — equal starting conditions — requires social revolution. Social revolution requires political power. Political power requires representation. Representation requires reservation."
— B.R. Ambedkar, paraphrased from Constituent Assembly Debates, 1949
70 Years of Scholarship Alternative

Tried Since the 1950s. SC Dropout Still 17.5%. Reservation Still Necessary.

The "scholarship instead of reservation" argument has been implemented as policy for 70 years. After 70 years: SC secondary dropout 17.5% vs 10.2% general. SC IIT faculty 3.8% vs mandated 22.5%. SC Group A civil service ~5% vs mandated 15%. The alternative has run for 70 years. Its proponents' response: it needs more funding and better implementation. They are right. They must also acknowledge that after 70 years, it has not produced adequate representation without reservation. The "better alternative" has been tried. This archive contains the results.

Privatisation — The Alternative That Ran Since 1991

33 Years of Market Alternative. SC in Senior Private Roles: Under 3%.

The market/privatisation alternative to reservation has operated since 1991. IT, financial services, pharmaceuticals, media — the fastest-growing sectors — have zero reservation. After 33 years of private sector growth without reservation: SC representation in senior private roles under 3%. Board level: near zero. The 33-year experiment with the market alternative is complete. Its results are documented. They do not support the alternative.

Kerala — Both/And, Not Either/Or

Best SC Outcomes in India. Strong Reservation + Strong Public Investment.

Kerala: SC literacy 83.7%. SC crime rate 18.2 per lakh (national average ~30). Most functional reservation implementation. Highest SC professional representation of any large state. Also: strongest public education system, highest teacher presence, best health infrastructure for SC communities. The state with the best outcomes did both reservation and public investment. Not one instead of the other. The "either/or" is falsified by the state that refused to accept it.

The Honest Conclusion

No Alternative Has Been Proposed That Works at Scale. Reservation Remains Least-Bad.

Ambedkar was correct: the "better alternative" requires social revolution — genuine transformation of the caste ideology that produces the exclusion reservation is correcting. That revolution has not happened in 75 years of independence, 3,000 years of caste, and despite constitutional abolition of untouchability. In its absence, reservation is not the ideal policy. It is the only policy that produces measurable, documented reduction in exclusion — imperfectly, incompletely, with real costs — in the real India that exists. Not the India where social revolution has already succeeded.

Religion & Bhakti

Radical Theology, Stolen Legacy

The saints who preached caste abolition — and how Brahminical institutions absorbed them after death, placed their images in temples their living descendants could not enter, and made them safe.
+

Bhakti movement span (1200–1800 CE)

Ambedkar's Nagpur conversion — 600K+ on Day One
7.95M
Buddhists in India, 2011 census (largely Dalit)
