The Structural Argument

Why the Saints Were Revolutionary — and Why That Gets Erased

The Bhakti saints were not simply devout. They were radical. They argued that God is accessible to any human being without the mediation of priests, temples, rituals, Sanskrit, or caste identity. This is not a minor theological point — it is a direct destruction of the entire Brahminical economic and political order, which rested on the claim that only Brahmin priests could access and transmit divine power. The saints sang in vernacular languages — Marathi, Tamil, Kannada, Braj Bhasha — rather than Sanskrit. They were illiterate leatherworkers, weavers, barbers, and potters writing theology that Brahmin scholars could not match. The upper-caste response during their lifetimes: persecution, mockery, legal harassment, physical violence. The upper-caste response after their deaths: canonisation with the radical content removed.

c. 1300–1350 CE · Maharashtra · Mahar (Untouchable)

Chokhamela — The First Dalit Poet

Chokhamela is the earliest documented Dalit poet in any Indian language. He was a Mahar — the community that would later produce Ambedkar — and he wrote directly, in raw Marathi, to the god Vitthal at Pandharpur: "Why, O God, did you create me untouchable? What sin did I commit before my birth?" He was not permitted inside the Pandharpur temple to the god he addressed these verses to. He died in an accident performing veth (forced unpaid labour on a state project). When his bones were being cleared from the site, his followers claimed the bones themselves called out his name — a miracle. His bones were subsequently carried inside the temple. The structure is exact: Dalit body enters temple only after death, only after miraculous transformation, only after upper-caste mediation. Not while breathing, not as a person, not with dignity.

His wife Soyarabai and sister Nirmala also wrote abhangas (devotional poems). A family of Dalit poets writing the most radical theology of 14th-century Maharashtra. Their complete works are now part of the Varkari canon — managed by upper-caste institutions who did not permit the community these works came from to enter the temple complex for 600 years after.

c. 1440–1518 CE · Varanasi · Julaha Muslim Weaver

Kabir — The Heretic Both Sides Claimed

Kabir was born into a Muslim weaver family — Julaha caste, considered low in both Hindu and Muslim hierarchies. He is the most subversive religious writer in Indian literature because he attacked both Brahminism and Islamic orthodoxy simultaneously: "There is no Hindu and no Muslim in the sight of God. The one who searches for God in temples and mosques has not found God in themselves." He called out Brahmins who memorised Sanskrit scriptures they did not understand. He called out maulvis who performed rituals rather than seeking truth. He called out pilgrimage, fasting, caste pride, and religious performance of every kind. He was probably illiterate. His verses were oral and were written down by disciples.

After his death, both Hindus and Muslims claimed him. Brahmin hagiographers produced biographies arguing he was actually a Brahmin child abandoned and found by a weaver family — erasing his lower-caste birth to make his genius "legitimate" within the caste order. The Kabir Panth today is controlled in most regions by upper-caste Hindu leadership. His radical political theology has been reduced to accessible spiritual aphorism printed on inspirational posters.

c. 1450–1520 CE · Varanasi · Chamar (Cobbler/Leatherworker)

Ravidas — Begampura: The City Without Caste

Ravidas was a cobbler — a Chamar, one of the most stigmatised occupational castes in North India, considered ritually impure for handling dead animal hides. He wrote theology that stripped divinity from all ritual and located it exclusively in human dignity: "The city of God has no sorrow, no tax, no fear, no degradation, no second-class citizen." His Begampura — the city without sorrow — is the earliest utopian text in any Indian language that explicitly makes caste abolition the condition for the good society. His poetry was included in the Guru Granth Sahib by Guru Arjun Dev, making Ravidas the only untouchable whose theology is part of a world religion's scripture.

The Ravidassia community — built around his legacy, primarily Dalit Sikh Chamars — grew into a parallel religious structure of millions across Punjab and the diaspora when they were made unwelcome in Jat-dominated gurdwaras. In 2009, Ravidass leader Sant Niranjan Dass was shot dead in Vienna by Sikh extremists. Three died in subsequent Punjab riots. The Ravidassia community formally separated from Sikhism as a distinct religion. The man excluded from one religion's mainstream because of his caste ended up founding a separate religion centuries after his death.

1598–1650 CE · Maharashtra · Kunbi-Shudra

Tukaram — The Manuscript They Demanded He Drown

Tukaram was from the Kunbi community — a Shudra agricultural caste — which Brahmin priests in 17th-century Maharashtra considered below the threshold of eligibility for composing religious poetry. When Tukaram began composing abhangas in Marathi and attracting followers, the local Brahmin authorities told him directly: you are Shudra and have no right to create sacred poetry in a Brahmin language. They ordered him to throw his handwritten manuscript into the Indrayani river. He complied. The manuscript floated to the surface after 13 days — a miracle, according to his followers. This story is almost universally told in Maharashtra as a spiritual tale about divine confirmation of Tukaram's worthiness. It is rarely told as what it also was: a caste power structure demanding that a lower-caste man destroy his own creative work, and his humiliating compliance until the supernatural confirmed what caste law denied.

c. 1150 CE · Karnataka · Brahmin Woman (Renounced Caste)

Akkamahadevi — Rejection of All Authority

Akkamahadevi was a Brahmin woman — which places her in a different position than the Dalit saints, but her vachanas (prose-poetry) were among the most radical critiques of all social authority in 12th-century Karnataka, including caste. She rejected marriage, family, property, clothing, and all social expectation. She wandered naked, writing to Chennamallikarjuna (Shiva) about a love that made all social categories meaningless. The Lingayat Veerashaiva movement she was part of explicitly rejected caste — Basavanna, its founder, accepted disciples from all castes including untouchables. The movement lasted one generation before the Brahminical order reasserted itself. Akkamahadevi herself was interrogated by male religious authorities who questioned her right to spiritual knowledge. Her vachanas are taught in Karnataka schools. The radical social content — including the legitimacy of caste abolition and women's authority over their own religious practice — is rarely the focus.

c. 1498–1546 CE · Rajasthan · Kshatriya Woman (Royal)

Mirabai — Royalty Who Chose Humiliation Over Caste

Mirabai was a Rajput royal who is not a Dalit story — but her devotional life was a direct rejection of caste propriety. She danced publicly with commoners, accepted food from an untouchable saint (Raidas/Ravidas himself, according to hagiography), and refused the authority of her royal household to control her religious practice. She was, by her own lineage, untouched by untouchability — but her closeness to Ravidas and her rejection of social hierarchy were understood as pollution by her family. Her husband's family reportedly attempted to poison her multiple times. She is now worshipped. Her story is marketed as love-devotion. The political content — a woman of high caste explicitly choosing to cross caste lines, accepting a Dalit saint as her guru — is consistently softened in the popular telling.

South India — The Tamil Bhakti Saints

Nandanar, Tiruppanalvar, Thiruvalluvar — The Untouchable Theologians of the South

The Nayanmars (Shiva devotees) and Alvars (Vishnu devotees) of Tamil Nadu (c. 600–900 CE) included several poets from low and untouchable castes. Nandanar — a Pulaya (untouchable) — wished to see Shiva at Chidambaram temple. The path was blocked by caste. In the most widely circulated version of his story, he walks through fire and emerges purified as a Brahmin, then enters the temple. The same structure as Chokhamela: the only way a Dalit body is allowed inside a temple is after miraculous transformation into something that is no longer Dalit. The original radical request — let me in as I am — is resolved not by justice but by metamorphosis. Tiruppanalvar — also from an untouchable community — is one of the twelve Alvars whose verses became the Nalayira Divya Prabandham, the Vaishnava canon. He died outside the temple at Srirangam, carried inside only when a Brahmin priest received a divine vision commanding it. Thiruvalluvar's Tirukkural — 133 chapters of ethical philosophy, possibly the greatest ethical text in any Indian language — says nothing that supports caste. His caste is disputed, but most scholarly and community traditions identify him as low-caste or outcaste. The Tirukkural is mandatory curriculum across Tamil Nadu. Caste is never discussed in that context.

The Architecture of Exclusion

Temples as Caste Property — The Political Economy of Sacred Space

Hindu temples in pre-colonial and colonial India were not simply places of worship. They were the central economic institutions of their localities — controlling vast landholdings (temple trusts in Tamil Nadu today still hold land worth hundreds of crores), employing entire communities in ritual roles distributed exactly along caste lines, and distributing prasad, employment, and social legitimacy in a hierarchy that reproduced caste at every ceremony. Untouchable exclusion from temples was therefore not merely spiritual insult — it was economic exclusion, political exclusion, and social death simultaneously. The Temple Entry Satyagrahas of the 20th century were not polite requests for inclusion. They were assaults on the political economy of caste.

Key Temple Entry Movements — Timeline & State
1924–1925 · Kerala

Vaikom Satyagraha — Gandhi's Compromise, Periyar's Confrontation

The Vaikom Satyagraha was launched in March 1924 to demand the right of lower-caste Hindus to use roads surrounding the Vaikom Mahadeva temple in Travancore, which were restricted. Gandhi, who visited and led negotiations, ultimately accepted a compromise: lower castes could use some roads, not others. The temple itself remained closed to untouchables. Periyar, who participated in the satyagraha as a Tamil activist, was disgusted by Gandhi's settlement and broke openly with the Gandhian approach to caste. His view: Gandhi consistently prioritised Hindu unity over Dalit rights whenever the two conflicted, and the Vaikom outcome proved it. The roads were "opened" — the temple remained closed, the untouchable bodies remained outside the sacred boundary, and Gandhi declared victory.

1930 · Nashik, Maharashtra

Kalaram Temple Satyagraha — Ambedkar's March, Police Baton Charge

On March 2, 1930 — the same day as Gandhi's Salt March — Ambedkar led approximately 15,000 Dalits in a procession to the Kalaram temple in Nashik to demand the right of entry. The temple had been built on a plot that was partly former Mahar (Dalit) land. The procession was blocked by police and by upper-caste crowds. Over six years of continued satyagraha (1930–1935), protesters were beaten, imprisoned, and subjected to economic boycott by the dominant castes. The temple did not open. In 1935, Ambedkar publicly announced that he had been born a Hindu but would not die a Hindu — the first articulation of his eventual conversion twenty-one years later. The Kalaram Satyagraha failure convinced him that caste could not be reformed from within Hinduism.

1931–1932 · Guruvayur, Kerala

Guruvayur Satyagraha — Gandhi's Fast, the Temple Stays Closed

The Guruvayur temple satyagraha was launched by K. Kelappan in 1931 demanding entry of lower castes into the Guruvayur Krishna temple, one of Kerala's most important Hindu shrines. Gandhi undertook a fast-unto-death in support in 1932. A referendum was agreed — on the question of whether local upper-caste Hindus supported temple entry. The referendum was manipulated and the results disputed. The temple remained closed to untouchables. Gandhi ended his fast. The Guruvayur temple did not officially open to all Hindus until the Travancore Temple Entry Proclamation of 1936 — a royal proclamation by the Travancore government, not a victory of satyagraha.

1936 · Travancore, Kerala

Temple Entry Proclamation — The Exception That Proves the Rule

The Maharaja of Travancore issued a proclamation in November 1936 opening all state-administered temples to all Hindus regardless of caste. It was a genuine royal concession, driven partly by Christian missionary pressure (conversions were accelerating as Dalits in Travancore converted to escape caste discrimination) and partly by the organised satyagraha movement. It is often cited as a success story. It was, comparatively, real. But: the proclamation applied only to Travancore. Temple entry remained restricted in most of India. And the proclamation applied only to entry — the caste structure of temple employment, trust management, ritual roles, and economic benefit remained untouched. You could now walk through the door. The priests were still Brahmin. The land was still theirs.

2016–Present · Shani Shingnapur & Haji Ali, Maharashtra

Women's Entry — Caste Meets Gender in Contemporary Temple Politics

In 2016, the Bombay High Court ruled that women must be allowed entry to the Shani Shingnapur sanctum sanctorum (inner shrine), from which they had been excluded for centuries. The BJP-controlled state government initially opposed the ruling. The Haji Ali Dargah trust in Mumbai simultaneously defended their ban on women entering the inner shrine — a ban that had been introduced in 2012, reversing earlier practice, explicitly citing Shariat. Both cases were fought and won by women's activists. The parallel matters: Hindu temple exclusion of women and Dalit men runs on the same logic — ritual purity, bodily hierarchy, the claim that some bodies contaminate sacred space. The 2016 victories were real. They were also limited: the legal victories did not change the caste employment structure of temples, and Dalit exclusion from priestly roles remains total.

1927 · Mahad, Maharashtra

Mahad Satyagraha — Water First, Manusmriti Burned

The Mahad Satyagraha of March 1927 was Ambedkar's first major public action. The target: a public water tank (the Chavadar tank) in Mahad, which the Bombay legislature had formally opened to all castes in 1924 — but which the upper-caste community had never allowed Dalits to access. Ambedkar led approximately 3,000 people to the tank to drink. The procession was attacked by upper-caste crowds on the return. A rumour that Dalits had entered the local temple (they had not) triggered a mob that attacked the Dalit camp. In December 1927, Ambedkar returned to Mahad for a second conference — and publicly burned the Manusmriti. This was the symbolic declaration: the document that provided religious sanction for untouchability must be destroyed. The Manusmriti burning remains one of the most contested acts in Indian religious-political history. It is annually commemorated by Ambedkarite communities and annually condemned by Hindu nationalist organisations.

The Mechanism

How Brahminical Institutions Absorb and Defuse Radical Theology

The pattern is consistent across six centuries and multiple regions. A lower-caste or untouchable saint produces radical anti-caste theology. They are ignored, persecuted, or marginalised during their lifetime. After death, when the theology has proven its popular appeal, upper-caste institutions absorb it — through a four-stage process: (1) Canonisation: the saint's works are included in official religious collections. (2) Hagiography: biographies are written that emphasise spiritual devotion and remove or reinterpret political content. (3) Institutionalisation: temples, trusts, and commemorative structures managed by upper-caste authorities are built around the saint's memory. (4) Sanitisation: the descendants of the saint's original community continue to face the exact discrimination the saint's theology attacked, but now the theology has been neutralised as a challenge because it exists safely inside the canonical tradition.

The Kabir Case Study

From Revolutionary Heretic to Inspirational Poster

Kabir's original dohas attacked: Brahmin hypocrisy directly and by name, the pretence of ritual purity, the caste pride of the learned, the stupidity of idol worship, the claim that birth determines spiritual worth. These are not gentle critiques. They are demolitions. The upper-caste response during his lifetime was marginalisation. After his death, the co-optation was systematic. Stage 1 — Brahmin hagiographers produced the story that Kabir was actually a Brahmin infant, son of a widow, set adrift on a lake, found by a Julaha weaver family. This biography erases his lower-caste birth by making it accidental rather than natal. Stage 2 — Kabir Panth institutions were established by upper-caste followers in most regions, marginalising the Julaha weaver community whose member produced this theology. Stage 3 — Kabir's verses are now on government buildings, in school syllabi, in motivational content across social media — stripped of their specific anti-caste target and repackaged as universal wisdom about "true devotion." Stage 4 — Julaha weavers in Uttar Pradesh continue to face caste discrimination. The man who wrote the demolition of caste hierarchy from within that community has become an icon of Hindu spirituality.

The Tukaram Case Study

How the Warkari Canon Was Upper-Caste-Controlled

The Warkari pilgrimage tradition — the biannual walks to Pandharpur that are Maharashtra's most popular Hindu movement, involving millions of people — was built on the poetry of saints including Chokhamela (Mahar/untouchable), Tukaram (Shudra), Janabai (woman domestic servant), and Eknath (Brahmin who explicitly ate with untouchables and was socially ostracised for it). The radical social content — the inclusion of untouchable voices, the critiques of caste, the declaration that God was accessible to any person — was the movement's original basis. Management of the Warkari tradition — including the Pandharpur temple trust, the publication of the saints' poetry, the conduct of the pilgrimage — passed over time into Brahmin institutional control. The Mahar saint Chokhamela's body is buried outside the Pandharpur temple wall, in a position that replaces his exclusion from the living temple with a permanent marker of his exclusion. His descendants were not permitted inside the temple until the 20th century. His image is inside it.

The Living Example

The Varkari Pilgrims Who Cannot Eat With Each Other

The Warkari pilgrimage in 2023 attracted an estimated 2.5 million participants walking to Pandharpur. Researchers studying the pilgrimage — including teams from Pune University — have documented that caste segregation in eating and sleeping arrangements among pilgrims persists despite the tradition's explicit anti-caste founding theology. Dalit Warkari pilgrims in multiple documented cases set up separate camps because upper-caste pilgrims refused to eat communally with them. They are walking together to worship at a temple built partly on the theology of Chokhamela — who could not enter it — in separate groups that do not share meals. The co-optation is not ancient history. It is the pilgrimage happening this year.

Ambedkar's Analysis

Why Reform Cannot Come From Within — The Annihilation of Caste Argument

Ambedkar's fundamental argument — made most completely in Annihilation of Caste (1936) — was that the co-optation of reform movements was not accidental. Caste is not a social practice maintained by ignorance or prejudice that can be overcome by education and dialogue. It is a structure given divine sanction by religious texts — specifically the Brahminical scriptures. As long as those texts are considered authoritative, caste reform is impossible from within the tradition, because the tradition's core documents command the hierarchy. The Bhakti movement had proven this: radical anti-caste theology from within the tradition produced six centuries of poetry, then was absorbed and neutralised without changing a single element of the caste structure. His conclusion: "You cannot have political reform, you cannot have economic reform, unless you kill this monster" — and the monster is not practised by ignorant people who do not know better. It is sanctioned by scripture that educated Hindus consider sacred.

The Conversion Question

Exit from Hinduism — and Caste's Follow Through

Dalit communities have converted to Islam, Christianity, Sikhism, and Buddhism across Indian history — primarily as a strategy of exit from the caste system. The pattern that follows each conversion is consistent: the converted community carries their occupational caste identity with them. The new religion's community recognises them as converts from low castes and replicates caste discrimination within its own structures. The exit from Hinduism does not equal exit from caste. What it does produce, in many cases, is real improvement: access to education through mission schools (for Christian converts), protection of a community identity that resists upper-caste landlord violence (for Muslim converts in some regions), political organisation (for Buddhist converts post-Ambedkar). The improvement is real and documented. Caste abolition within the new religion: not achieved.

Buddhist Population Growth in India — Census Data 1951–2011
Near-zero pre-1956 reflects no organised Ambedkarite Buddhist community. 1961 spike = Nagpur conversion movement. Growth to 7.95M by 2011. Significant undercount likely as many Navayana Buddhists are also counted as SC Hindu.
Christianity — The Mission School Legacy

The Largest Conversion Movement Before Ambedkar

Christian missionary activity in India from the 18th century onward was the first large-scale exit route for Dalit communities. Mission schools provided literacy to communities that were legally forbidden from education under traditional caste arrangements. Mission hospitals provided healthcare. In Andhra Pradesh, Tamil Nadu, and Kerala, large-scale conversions of entire communities of Madigas, Paraiyars, Pulayas, and other Dalit groups occurred through the 19th and early 20th centuries. The result is that approximately 60–70% of Indian Christians today are from Dalit or Adivasi backgrounds, making Christianity in India predominantly a low-caste and tribal faith despite its European colonial associations.

The constitutional problem: Dalits who convert to Christianity or Islam lose their Scheduled Caste status and all associated constitutional protections — reservation, SCSP (Scheduled Castes Sub Plan) funding, protections under the SC/ST Prevention of Atrocities Act. This is explicit in the Constitution (Scheduled Castes Order 1950, extended to Sikh and Buddhist Dalits but not Christian or Muslim). The National Commission for Religious and Linguistic Minorities (Ranganath Misra Commission) recommended in 2007 that SC status be extended to all Dalits regardless of religion. The recommendation has not been implemented.

Islam — Medieval and Colonial Conversion

Caste Within Indian Islam — Ashraf, Ajlaf, Arzal

Muslim communities in India are themselves internally stratified by caste. The hierarchy: Ashraf (high-caste — Syed, Sheikh, Mughal, Pathan — claiming Arab, Central Asian, or Rajput descent), Ajlaf (middle-caste — weaver Momins, Dhunias, Qureshis, etc. — converted from middle Hindu castes), and Arzal (lowest — converted from untouchable castes — Halalkhors, Lalbegis, Dhobis). The Arzal are Muslim Dalits who face discrimination from within the Muslim community despite Islamic theology's explicit statement of human equality before God. They are excluded from burial in the same sections of Muslim graveyards in some regions. They face residential segregation within Muslim localities. In Bihar and UP, there are documented cases of Arzal Muslim children being excluded from madrasa education by Ashraf teachers. The conversion did not escape caste — it reproduced caste inside Islam.

Sikhism — The Gurdwara Segregation Crisis

Mazhabi Sikhs and the Parallel Gurdwara Structure

Sikhism's founding theology is more explicitly anti-caste than any other major religion in the world. The Gurus explicitly condemned the caste system. The Guru Granth Sahib includes the verses of untouchable and low-caste saints. The langar — compulsory communal eating — was designed as an anti-caste institution. The result in 2024: Dalit Sikhs (Mazhabi and Ramdasi Sikhs — together approximately 30% of Punjab's Sikh population) are effectively excluded from or marginalised in Jat-dominated gurdwaras and have built a separate network of Dera Ravidass establishments, Mazhabi gurdwaras, and affiliated religious spaces. This parallel structure is not separatism — it is the product of exclusion from the mainstream structure. The Jat Sikh community that controls the SGPC (Shiromani Gurdwara Parbandhak Committee) has never had a Dalit Sikh as its president.

Reconversion — Ghar Wapsi and the Law

Anti-Conversion Laws as Caste Enforcement Tools

Anti-conversion laws in India — passed by BJP-ruled states including UP (2021), Madhya Pradesh (2021), Gujarat (2021), Karnataka (2022), Uttarakhand (2018) — prohibit conversions through "fraud, force, or allurement." The stated target is Muslim and Christian missionaries. The documented effect: Dalit individuals who convert to escape caste discrimination face criminal complaints filed under these laws by members of the upper-caste Hindu community they are leaving. In UP, multiple documented cases involve Dalit women who converted to Islam to escape caste-based sexual violence having criminal anti-conversion cases filed against them or the men they married. The anti-conversion laws function as legal mechanisms to prevent Dalit exit from Hinduism — and thus from the caste structure that is rooted in Hindu religious authority.

Scheduled Caste Percentage by Religious Community — India (Census 2011 + NSSO)
SC status is formally recognised only for Hindu, Sikh, and Buddhist Dalits. Christian and Muslim Dalit percentages are estimates from NSSO and National Commission surveys. Actual figures may be higher.
October 14, 1956 · Nagpur

The Conversion — 600,000 People, One Afternoon, Six Weeks Before Ambedkar's Death

Ambedkar had announced in 1935 that he would not die a Hindu. He spent two decades studying every major world religion — Christianity, Islam, Sikhism, Buddhism — before concluding that Buddhism, specifically as he reconstructed it, offered what he needed: a system of ethics based on human reason and compassion rather than divine revelation; an explicit rejection of caste hierarchy; a tradition that came from within India rather than from colonial powers; and a historical founder — Siddhartha Gautama — who had himself broken with Brahminical orthodoxy. On October 14, 1956, in a field in Nagpur, in a ceremony he had designed himself, Ambedkar accepted the Three Refuges and Five Precepts from a Sri Lankan Buddhist monk, then turned to administer them to approximately 600,000 followers. He took 22 vows — including explicit rejections of Brahminical Hindu beliefs, of the caste system, of karma as justification for untouchability, and of the authority of Hindu gods. He died on December 6, 1956 — six weeks later.

The Theological Innovation

The Buddha and His Dhamma — A Buddhism Without Karma, Without Rebirth, Without God

Ambedkar's The Buddha and His Dhamma, completed in the weeks before his death, is the founding text of Navayana ("new vehicle") Buddhism. His argument: the historical Siddhartha Gautama was a social reformer who lived in a society stratified by caste (the Brahminical Varna system) and who responded to it by building a community — the Sangha — explicitly based on equality of all members regardless of birth. The metaphysical elements of later Buddhist tradition — karma, rebirth, the cycle of samsara, meditation as the path to individual nirvana — Ambedkar understood as later accretions that served to make Buddhism theologically compatible with the same Brahminical worldview it originally challenged. His Navayana strips out karma and rebirth as doctrines and recenters Buddhism as: the Five Precepts as social ethics; the Sangha as a democratic community of equals; the Dhamma as a guide to building a just society. It is a reading that most traditional Buddhist scholars — including Theravada and Mahayana scholars — consider unorthodox. It is a reading that has given 7.95 million people a religion specifically designed for social liberation.

The 22 Vows

What Ambedkar Required His Converts to Reject

The 22 vows Ambedkar administered at Nagpur are the most explicit list of rejections in any religious conversion ceremony in history. Among them: I shall have no belief in Brahma, Vishnu, and Mahesh. I shall have no belief in Rama and Krishna. I shall have no belief that Ram and Krishna were the incarnation of God. I shall not observe shraddha (ancestor worship rites performed by Brahmin priests). I shall not take prasad from temples. I shall not have the sacrament performed by a Brahmin priest. I do not believe and shall not believe that the Lord Buddha was an incarnation of Vishnu (a Brahminical claim designed to absorb Buddhism back into Hinduism). I renounce Hinduism, which is harmful to humanity and impedes the advancement and development of humanity, and I embrace Buddhism as my religion. These are not individual spiritual choices. They are a collective political declaration — the rejection of the religious system that sanctioned untouchability, made by 600,000 people simultaneously.

The Global Spread

Navayana Buddhism Beyond India

The Ambedkarite Buddhist movement has spread along Dalit diaspora pathways. In Japan, Dr. Surai Sasai — a Japanese Buddhist monk who became a devoted follower of Ambedkar's vision — spent decades organising conversions in Maharashtra and founded Nagaloka, a major Ambedkarite training centre in Nagpur. The Japanese connection is partly explained by the shared appeal of socially engaged Buddhism across Asian Buddhist traditions. In the United Kingdom, the Triratna Buddhist Community (formerly Friends of the Western Buddhist Order), founded partly by Bhante Sangharakshita who worked in India in the 1950s, has maintained strong connections to the Dalit Buddhist movement and runs Ambedkarite Buddhist activities in British cities. In the United States, the Dalit diaspora — particularly in California and New Jersey — has established Ambedkarite Buddhist centres that function simultaneously as religious and political organising spaces. The International Buddhist Confederation formed to coordinate these communities across countries.

The Continuing Struggle

Buddhist Converts Remain SC — But Face Hindu Nationalist Hostility

The 1990 amendment to the Constitution (Scheduled Castes Order) recognises Buddhists as Scheduled Castes — restoring the rights removed when Ambedkar's converts were initially excluded in 1950. This means Navayana Buddhist Dalits have constitutional protections and reservation rights. But the Hindu nationalist movement has consistently portrayed mass Buddhist conversions as attacks on Hindu civilisation and foreign (specifically Pakistani and missionary) conspiracies. In BJP-ruled states, Dalit Buddhist communities face increased hostility in land disputes, in access to SCSP funds administered by Hindu-controlled gram panchayats, and in symbolic assertion of identity. The Ambedkar Jayanti celebrations on April 14 — the largest annual mass gathering in India, dwarfing any other political or religious event — are sites of frequent upper-caste violence, particularly against Dalit communities who display Ambedkar's image prominently in Hindu-majority areas.

The Universal Pattern

Caste as a Social Technology — Not a Hindu Theological Property

Every major religion that has significant Indian presence maintains caste hierarchy within its community — despite each religion's explicit theology of human equality. This is not coincidence. Caste is not a Hindu belief system that disappears when belief changes. It is a social technology of resource allocation and labour exploitation that the dominant communities in any religious organisation have found useful to maintain. The theological arguments for it differ by religion. The social outcomes — occupational heredity, endogamy, residential segregation, economic exclusion — are consistent across faiths. When Ambedkar said that caste was not the product of religion but that religion was a product used to justify caste, he was describing a process visible in every religious community in India.

Hinduism

The Origin and the Core Case

The theological justification for caste in Brahminical Hinduism is explicit: the Purusha Sukta (Rigveda X:90) places Brahmins from the mouth of the cosmic being, Kshatriyas from the arms, Vaishyas from the thighs, Shudras from the feet. Untouchables are not in the body at all — they are outside the cosmic being. The Manusmriti codifies the laws. The doctrine of karma justifies the current birth as the result of past-life actions. This triple structure — cosmic origin, legal code, karmic justification — makes the caste system self-replicating within Hindu theology in a way that no reformist tradition has been able to dissolve. The Bhakti saints challenged the cosmic origin claim. They were absorbed. Liberal Hindus challenge the Manusmriti. The karma doctrine survives. Temple management, priestly roles, and the economic structure of Hindu religious institutions remain completely Brahmin-controlled.

Sikhism

The Most Anti-Caste Founding, The Most Visible Failure

No major religion has a more explicit founding anti-caste theology than Sikhism. The Gurus wrote, preached, and institutionalised against caste. The result in Punjab 2024: Jat Sikhs own ~75% of Punjab's agricultural land. Dalit Sikhs (who are ~32% of Punjab's population) own less than 5% of agricultural land. The SGPC — the supreme Sikh religious body — has never had a Dalit Sikh president. Separate Dalit gurdwaras and Dera Ravidass establishments exist across Punjab and the diaspora because Dalit Sikhs were made unwelcome in mainstream gurdwaras. The reason is economic, not theological: Jat Sikhs controlled land and used gurdwaras as instruments of community social power. Dalit Sikh access to gurdwara management meant Dalit Sikh access to land dispute mediation, marriage network facilitation, and social legitimacy — all of which the Jat community had strong economic reasons to control.

Christianity

The Kerala Church's Two Cemeteries

The Syrian Christian community of Kerala — claiming descent from converts of the Apostle Thomas in 52 CE — maintained a caste hierarchy from within Christianity: St. Thomas Christians (Nasranis) treated themselves as Brahmin-equivalent and practiced endogamy and occupational separation from lower-caste Christians. In some Kerala churches in the 19th and early 20th centuries, Dalit Christians were buried in separate sections of church cemeteries, seated in separate sections during mass, or attended separate services entirely. The Church of South India's anti-caste reforms have reduced (not eliminated) formal segregation. Caste-endogamous marriage among different Christian communities in Kerala and Tamil Nadu is documented in every matrimonial survey — most Indian Christian matrimonial advertisements specify caste: "Nadar Christian", "Vellalar Christian", "SC Christian". The Catholic Bishops Conference of India has acknowledged caste discrimination within the Church in multiple official statements. It has not been resolved.

Islam

The Arzal — Muslim Untouchables

The Sachar Committee report (2006) — the most comprehensive government study of Muslim socioeconomic status — documented that Muslim communities are internally stratified with outcomes that correlate strongly with pre-conversion Hindu caste status. Ashraf Muslims (claiming high-caste Arab/Central Asian descent) have significantly higher educational attainment, income, and occupational status than Ajlaf or Arzal Muslims. The Arzal — Muslims from untouchable caste backgrounds (Halalkhors, Lalbegis, Mehtar communities) — have socioeconomic indicators comparable to Hindu Scheduled Castes. Separate burial in some regions. Documented exclusion from some mosques and madrasas. The conversion from Hinduism removed the theological structure of caste — there is no Islamic doctrine of karmic birth pollution — but did not remove the social and economic structure. The Pasmanda Muslim movement — representing lower-caste Muslims in North India — has been organising since the 1990s on this basis, arguing that Muslim representation in political parties and institutions must account for internal caste stratification, not just the Hindu-Muslim binary.

Caste & Indian Democracy — Whose Republic?
Judiciary, media, military — formal equality, structural capture

The Central Contradiction

A Republic of Equals — On Paper

India is constitutionally a republic of equals. It is also the country where in 1997, 58 Dalits were massacred in Laxmanpur Bathe and every convicted killer was later acquitted. Both facts are simultaneously true. Democracy is not just formal institutions — it is the social power to make those institutions work for you. A constitution guaranteeing equality does not produce equality if the police, judges, bureaucrats, politicians, and journalists who run it all come from communities that benefit from inequality.

~1–2
Dalit judges ever in Supreme Court history (out of 200+)
0%
Dalit editors in 121 major Indian media orgs — Oxfam 2019
88%
Upper-caste Hindus in senior media leadership
0
Dalit SGPC presidents in Sikh body history
The Court That Decides Dalit Rights Has Almost No Dalits

Supreme Court of India — Caste Composition

The Supreme Court has 34 judges. In the entire history of independent India, approximately 3–4 Dalit judges have ever served on the Supreme Court. As of 2024, there is approximately 1 Dalit judge on the bench. The court that is the final arbiter of constitutional rights — that allowed the Laxmanpur Bathe acquittals to stand — has virtually no Dalit perspective in its deliberations. The appointment mechanism (collegium system, where sitting judges recommend new judges) has no diversity requirement and no accountability for caste composition. It is structurally self-replicating upper-caste.

District Courts — Where Atrocity Cases Actually Go

Caste of the Judge = Outcome of the Case

District judges — who hear SC/ST Atrocity Act cases — come predominantly from upper-caste backgrounds in UP, Bihar, Rajasthan, and Gujarat, where caste violence is highest. Research by the Vidhi Centre for Legal Policy found that acquittal rates in atrocity cases correlate with the social distance between the judge's background and the victim's community. The systemic problem: the people deciding whether caste violence happened are overwhelmingly from communities that have never experienced it and have historically benefited from the structures that produce it.

Witness Intimidation — The Invisible Variable

Why Every Massacre Gets Acquitted

The pattern across Kilvenmani, Belchi, Laxmanpur Bathe, Bathani Tola — identical in each case. Lower court convicts. High Court acquits citing "contradictions in witness testimony." The contradictions are manufactured across 10–20 years of witness intimidation — economic boycotts, physical threats, false counter-FIRs against witnesses, bribing or threatening families. The accused have resources. The witnesses have nothing. The High Court reads "contradictions" as insufficient evidence. The acquittals are legally defensible. They are also politically engineered. Both are true.

SC Representation in Indian Judiciary vs. Mandated 15% — 2022
Representation falls sharply at senior levels. District court data from Law Commission. HC and SC figures from RTI responses and Law Ministry data.
Who Decides What India Reads About Dalits

The Oxfam-Newslaundry 2019 Survey — "Who Tells Our Stories"

Survey of 121 major Indian media organisations — newspapers, TV channels, digital outlets, magazines. Finding: 0 Dalits (0%) in senior editorial leadership positions (editor, editor-in-chief, editorial director) across all 121 organisations. Upper-caste Hindus: 88% of leadership positions. Every editorial decision about whether to cover a caste atrocity, how prominently, what language to use, whose perspective to foreground, when to move on — is made by people from communities that have historically benefited from Dalit oppression. The survey was published. Zero major newsroom made editorial hires specifically in response. The data was too damning for those who controlled the response mechanism to respond to.

Coverage Bias — Documented

Which Deaths Get Named

The Media Rumble / Newslaundry atrocity coverage audit (2018–2022): Dalit atrocity cases received an average of 0.3 news cycles of coverage in English national media. The Delhi gang rape (December 2012) — victim from a lower-middle-class but upper-caste background — received 847 English-language news segments in the first month. The Hathras gang rape (September 2020) — victim Dalit, body cremated by police at night without family consent — received 12 English-language news segments in the first month before activist pressure expanded coverage. The victim's caste determined how many people decided her death deserved witness.

The Language of Caste in Newsrooms

How Upper-Caste Reporters Frame Dalit Stories

Documented patterns in English-language coverage of Dalit issues: perpetrators identified as "villagers" or "local community members" rather than by caste. Victims described as "members of a marginalised community" in passive constructions. Caste of the perpetrator omitted; caste of the victim mentioned as "context." Massacres described as "clashes" suggesting mutual violence. Honour killings described as "family disputes." The language is not neutral — it systematically obscures the power structure. Upper-caste journalists use these framings not from malice but from cultural conditioning that makes the hierarchy invisible because it has always worked in their favour.

The BSP Experiment — And Its Destruction

What Dalit Electoral Power Looked Like at Its Peak

Kanshi Ram built the Bahujan Samaj Party on one thesis: the oppressed 85% can outvote the privileged 15% if united. Mayawati became Chief Minister of Uttar Pradesh four times — the most powerful direct exercise of Dalit political power in world history by population. At its peak in 2007–2012, the BSP governed India's most populous state with a Dalit woman at the top. Measurable outcomes: increase in SC/ST Sub Plan spending, increase in FIRs actually registered against upper-caste perpetrators, visible change in police and administrative behaviour in Dalit-majority districts. The upper-caste response was a decade-long counter-mobilisation using BJP's Hindutva narrative — pulling OBC communities back into the Hindu fold, fragmenting the Bahujan coalition, reducing BSP to its current shell. The experiment showed Dalit political power is possible. It also showed how it gets dismantled.

Reserved Seats — The Permanent Ceiling

84 Lok Sabha Seats, Zero Power Over Their Own Future

84 Lok Sabha constituencies are reserved for SC candidates. This guarantees SC presence in Parliament — it does not guarantee SC political power. Reserved constituencies can only be won by SC candidates — but the parties that field those candidates, control the party whip, determine ministerial appointments, and set legislative agenda are overwhelmingly upper-caste led. A Dalit MP from a reserved constituency who challenges his party's upper-caste leadership loses his ticket next cycle. The reservation structure produces Dalit faces in Parliament attached to upper-caste political machines. It is not representation — it is tokenism with constitutional machinery.

Melavalavu 1997 — Democracy's Price

Win an Election. Lose Your Head.

Murugesan won the Melavalavu panchayat presidency by democratic vote — the first Dalit to do so in that area. The Thevar dominant caste community refused to accept the result. On May 1, 1997, a mob intercepted Murugesan and colleagues. Six were killed. Murugesan's head was severed and paraded through streets. The district collector had been warned in advance. No protective deployment was made. Partial convictions followed, challenged for decades on appeal. The message sent to every Dalit candidate across India: your vote counts on paper. On the ground, we decide who leads.

Dalit MPs in Lok Sabha — Reserved vs. Won on General Seats (1952–2024)
Near-zero wins on unreserved general seats throughout history. Reserved seats guarantee presence, not power. Data: Election Commission of India.
The IAS — India's Permanent Government

Who Actually Runs the Country

The Indian Administrative Service is the permanent executive — the 5,000 officers who run ministries, districts, and state governments regardless of which party is elected. IAS officers implement reservation policy. They deploy police during atrocities. They certify SC/ST status documents. They control SCSP (Scheduled Castes Sub Plan) budget allocation. Their caste composition matters more than almost any other institution because they exercise direct administrative power over the conditions of Dalit life. IAS composition data from UPSC (2022): SC officers represent approximately 14.5% of the total IAS cadre — close to the mandated 15%. But representation at Joint Secretary and above (the decision-making levels): drops to approximately 8–9%. At Chief Secretary level (most senior state position): roughly 5–6%. Reservation at entry does not survive the promotion pipeline.

The SCSP — Money That Disappears

₹4 Lakh Crore Allocated. Fraction Spent on Dalits.

The Scheduled Castes Sub Plan mandates that state budgets allocate funds proportional to SC population percentage (16.6% nationally) specifically for SC welfare. It is law. National Dalit Movement for Justice RTI data across 10 states (2018–22): average actual SCSP spending as percentage of allocated funds: 43%. Funds are regularly diverted to general schemes that benefit all castes, counted against SCSP targets, and never audited effectively. The bureaucrats doing the diverting are implementing decisions made by upper-caste politicians and senior officers. The Dalit communities meant to receive the funds have no mechanism to audit the diversion. This is not corruption — it is the administrative system doing what it was set up to do: protect upper-caste resource control while maintaining the paperwork of equality.

Police — The Violence Is the Point

Dalit Representation in Police vs. Dalit Experience of Police

NCRB 2022: 50,291 crimes against SC communities registered. Every registered crime required a police officer to register it — which Dalit survivors document as the first site of refusal, harassment, and counter-FIR filing. Dalit representation in Indian Police Service (IPS, the officer corps): approximately 12% across all ranks, falling to under 5% at DIG and above. State police forces in UP, Bihar, Rajasthan — where SC crime rates are highest — have among the lowest SC representation in officer ranks. A Dalit woman who reports rape by an upper-caste man walks into a police station where the officer is statistically likely to be upper-caste, likely to know the family of the accused, and operating in a system where registering a case against an upper-caste man has social and professional consequences for him. The system produces the outcomes it was built to produce.

Caste Regiments — Institutionalised in the Army

The Mahar Regiment — Ambedkar's Heritage, the Army's Contradiction

The Indian Army maintains "class regiments" — units recruited from specific castes: Rajput Regiment, Jat Regiment, Sikh Regiment, Mahar Regiment. The Mahar Regiment has direct Dalit identity — Ambedkar's father served in it, and the regiment carries Ambedkarite political significance for Maharashtra's Dalit Buddhist community. However, the regiment system institutionalises caste into the military's chain of command. "Martial" caste regiments (Rajput, Jat, Gurkha) have historically received more prestigious postings, better equipment, and higher promotion rates — a classification system whose origins are explicitly the British colonial "martial races" theory, which assigned front-line combat roles to certain castes and support roles to others based on colonial stereotypes that served colonial recruitment needs. The "martial races" theory has never been formally repudiated. The caste regiment system remains in operation. Its equity implications have never been officially studied.

Officer Corps Composition — No Data Available

The Institution That Will Not Count Its Own Caste

The Indian Army does not publish caste-disaggregated data for its officer corps. RTI requests for caste composition of commissioned officers have been denied on national security grounds. This means it is impossible to publicly verify what is observable anecdotally: that the officer corps is overwhelmingly upper-caste, that promotion above Colonel level is effectively inaccessible to Dalit officers in most arms, and that the social culture of officer messes in many regiments replicates the caste hierarchy of the civilian world it drew its officers from. The refusal to publish the data is itself evidence that those who control the data know what it shows. If the data showed equity, they would publish it.

Digital Age & Caste — The Algorithm Problem
Silicon Valley, social media, surveillance, and the new face of untouchability

The New Infrastructure of Discrimination

Caste Went Digital. The Law Didn't Follow.

The internet did not dissolve caste. It gave caste new infrastructure. Matrimonial algorithms sort by caste by default. WhatsApp groups organise caste-based economic boycotts in minutes. Twitter/X is used to coordinate mass harassment of Dalit writers, journalists, and activists. Aadhaar and digital ID systems encode the same caste categories into permanent digital records. And in Silicon Valley — the industry that built the tools — Dalit tech workers are filing discrimination complaints at companies whose HR manuals have no caste category because the people who wrote them never thought it was relevant.

95%
Indian matrimonial sites allow caste-based filtering by default
2021
Cisco case — first US corporate caste discrimination lawsuit, California
67%
Dalit tech workers in US report caste discrimination (Equality Labs 2018)

Major Indian tech companies with explicit caste non-discrimination policy (2023)
The Cisco Case — 2020

America's First Corporate Caste Discrimination Lawsuit

California's Department of Fair Employment and Housing sued Cisco Systems in 2020, alleging that a Dalit engineer was discriminated against by two higher-caste Indian managers. The complaint described a pattern of professional sabotage: exclusion from meetings, blocked promotions, public humiliation, and a hostile work environment rooted in the victim's caste identity. Cisco argued that US anti-discrimination law does not cover caste — that caste is not "race," "national origin," or "ancestry" as defined by US law. This is the defining legal question: the most powerful anti-discrimination law system in the world was written without caste in mind, and the communities it protects are contesting whether caste fits any existing category. The case has not reached final verdict. It has already changed the conversation.

Equality Labs 2018 Survey — 1,500 Dalit Tech Workers in US

The Numbers That Shocked Silicon Valley

67% of Dalit respondents reported facing caste discrimination at work. 60% faced verbal or physical assault based on caste. 80% felt they had to hide their caste identity at work. The survey was methodologically criticised by some Hindu advocacy groups — but the California Department of Fair Employment and Housing used it in its Cisco complaint. The pattern it describes — upper-caste Indian managers discriminating against Dalit subordinates, enabled by HR systems with no caste category — has since been documented in testimonies from workers at Google, Microsoft, Apple, and Indian IT firms operating in the US.

Seattle 2023 — The Anti-Caste Ordinance

First City in the World to Explicitly Ban Caste Discrimination

Seattle became the first city globally to explicitly add caste as a protected category in its anti-discrimination ordinance in February 2023. The vote was 6-1. It was immediately opposed by Hindu nationalist groups (Hindu American Foundation, CoHNA) who argued it would stigmatise the entire Hindu community and amount to anti-Hindu discrimination. California attempted a statewide caste anti-discrimination bill (SB 403) in 2023 — it passed the legislature but was vetoed by Governor Gavin Newsom, who called existing anti-discrimination frameworks sufficient. Dalit advocacy groups said Newsom's veto showed how political lobbying by upper-caste diaspora organisations can block legislation that would protect 5+ million Dalit Americans.

Dalit Tech Workers in US — Discrimination Experiences (Equality Labs 2018, n=1,500)
Self-reported. Methodology critiqued by some groups. California DFEH cited in Cisco complaint.
Coordinated Caste Harassment Online

WhatsApp, Twitter, YouTube — The New Caste Weapons

The social media harassment of Dalit writers, journalists, and activists follows a documented pattern. A Dalit intellectual posts an Ambedkarite argument or a critique of Brahminical practices. A right-wing account with large upper-caste following quote-tweets or screenshots it with a derogatory characterisation. Within hours, hundreds of accounts flood the original poster with casteist slurs, threats, and content designed to make them feel unsafe. The coordination is demonstrable — identical language, simultaneous timing, accounts with no other activity. Twitter/X India has been documented to respond to these campaigns differently depending on the political valence of the target: Dalit accounts reporting coordinated caste harassment receive slower responses and fewer enforcement actions than comparable targets from politically connected communities.

Rohith Vemula — 2016

The Death That Became a Hashtag and a Policy Battle

Rohith Vemula, a Dalit PhD student at the University of Hyderabad, died by suicide in January 2016 after being expelled from the university hostel under pressure from BJP politicians and the ABVP student union. His final letter — "My birth is my fatal accident" — became one of the most widely circulated documents in Indian social media history. The government's response was to dispute his Dalit identity (his mother's caste certificate was challenged), converting the political question of institutional casteism into an administrative question of documentation. The social media battle over Rohith Vemula's caste identity — simultaneously the most intimate possible question about a dead man's life and a high-stakes political dispute — showed how the digital space amplifies both the visibility of caste violence and the mechanisms that deny it.

WhatsApp — Caste in the Group Chat

Economic Boycotts Organised in Minutes

Upper-caste economic boycotts of Dalit businesses — previously requiring community meetings and word-of-mouth organisation — can now be coordinated through WhatsApp in hours. Documented cases in Gujarat (2016, after Una flogging), Rajasthan (2022), and UP (multiple cases 2018–2023): upper-caste WhatsApp groups circulating lists of Dalit-owned businesses with instructions not to patronise them, not to rent property to Dalits, not to hire Dalit labour. These boycotts are not reported to police (economic boycott has no criminal provision in most states). They are not tracked by NCRB. They are invisible in official data. They are a primary tool of contemporary caste enforcement.

Shaadi.com, Jeevansathi, BharatMatrimony

Caste Endogamy — Now With Machine Learning

India's matrimonial industry is worth $250M+ annually. The three dominant platforms — Shaadi.com, Jeevansathi, and BharatMatrimony — all offer caste-based filtering as a default feature. Users can filter potential matches by caste, sub-caste, and gotra. "Caste no bar" is a checkbox users tick to indicate deviation from the norm — making it structurally clear that caste-based filtering IS the norm from which you opt out. These platforms process tens of millions of matches per year. Their algorithms optimise for "match quality" — which, when caste preference is the dominant filter, means their recommendation engines actively learn to propose within-caste matches. The result: algorithmic enforcement of endogamy at population scale, running continuously on the phones of every young Indian looking for a spouse. No government has moved to regulate caste-based matrimonial filtering. The platforms argue it reflects user choice. It does. It also scales and automates the choice in ways that make it structurally more powerful than the offline equivalent.

The "Caste No Bar" Paradox

Opt-Out Design Tells You Everything

The design choice — caste filtering ON by default, "caste no bar" as opt-out — is not neutral. It encodes a social norm into software architecture. When a user ticks "caste no bar," they are signalling deviation from expected behaviour. The platform architecture tells users what is normal. This has measurable effects: matrimonial platform data (not publicly released but documented in academic studies via user surveys) shows that approximately 8–12% of users select "caste no bar." The 88–92% who do not are not necessarily consciously choosing endogamy in that moment — they are following the default. The default is the product's implicit value statement: we believe you want someone from your caste. The platform makes that belief true at population scale.

Aadhaar & Digital Identity

When the State's Database Encodes Your Caste

The Aadhaar system links biometric identity to a 12-digit number that interfaces with caste certificates, SC/ST status documentation, and government welfare databases. In theory, Aadhaar enables efficient delivery of reservation benefits. In practice, documented problems: (1) Rural Dalit households with low digital literacy face multiple authentication failures at ration shops equipped with biometric readers — starvation deaths linked to Aadhaar authentication failures have been documented in Jharkhand. (2) Caste certificates — required to access reservation benefits — are increasingly digitalised, creating a situation where a Dalit person's entire economic relationship with the state depends on a digital document chain that can be disrupted by administrative error, deliberate obstruction, or system failure. (3) Digital databases of caste status are accessible to local administrative officials — including police and bureaucrats who may use the information to identify Dalit activists and monitor their activity.

Facial Recognition + Caste

The Police Technology Problem

Indian police forces are deploying facial recognition technology — with no transparency about training data, accuracy rates, or safeguards. Facial recognition systems trained primarily on upper-caste faces (because upper-caste faces are overrepresented in training data sources — films, social media, official documentation) show higher error rates for darker-skinned faces. Darker skin is correlated — though not deterministically — with Dalit and Adivasi communities due to historical patterns of occupational and geographic distribution. A facial recognition system with differential error rates by skin tone will produce differential false-positive arrest rates by caste. This is not hypothetical. The NCRB is coordinating a national facial recognition database. No caste equity audit of facial recognition technology used by Indian police has been published.

Internet Shutdown — Who Gets Cut Off

Every Caste Violence Hotspot Gets Disconnected

India leads the world in government-ordered internet shutdowns — over 550 documented between 2012 and 2022. The stated justification is always "communal tension" or "law and order." The documented pattern: shutdowns are most frequent in districts where caste atrocities have occurred and where Dalit communities are attempting to organise. The Bhima Koregaon area (Maharashtra) saw repeated internet disruptions around the January 1 commemorations. Una (Gujarat) after the 2016 Dalit uprising. Hathras district (UP) after the 2020 gang rape — the UP government imposed a communications blackout that prevented journalists from reaching the family. Internet shutdowns in atrocity contexts function as evidence suppression: they prevent documentation, deny the story to national and international media, and interrupt the organisational response. The tool is marketed as law-and-order management. It functions as atrocity cover.

💰

Dalit Economy & Labour — The Wealth Apartheid

Land is the foundation of the Indian rural economy, and Dalits own almost none of it. SC/ST communities — 25% of India's population — own approximately 9% of agricultural land. The median SC household wealth (combining land, property, savings, livestock, and durable goods) is less than 40% of the national median. The median OBC household is at approximately 65%. The median upper-caste household: 250%+. This is not a legacy gap from a historical injustice that is gradually self-correcting. It is an active gap that is widening. In liberalised India since 1991, land prices, equity values, and capital returns have massively appreciated. Dalit communities, with almost no land or capital to begin with, did not participate in this appreciation. Their wages — the only income most Dalit households have — have not risen proportionately. The gap between what upper-caste families inherited and what Dalit families inherited in 1950 is larger in rupee terms today than it was at independence.

The bonded labour system. The ILO estimates India has the world's largest bonded labour population — approximately 40 million people. Dalits and Adivasis constitute the vast majority. The Bonded Labour System (Abolition) Act was passed in 1976. It is law. Bonded labour continues openly in: brick kilns across UP, Bihar, and Rajasthan (the National Human Rights Commission documented 300,000+ bonded workers in UP's brick kilns alone in 2012); carpet weaving in Mirzapur and Bhadohi (documented by Anti-Slavery International, ILO); agricultural bondage in Andhra Pradesh, Karnataka, and Punjab (where Dalit labourers are bonded to landlords through advance payment of wedding or medical expenses that are never repayable at prevailing wage rates); stone quarries in Rajasthan. The enforcement mechanism: Labour Inspectors are appointed by state governments. State governments are controlled by the same political parties whose core vote base is the landlord class that benefits from bonded labour. Enforcement is structurally impossible.

Caste discrimination is measurable and real in the private sector. The Thorat-Attewell CV experiment (2007, published in Economic and Political Weekly): identical CVs were sent to private employers in Delhi — same qualifications, different names. CVs with upper-caste Hindu names received significantly more callbacks than CVs with Muslim or SC names. Caste discrimination in the private sector — which employs 90%+ of India's workforce and is explicitly excluded from constitutional reservation requirements — is not invisible. It is measurable. This is the central argument against those who insist that reservations distort a "neutral" market meritocracy: the market is already caste-distorted, and the distortion benefits upper castes.

The "Dalit capitalism" debate. Milind Kamble founded the Dalit Indian Chamber of Commerce and Industry (DICCI) in 2005 on the premise that Dalit economic empowerment through entrepreneurship is necessary alongside political empowerment. The idea: if upper castes built economic power through business networks, Dalit communities must do the same. Critics — including Anand Teltumbde and Suraj Yengde — argue that Dalit capitalism misunderstands the problem: caste discrimination operates in market transactions themselves (credit access, supplier networks, customer trust, contract enforcement) so that Dalit businesses face caste discrimination at every stage of the value chain. A Dalit entrepreneur cannot simply "out-compete" a Patel business in Gujarat when the Patel's supplier gives credit the Dalit cannot access and the customer community trusts the Patel brand because of shared caste identity. Capitalism without caste abolition reproduces a few wealthy Dalits at the top while the structure at the bottom remains unchanged.

📱

Caste in the Digital Age — New Tools, Same Hierarchy

The early internet promised a space where identity could be obscured, hierarchies dissolved, and voices equalised by the absence of bodies. This promise was false. Digital India has not transcended caste — it has given caste new infrastructure, new velocity, new surveillance tools, and a new arena for both oppression and resistance. The same communities that face violence in villages face organised harassment online. The same state that arrests Dalit activists in person uses UAPA and IT Act provisions to arrest them for tweets and WhatsApp messages. And simultaneously, YouTube, Twitter, and podcast platforms have given Dalit voices reach that print media — historically upper-caste owned and edited — denied them for 70 years.

Online caste harassment — documented at scale. Equality Labs' 2018 survey of 1,200 South Asian Americans: 67% of Dalit respondents experienced caste-based discrimination online. In India, Dalit journalists like Dilnoza Yusupova, Meena Kotwal (The Mooknayak), and academics have documented sustained harassment campaigns — coordinated mass-reporting of accounts to get them suspended, organised flooding of mentions with caste slurs, doxxing (publishing home addresses), and explicit rape and death threats tied to their Dalit identity. The pattern: a Dalit journalist publishes a report on caste violence. Within hours, a coordinated network — using multiple accounts, often with traceable connections to Hindutva digital cell networks — floods their mentions. Twitter's/X's content moderation has historically been slower to act on caste slurs than on other forms of hate speech, because caste slur vocabulary is not in the default moderation training sets used by platforms built and moderated primarily by Silicon Valley employees, many of whom are upper-caste Indians who are not culturally familiar with what the slurs mean.

The state uses digital surveillance specifically against Dalit activists. The Bhima Koregaon case (2018–2021) is the most documented case of alleged digital evidence fabrication in Indian legal history: Arsenal Consulting, a digital forensics firm, examined the computers of multiple arrested activists and found that documents used as prosecution evidence — including an alleged letter "planning" the assassination of the Prime Minister — had been planted on their computers by malware months before the arrests. The malware was attributed to a group called "NetWire." The state has contested these findings, but they have been published in peer-reviewed security research and covered in the New York Times and Washington Post. Stan Swamy, who died in custody, was 84 years old with Parkinson's disease. He did not know how to use a computer. A document found on his alleged device contained formatting metadata inconsistent with when he allegedly created it.

The emancipatory dimension — Dalit digital media. Dalit Camera (YouTube, 2012–present) is the most watched Dalit media platform in India — documenting ground-level caste violence, atrocity survivor testimony, and Ambedkarite political events in real time, with no upper-caste editorial gate. The Mooknayak — founded by Meena Kotwal, a Dalit journalist — is a digital-only news outlet covering Dalit issues that mainstream media systematically ignores. Podcast networks in Tamil, Marathi, Telugu, and Hindi are building Dalit public intellectual culture outside institutional gatekeeping for the first time. The Dalit History Month Instagram campaign (April, modelled on Black History Month) has reached hundreds of millions of impressions globally. None of this would exist in print. The internet gave Dalit media the ability to exist without upper-caste gatekeepers.
