💊

Caste & Health — Whose Body the State Protects

Health data by caste in India reveals a country within a country. Dalit child mortality, maternal mortality, malnutrition, and disease burden numbers resemble those of the world's poorest countries — while upper-caste India's health numbers resemble middle-income countries. These are not separate nations with different development trajectories. They are the same hospitals, the same government health schemes, the same insurance programmes — producing radically different outcomes by caste. The difference is not explained by income alone: controlling for income, SC communities still have worse health outcomes than upper-caste communities at the same income level. Caste discrimination in healthcare delivery is the explanation that survives after economic adjustment.

The numbers. SC infant mortality rate (IMR): approximately 47 per 1,000 live births (NFHS-5, 2019–21), compared to 25 for upper-caste Hindus and 32 nationally. SC maternal mortality ratio: estimated 2–3x the national average in high-caste-concentration districts of UP, Bihar, and Rajasthan. Child stunting (chronic malnutrition): 42.8% of SC children are stunted, compared to 30.6% for general category — in a country already considered to have a malnutrition crisis, Dalit children are measurably more malnourished. Anaemia in Dalit women: 59% (NFHS-5). Dalit access to institutional delivery (hospital births rather than home births): 78%, compared to 90% for upper-caste women — the 12-point gap costs Dalit infant lives every day.

Hospital discrimination — documented cases. Multiple NHRC reports document Dalit patients being refused treatment at government hospitals, being assigned to separate wards or to floor sleeping rather than beds, being addressed with caste slurs by nursing staff, and — in at least four documented cases in the last decade — bodies of Dalit patients who died in suspicious custody circumstances being released without mandatory post-mortems (which are legally required), potentially concealing medical negligence or violence. In Rajasthan, Community Health Centres in Dalit-majority talukas have documented vacancy rates for doctors of 60–70% — government doctors simply refuse posting in Dalit areas and use political connections to remain in cities. The vacancies have existed for years without consequence.

Mental health — the invisible crisis. The first national survey specifically examining Dalit mental health (NIMHANS research, 2020) found chronic stress, PTSD symptoms, and depression at rates comparable to populations in active conflict zones — consistent with what would be expected from communities experiencing sustained, unpredictable, inescapable violence and humiliation across lifetimes. "Minority stress" frameworks developed in the US for racial minorities apply directly: the constant vigilance required to navigate caste-hostile environments — deciding which well to avoid, which road to not walk down, which official to not anger, which colleague to not contradict — is a sustained psychological load that has measurable health consequences across decades. The COVID-19 pandemic exposed every existing caste health gap simultaneously: Dalit daily wage workers could not afford lockdown. Dalit sanitation workers continued working without PPE. Dalit migrant workers who walked hundreds of kilometres home were overwhelmingly lower-caste. The vaccine rollout in many village panchayats followed caste hierarchy in who received priority appointments — documented by Dalit NGOs in UP, Bihar, and Rajasthan in 2021.

COMING SOON
🎬

Dalit Cinema & Art — Reclaiming the Gaze

For 100 years, Indian mainstream cinema portrayed Dalits as: comic servants (the village fool who speaks in caste-dialect for laughs); objects of upper-caste sympathy (the noble untouchable who suffers beautifully until a good-hearted upper-caste protagonist reforms the village); villains (the threatening low-caste man who desires an upper-caste woman); or exotic backdrop (the colourful scheduled-tribe community who dance in forest scenes). In all of these representations, Dalits exist as objects of someone else's story. They do not have interiority, agency, complexity, desire, or a point of view that the camera inhabits. They are seen. They do not see. The camera is always upper-caste.

Pa. Ranjith — the revolution from Tamil Nadu. Pa. Ranjith (born 1983, Pallar Dalit caste, Coimbatore) is the first major Indian director to consistently, unapologetically make Dalit protagonists as full human beings with interiority, desire, political consciousness, style, and agency. Kaala (2018, starring Rajinikanth) is a superhero film set in Dharavi's Dalit slums — in which the hero is unambiguously Dalit, the politics are explicitly Ambedkarite, and the villain is a Brahmin politician seeking to demolish the slum in the name of urban development. Kabali (2016) — Rajinikanth as a Dalit labour rights organiser in Malaysia — brought global Dalit diaspora identity onto a mainstream screen. Sarpatta Parambarai (2021) — a boxing film set in Chennai's Dalit working-class community — was praised by international critics as one of the finest sports films ever made in any language. Ranjith founded the Neelam Cultural Centre in Chennai as a permanent space for Dalit arts and Ambedkarite cultural production.

The non-Dalit gaze debate. Article 15 (2019, dir. Anubhav Sinha) — an upper-caste IPS officer investigates caste atrocities in UP — was praised as India's most honest mainstream caste film. Dalit critics raised a precise objection: the story is still told through the eyes of an upper-caste saviour. The Dalit victims are still objects of someone else's moral awakening. The film is a Dalit story filtered through an upper-caste camera. Jai Bhim (2021, dir. T.J. Gnanavel, star Suriya) — a Tamil film based on the real case of advocate Chandru defending a tortured Dalit tribal man — won the Google "Best Film of 2021" by user votes globally. Both Suriya and Gnanavel are upper-caste. The question posed: can non-Dalit artists tell Dalit stories? What is gained and what is lost? The debate is not abstract — it determines whose labour creates the representation, who profits from it, and whose perspective shapes how Dalit life is understood by millions of viewers who will form opinions from film before they form them from text.

Dalit literature as cinema's foundation. The Marathi Dalit literary movement of the 1960s–1990s produced the source material that Dalit cinema is only beginning to adapt: Baburao Bagul's short stories (collected in Jevha Mi Jaat Chorli Hoti — "When I Had Hidden My Caste"); Namdeo Dhasal's Golpitha poems; Sharankumar Limbale's autobiography Akkarmashi (translated as The Outcaste). Baby Kamble's The Prisons We Broke. Bama's Tamil Dalit autobiography Karukku (1992) — the first published Tamil Dalit woman's autobiography. Urmila Pawar's Aaydan. These are the texts that contain the full subjectivity — the interior lives, the rage, the love, the humour, the survival — that cinema has barely begun to represent.

COMING SOON
⚖️

The Reservation Wars — 75 Years of a Contested Right

Reservation — India's constitutional affirmative action for SC/ST and OBC communities in government employment and higher education — is the most contested domestic policy in independent India. The upper-caste anti-reservation narrative has remained essentially unchanged since 1950: that reservation violates "meritocracy," that it perpetuates caste consciousness rather than ending it, that the "best" candidates are being displaced by "less qualified" reserved-category applicants. This narrative has been repeated so consistently and in so many official contexts — exam prep books, newspaper op-eds, Supreme Court rulings, dinner table conversations in upper-caste families — that it functions as settled common sense for much of India's English-language public sphere. This section will examine every element of that argument and show precisely why each element is false, who benefits from believing it, and what the actual 75-year record shows.

The Mandal Commission (1979) and the 1990 anti-OBC riots. The Mandal Commission, established by the Janata Party government, recommended extending reservation to OBC communities (Other Backward Classes) — expanding the beneficiary base from ~22% of the population (SC/ST) to approximately 52%. The Commission's report gathered dust for a decade. V.P. Singh implemented it in 1990. The upper-caste response was the most sustained anti-reservation violence in Indian history: young upper-caste men (some with documented connections to BJP/VHP student wings) self-immolated in protest — a tactic of sacrifice carefully calibrated for media impact. Riots across North India. University closures. The media's language was openly sympathetic to the anti-reservation protesters and dismissive of OBC communities, revealing whose perspective the English-language media considered legitimate. The Mandal implementation survived the opposition. It transformed Indian politics by making OBC assertion a permanent force — which the BJP would eventually absorb through Hindutva.

What 75 years of reservation actually produced. 15% SC reservation in central government Class I (senior administrative) positions. The actual SC representation in Class I positions: approximately 13% (2022 data) — still below the mandated 15%, despite decades of the provision being in force, because promotions and postings within the service are controlled by human discretion that reintroduces caste bias. In the Supreme Court of India — the institution responsible for upholding the reservation right — Dalit representation among judges has never exceeded 1–2 of 31 positions at any point in India's post-independence history. In India's premier IIMs, SC faculty positions are consistently below mandated levels because recruitment processes give committees discretion to declare "no suitable candidates found." The data does not show reservation "working too well." It shows the system producing tokens of inclusion while the power structure above the reservation layer remains overwhelmingly upper-caste.

The creamy layer controversy and OBC vs SC fracture. The "creamy layer" principle — established by the Supreme Court for OBC reservation — excludes OBC individuals above a certain income threshold on the grounds that they are no longer "backward." Upper-caste political formations have repeatedly pushed to apply creamy layer to SC/ST reservation as well, which Ambedkarite and SC political organisations have consistently and correctly opposed: caste discrimination is not primarily an economic phenomenon (a wealthy Dalit still faces caste-based social exclusion, violence, and discrimination), so economic exclusion from reservation does not make logical sense. The push to apply creamy layer to SC reservation is best understood as an attempt to shrink the beneficiary pool while appearing to be concerned with "genuine backwardness." This section will also examine the deliberately cultivated OBC vs SC tension — in which upper-caste political formations encourage OBC communities to view SC reservation as competition rather than recognising that both face the same structural hierarchy.

COMING SOON
🌾

Land — The Root of Every Atrocity

Read every massacre in the Massacres section of this archive. Read every atrocity in Today's Crimes. In almost every case, if you follow the chain of causation back far enough, you arrive at land. The Kilvenmani workers were demanding wages from landlords whose power was land. Laxmanpur Bathe: Dalit communities were organising for land redistribution. Tsundur: Dalit families had accumulated enough money to consider buying land, and their children had started entering colleges — threatening the monopoly on educated professions that land-wealth had always financed. Honour killings: Dalit men marrying into landowning families threaten to transfer property out of the caste. Every massacre, every honour killing, every village boycott ultimately protects a material interest: the upper-caste monopoly on the land that produces the food, the income, and the power that everything else rests on.

The numbers. SC/ST communities: 25% of India's population. SC/ST land ownership: approximately 9% of agricultural land. In Bihar — where the Ranvir Sena massacres happened — Dalit communities own less than 3% of agricultural land despite being 16% of the population. In Rajasthan, the figure is similar. In Punjab, Dalit Sikhs (32% of the population) own less than 3% of agricultural land. The mandatory panchayat land allocation — 1/3 of common land to SC households, legally required since the 73rd Constitutional Amendment (1992) — has been implemented in approximately 10% of villages nationally by activist survey estimates. Thirty-two years of a mandatory legal provision, and 90% of villages have not complied. No panchayat member has ever been prosecuted for non-compliance.

Two approaches, both failed. Vinoba Bhave's Bhoodan Movement (1951–1965) asked landlords to voluntarily donate land — specifically 1/6th of their holdings — for redistribution to landless communities. Approximately 4.5 million acres were pledged. The majority was rocky, infertile, legally disputed, or fraudulently donated land that was already the subject of litigation. Actual redistribution to Dalit families: a tiny fraction of the pledged amount. The voluntary approach assumed goodwill that did not exist. Naxalite land redistribution (1967–present) took the opposite approach: armed cadres entered landlords' properties and physically redistributed land to Dalit and Adivasi families. In some areas of Bihar, West Bengal, and Andhra Pradesh, this produced real — if temporary — land access for Dalit families. The state's response was counterinsurgency operations that killed far more Dalit labourers who had joined the movement than landlords who had opposed it. The land was typically reclaimed after security operations ended. Both approaches have failed because neither addressed the institutional power that makes land reform politically impossible in practice: the same landlord class controls the district administration, the courts, and the political parties that would need to implement redistribution.

The urban dimension. Dalit landlessness is not only a rural problem. Urban Dalits are concentrated in informal settlements — slums, chawls, basti colonies — with no formal property rights, maximum vulnerability to "slum clearance" and "urban development" demolitions, and zero ability to use home ownership as collateral for credit. The Kaala film's premise — a Dalit slum leader fighting the demolition of Dharavi — is not fictional fantasy. It is the lived political reality of every major Indian city. Dharavi itself faces continued redevelopment pressure that would displace hundreds of thousands of Dalit and migrant residents. The replacement housing offered in redevelopment schemes is consistently documented as inaccessible: too expensive, too far from livelihoods, with too many documentation requirements for families who have lived informally for generations.

COMING SOON
🧠

The Ideology — How Caste Justifies Itself

Every system of oppression requires an ideology — a set of ideas that make the oppression appear natural, inevitable, deserved, or beneficial. Without ideology, raw power requires constant visible enforcement, which is expensive and unstable. With ideology, the oppressed themselves participate in their own oppression because they have been made to believe it is the order of the universe. Caste's ideology is among the most sophisticated and durable ever constructed. It has survived 3,000 years, British colonialism, constitutional abolition, literacy, urbanisation, and globalisation. Understanding how it works — precisely, mechanically, in daily interactions and in grand philosophical texts — is the prerequisite for understanding why legal abolition has not abolished it.

Pillar 1: Karma. You are born into your caste because of sins committed in previous lives. Your untouchable birth is your own moral failure, carried forward. This is not punishment by society — it is the cosmic accounting of your own deeds. Therefore: resisting your caste position is resisting the cosmic moral order. Ambedkar's demolition of this argument is one of his greatest intellectual contributions: karma as a caste ideology requires you to accept that the child born into a sweeper family was morally inferior in a previous life and therefore deserves hereditary sewage work in this one. The child. Born into it. Who has done nothing yet. Karma applied to inherited birth position is not a moral system — it is an immunisation of existing privilege against moral challenge. If your high birth is deserved, it requires no justification. If my low birth is deserved, my suffering requires no remedy.

Pillar 2: Dharma. Every caste has its dharma — its sacred duty — which includes its occupational assignment. A Brahmin's dharma is to study and teach the Vedas. A Valmiki's dharma is to clean sewage. To violate your dharma is cosmic sin — not merely social transgression. Therefore: a Dalit who refuses to clean sewage is not a worker demanding dignity. He is a sinner violating sacred order. The genius of dharma as an ideological tool is that it converts economic exploitation into religious virtue — for both parties. The Brahmin who receives the Valmiki's service is not exploiting him. Both are fulfilling their divine function. Ambedkar: "The Hindu must be... a slave not only in his economic life but in his spiritual life as well. The very God he worships has commanded him to remain in servitude."

Pillar 3: Ritual Pollution. Contact with certain bodies — and the products of those bodies — is pollution. Untouchability is therefore not cruelty. It is hygiene. The avoidance of the Dalit body is the same as the avoidance of disease. This framing converts the act of social exclusion into a protective act — the upper caste is not harming the Dalit by refusing their touch. He is protecting himself from contamination. Periyar's rationalist critique targeted this directly: if the Brahmin who spent the morning performing Vedic fire rituals is spiritually pure, and the Mahar who spent the morning removing a dead body is spiritually polluted, and both then go to the same river to bathe — who is the river polluted by? The ideological claim is physically incoherent. It survives not because it is rational but because it is useful to those for whom it is useful.

Pillar 4: Community Harmony. The contemporary upper-caste framing: caste discrimination is bad, but "enforcing" inter-caste marriage or "reservations" disturbs "natural" community cohesion. Every community has its traditions. "Harmony" requires respecting boundaries. This is the softest ideological pillar — the one that operates in liberal and progressive discourse rather than explicitly religious discourse — and in some ways the most insidious, because it converts the demand for equality into "aggression" and the defence of hierarchy into "moderation." Ambedkar addressed this in Annihilation of Caste directly: the Hindu social order is not harmonious. It is a violent hierarchy. Calling it "harmony" is naming peace as the condition in which the oppressed have stopped resisting.

Caste & Indian Democracy — Whose Republic?

India is constitutionally a republic of equals. It is the country where, in 1997, 58 Dalits were massacred in Laxmanpur Bathe and every convicted killer was subsequently acquitted. Both of these facts are true simultaneously. Understanding how requires understanding that democracy is not merely a set of formal institutions — it is also the social power to make those institutions work for you. A constitution that says all citizens are equal does not produce equality if the police who enforce it, the judges who interpret it, the bureaucrats who implement it, the politicians who legislate it, and the journalists who report on it all come disproportionately from communities who benefit from inequality. India has built formal democratic institutions on a social structure that has not been democratised. The result is institutions that serve the majority of their users — who are upper-caste — and pay formal tribute to equality without producing it.

The judiciary. The Supreme Court of India has 34 judges. As of 2024, there have been a total of approximately 3–4 Dalit judges in the history of the Supreme Court. Currently, there is approximately 1 Dalit judge on the bench. The court that is the final arbiter of Dalit constitutional rights — that acquitted the Laxmanpur Bathe killers through the Patna High Court's ruling that the Supreme Court allowed to stand — has virtually no Dalit perspective in its deliberations. The High Courts are similar. District judges — who hear atrocity cases — come predominantly from upper-caste backgrounds in states like UP, Bihar, Rajasthan, and Gujarat where caste violence is highest. The appointment mechanism for judges (collegium system, in which sitting judges recommend new judges) has no diversity requirement and no accountability mechanism for caste composition.

The media. The "Who Tells Our Stories" survey (Oxfam India and Newslaundry, 2019) examined the masthead composition of 121 major Indian media organisations — newspapers, TV channels, digital outlets, magazines. Finding: 0 Dalits (0%) in senior editorial leadership positions (editor, editor-in-chief, editorial director) across all 121 organisations surveyed. Upper-caste Hindus: 88% of leadership positions. The survey noted that India's English-language media — which sets the national agenda and influences international coverage — is almost entirely upper-caste in its editorial decision-making. This means every editorial decision about whether to cover a caste atrocity, how prominently to place it, what language to use, whose perspective to foreground, and when to move on — is made by people from communities who have historically benefited from Dalit oppression and who may not experience the social cost of under-coverage.

The military — caste regiments. The Indian Army maintains "class regiments" — units recruited from specific castes or communities: the Rajput Regiment, the Jat Regiment, the Sikh Regiment, the Mahar Regiment. The Mahar Regiment is the most famous: it was the regiment in which Ambedkar's father served, and it carries a direct Dalit identity. However, the regiment system institutionalises caste into the army's chain of command in ways that reproduce caste hierarchy: regiments from "martial" castes (Rajput, Jat, Gurkha) have historically received more prestigious postings, better equipment, and higher promotion rates than "non-martial" regiments, in a classification system whose origins are explicitly colonial (the British "martial races" theory determined which communities were recruited for front-line combat and which were assigned support roles). The caste regiment system has never been formally examined for its equity implications. It remains in operation.

🌍

Global Dalit Movement — From Geneva to San Jose

India has spent decades in international forums arguing that caste is a purely domestic matter — a cultural tradition being reformed from within — and that any international involvement constitutes interference in Indian sovereignty and a misunderstanding of Indian culture. This position has been remarkably effective: caste is conspicuously absent from most international human rights frameworks, UN treaty bodies rarely name it as explicitly as they name race or ethnicity, and major international corporations with thousands of upper-caste Indian employees in leadership positions have historically treated it as invisible. The Dalit diaspora — particularly in the United States, United Kingdom, Germany, and Japan — has spent the last 20 years systematically dismantling this diplomatic erasure, bringing caste into international legal frameworks, corporate HR policies, and municipal anti-discrimination ordinances. The battle is not over. But it is further along than India's government would like.

Durban 2001 — The First International Battle. The UN World Conference Against Racism in Durban, South Africa, 2001. A coalition of Dalit NGOs — led by the National Campaign on Dalit Human Rights and supported by Human Rights Watch — submitted extensive testimony documenting caste as a form of descent-based discrimination equivalent to racial discrimination, requesting the conference declaration to explicitly name caste. India, with support from other caste-practising South Asian states (Nepal, Sri Lanka, Pakistan — each with their own political reasons to avoid the issue), lobbied successfully to exclude explicit mention of caste from the final Durban Declaration. The word "caste" appears nowhere in the document. The Dalit NGOs published a parallel statement documenting what had been excluded and why. It was the opening act of a 20-year international campaign.

Equality Labs and the US anti-caste movement. Equality Labs, founded by Thenmozhi Soundararajan (a Dalit American Tamil woman), published the first large-scale survey of caste discrimination in the United States in 2018. Key findings: 67% of Dalit respondents reported experiencing caste discrimination at their workplace; 59% in educational settings; 25% reported physical assault. The survey specifically documented discrimination in the tech industry — where a large upper-caste Indian immigrant population has created a replicated caste hierarchy inside American corporations. Seattle passed the first US municipal anti-caste discrimination ordinance in 2023 — adding caste as a protected category under the city's anti-discrimination law. California's state legislature passed AB 1815 in 2024, adding caste to the state's anti-discrimination protections. The Indian government and Hindu American nationalist organisations (HASAN, HAF, CoHNA) lobbied vigorously against both — arguing that caste-based protections "stigmatise" Indian Americans and promote "Hindu-phobia." Dalit American organisations countered that Hindu-phobia and caste discrimination are not the same thing: one targets religious identity, the other targets hereditary social position.

The Cisco case — corporate caste discrimination proven. In 2020, California's Department of Fair Employment and Housing filed a lawsuit against Cisco Systems, alleging that two upper-caste Indian managers had systematically discriminated against a Dalit Indian employee — denying him promotions, undermining his work, and creating a hostile environment specifically targeting his Dalit identity. It was the first caste discrimination lawsuit filed against a major US corporation. Cisco settled in 2023. The settlement amount was not disclosed, but the settlement terms included requirements for caste sensitivity training and non-discrimination policy implementation. The case established that caste discrimination in the American workplace is legally actionable. It opened the door for future cases. The Indian tech corridor — where upper-caste Indian managers disproportionately hire, promote, and give work to other upper-caste Indians in patterns documented by Equality Labs — has not yet been systematically litigated. The Cisco case suggests it eventually will be.

The UK — unfinished business. The UK Equality Act 2010 includes a provision (Section 9(5)) that specifically empowers the government to add "caste" as an aspect of the protected characteristic of "race." This provision was included specifically because of Dalit British advocacy documenting caste discrimination in UK South Asian communities — in Hindu temples that exclude lower-caste worshippers, in gurdwaras with caste-segregated seating, in marriage markets, and in South Asian-owned businesses. The Conservative government elected in 2010 declined to implement the caste provision for 13 years. A 2018 Supreme Court ruling held that caste discrimination was already covered under existing "ethnic origins" protections — providing some protection without explicit legislation, but not the comprehensive protection that Dalit advocates sought. The Labour government elected in 2024 has signalled possible movement on the issue. As of 2025, it remains unresolved.
